O, sons, since I have completed for you this book, which is the culmination of the science of medicine,  through its completion, and through its expositions, and through its declarations, it seems to me that I should complete that for you with this treatise, which is a part of the operation with the hand, that is, surgery.
Because the operation with the hand is almost entirely absent in our region and in our time, to the point that the science of it is greatly diminished, and its vestiges are lost; and there remains of it only some small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which have been mostly altered and have fallen into errors and hesitations, so that their intentions are obscured and their power and art are weakened.
And it seems to me that I should revitalize it with the ordering of this treatise, according to the way of exposition, declaration, and abbreviation; and that it should come with the forms of cautery irons and other instruments of the work, with the addition of their explanation and preparation, which is necessary.
But the reason why a skilled artisan with his hand is not found in our time is that the art of medicine is extensive, and its practitioner must first be trained in the science of anatomy, which is lacking in Gaul, so that he knows the arrangement of the limbs and their forms, and their connection and separation; and has knowledge of the bones, nerves, and muscles, and their number and distribution; and of the pulsating and quiet veins, and the places of their origin.
And for this reason Hippocrates says that in name it is much, but in practice little, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already spoken of this in the introduction to this book; for whoever is not knowledgeable about what we have said about anatomy is not exempt from falling into error, by which men will die, as I have seen many, for some are trained in this science and practice it without knowledge and experience.
For I saw a foolish and unskilled physician incise a scrofulous abscess in the neck of a woman, and he cut some arteries in the neck, so that a great deal of blood flowed until the woman died in his hands.
And I saw another physician who undertook to remove a stone from a man who was advanced in years, and the stone was large, and he came to him and extracted that stone with a piece of the bladder, so that that man died on the third day.
And I was called to extract it, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the condition of the patient, I knew that he would die.
And I saw another physician who received a stipend from a duke of our land for surgery, and a black eunuch came to him who had a fracture in his thigh; and the physician, through his ignorance, extracted the fracture, over the wound, with cushions and splints with tight binding, and did not allow the wound to breathe.
And then he unbound it according to his wishes, and then he bound it again, and then he left it for several days and commanded that we unbind the binding; and it remained thus until the thigh and foot were suppurated, and it came to ruin.
And I was called to him, and I prepared to unbind the binding, and tranquility was achieved and thus a lessening of his pains.
But however, corruption was already confirmed in the limb, so that I could not restrain it, and the corruption did not cease to spread in the limb until the patient perished.
And I saw another physician who pierced a cancerous abscess, and it was ulcerated after a few days, until its malignancy was magnified by its master (or owner).
The reason is that because the burning cancer is pure melancholic humor, therefore it is not appropriate that it be touched with iron at all, but rather be in a limb which suffers so that it is completely stopped.
And therefore, son, it is necessary for you that the operation with the hand be divided into two parts: that is, in an operation which is associated with health, and in a work with which there is danger in the greatest dispositions.
And I have already explained this in every part of this book where operations occur in which there is error and fear; therefore it is necessary for you to observe this and avoid that, so that fools do not find a way to speak and dishonor you.
Therefore, take up your instruments with diligence and with the protection of God, and treat your patients with happiness and firmness.
And use the best method, by the grace of God, leading to health and laudable success, and leave the terrible diseases of difficult healing, and put away your instruments from that which makes you fear, so that hesitation in your faith and in your name does not overcome you: for it is more conducive to your glory, and higher in the world and behind your sanctities.
Galen already said in some of his admonitions: Do not treat a bad disease, lest you be called a bad physician".
And I have divided this book into three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter deals with the head down to the feet, the forms of instruments and the tools of cautery, and what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, of suction and perforation, and of cupping glasses, and of wounds, and of the extraction of arrows and similar things, all chaptered and ordered according to the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, of restoration and dislocation, and of the treatment of sprains and similar things; a chapter ordered from head to foot, and of the forms of the instruments.
It is necessary that I say the quality of the benefits of that cautery and its harms, and in what constitution cautery is prohibited.
Therefore I say that the discourse on the quality of the benefit of cautery and its harm is a long and prolix discourse, and subtle in science, and a hidden secret; and many wise men have already spoken of it, and they disagree about it, and a little of their discourses suffices for me, fearing prolixity.
And therefore I say that cautery benefits all complexions universally, whether with matter or without matter, except two complexions, which are hot complexions without matter.
In the hot and dry complexion with matter, there is disagreement; for some say that cautery is beneficial to that, and others say the contrary, that cautery is not suitable in an illness which is due to heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness, and it is inconvenient that a hot and dry illness should be healed with that which is hot and dry.
And I say that he who says the contrary is wrong, that benefit is done by cautery in every hot and dry illness that comes to the bodies of men, because when you compare the body of man and its humidities to the complexion of fire, you find the body of man cold.
And I say, regarding this discourse, that experience has revealed this to me several times.
However, it is not fitting that this should come upon someone who has already been exhausted and exercised in the chapter on cautery by prior practice, and who has known the diversity of the complexions of men and the disposition of illnesses in themselves, and the causes of those, and their accidents, and the span of time of those.
But in other complexions, there is no fear for you, and especially in cold and humid illnesses, because all doctors have agreed on those, and are not in disagreement about the benefit of cautery in those.
And know, sons, that the secret of healing by cautery with fire, and its goodness, is preferable to cautery with burning medicine, because fire is simple, whose action does not reach beyond the member which is cauterized, and does not harm another member joined to it, except with little harm.
But the action of burning medicine reaches to those members which are distant from it, and quickly brings to the member an illness of difficult healing and quickly kills.
But fire, by its sublimity and the goodness of its substance, does not do this, except to purge. And it is known to us by experiment and by long service of study in the art concerning the truth of the causes; and for this I am excused from the length of discourse. And if it were not that it is not fitting for my book to leave hidden from you this secret regarding fire, and the quality of its operation in bodies, and its expulsion by which it expels illnesses, with philosophical demonstrative discourse, which would be tedious to your minds.
And know, sons, that they disagree on the time in which it is convenient to perform cautery, and they consider springtime the best time.
And I say that cautery is convenient at all times, because the harm caused by the accidental time is submerged in the benefit that is gained with cautery, and especially if the cautery is for pains, or for necessary, strong, and urgent pains, which do not suffer delay because it is feared that those [pains] will bring harm, which is greater than the small harm incurred by the time [of year].
And this does not enter your minds, O sons, this is only esteemed by the vulgar people and foolish physicians, that is, that to that illness which is healed by cautery there is no remission at any time, and they posit this consequence.
And the cause is not at all as they think, for cautery is only in the place of medicine which alters the complexion and dries the humors which are the cause from which the pains come.
However, cautery surpasses medicine in the speed of its efficacy and virtue of its operation and greatness of its effect.
And it is possible that his illness will return at some time, according to the complexion of the patient and the location of his illness, and his strength, and that which is prepared in his body from the aggregation of superfluities in him, and that which has come to him in his acquisition in food and similar causes; unless the illness in which cautery is administered is a subtle illness in a limb with few superfluities and humors, such as cautery of a tooth for pain and similar to that, it is possible that that pain will not return to him, but this happens rarely.
And a more common saying is that cautery is the last resort of medicine, and this is a correct saying, not in the sense that they understand it, for they believe that it is not a cure that benefits with medicine or any other cause besides cautery.
And the reason is contrary to that; and the intention that cautery is the last resort of medicine is only when we administer methods of cure in some illnesses, and those medicines do not work, and then at the end of the process one administers cautery, and it benefits in that case. And therefore it is clear that cautery is the last resort of medicine, not according to the understanding of the common people and too many foolish physicians.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say this except for the purity of gold and the nobility of its substance.
And they said that the place of cautery does not scar, although that is not entirely true, for I have already proven this and found that it does not do so except in some bodies and not others, and cautery with it is good and better than with iron as they said; however, when you cauterize with fire with gold, it is not apparent to you, because it is consumed according to the amount you want because of the softness of the gold, and because it prepares to receive cold; and if you apply too much heat, the gold melts from the fire, and some laminae fall into the fire so that the artisan is distracted by that.
And for that reason, cautery with iron is done more easily and more appropriately for the correctness of the operation.
And I have already ordered this chapter on cautery according to chapters, and I have ordered those from the head to the feet, so that I might offer to the inquirer that which he desires from it.
The first chapter.
This cauterization combats the humidity and coldness on the brain; the accidents of which are two causes: that is, the cause of sudden and abundant rheumy flux, called coryza, flowing from the head to the parts of the eyes and ears, and much sleepiness, and toothache and throat ache, and universally any illness that comes from coldness, such as paralysis, sudden unconsciousness, apoplexy, and similar illnesses.
The form of this cauterization is this: first, order the patient to be evacuated with laxative medicine, purifying the head, for three or four nights, depending on the strength of the patient, his age and his constitution.
And then order him to shave his head with a razor, and then make him sit between your hands on a low bench and have him place his hands on his feet.
And then place the root of your palm near the bridge of the nose between his eyes. However, when your middle finger has reached that place, mark that spot with a probe, and then ignite the olive-shaped cautery, of which this is the form:
And then place that [cautery] on the spot marked with the probe, in such a way that it descends, with which your hand is slowly constrained, and you rotate it.
And then lift your hand slightly and observe the area, and if you see some quantity of the head of the bone uncovered, about the size of a grain of barley, then lift your hand, and if not, return your hand with that iron, or with another if this one is cold, until that which I have told you is seen of the bone.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it on the area and leave it for three days.
And then place on it lint soaked in wine and leave it there until the burn scar is destroyed. And then treat the area with tetrafarmacon ointment until it is healed.
And it is said that when the wound remains open, it better discharges the arm, for that is better and more beneficial.
And some of them say that the fistula should be cauterized to the bone, and the cautery should be held there until some part of the bone's substance is burnt.
And then scrape away what is burnt from the bone, and then it should be cured.
And others say that with the cautery, penetrate to the back, until it penetrates strongly into the bone, until the bone is burnt, and this is the form of a small abscess: And they believe that vapors from the head exhale from that place.
And let the wound be kept open for a long time, and then let it be cured until it is healed.
But I have not seen these two types of cautery at all, except in some men, as a matter of fear; and leave that, for my part, better is health when it is; for the head is weakened by the solution of its continuity, as we have testified in other members, and especially when the patient's head is of a weak nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better, in my opinion; and I use and work with that one for health.
Chapter Two.
Concerning the cautery of the head.
When chronic and prolonged pain occurs in the whole head, and this is prolonged in the patient, and he uses leeches, and cuppings, and purgatives, and plasters, and poultices, and cauteries, and unguents, and similar things, and especially if he has already been cauterized with a cautery which we recount and none of these things benefits him, then consider whether the patient's head is of strong constitution by nature; and it is not weak and finds much great cold, then cauterize that with another cautery a little below that one. And then cauterize that above the horn of his head with a cautery until the scab is parted. Incise the scab and let the bone be uncovered to the extent we have recounted, and cauterize that with a cautery in the rear part of the head in the place which is called the vas—and this is the place where a cupping glass is placed. And lighten your hand in this, and do not uncover the bone, for the patient thereby finds much great pain, different from the pain of all the other cauterizations of the head.
And I say this cautery in its place, because it is necessary that the cautery with which the horns of the head and the occiput are cauterized is a more subtle cautery than that with which the middle place of the head is cauterized; and this is its form.
Chapter Three.
On the cauterization of recent migraine.
When a sudden pain occurs in the head, or a spreading pain within the eye, let the head be evacuated with purifying medicines, and let the other treatment be administered, which I have described in the sections on diseases; and if all this does not help, then cauterization in this case is done in two ways: either cauterization with an acute burning medicine, or with iron.
But cauterization with a burning medicine is that you take a clove of garlic, and peel it and cut off its extremities from both ends. Then incise the location of the pain in the temple with a lancet, so that a place is made under the skin, so that it is hidden.
Then bind it tightly with a compress to cause constriction, and leave it for three hours; then unbind it and remove the garlic, and leave the wound for two or three days, and then apply a cotton swab soaked in vinegar until the place becomes inflamed.
And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines which I have written about in the twelfth treatise on burning medicines.
But cauterization with iron in this manner:  heat the cautery, of which this is the form:
and it is called a nail or peg, because its head is like the shape of a clove, in which there is some curvature, and in the middle of it is a small eminence.
Then place it over the area of pain, and hold your hand — so that the cauterizing agent below is not burned — and rotate the iron little by little with speed.
And if the amount that is burned by the specified cauterization is only half of it, then lift your hand so that the artery underneath is not burned, so that a flow of blood does not come.
Then soak cotton in salt water and place that on the area, and leave it for three days; then place cotton soaked in a poultice, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the tip of a knife according to the cautery method, and beware of cutting an artery in this specific, not-ancient, migraine.
Chapter four.
On the cauterization of chronic migraine.
When you treat migraine with what we have said in the preceding treatment, and what we have said in the divisions of illnesses, and the treatment is not effective, and sometimes because of the illness, what we have said about the first cautery with medicine or the cautery with fire is insufficient, then you must heat the knife cautery until it is white-hot, after marking the location of the pain with a line whose length is half a finger, or thereabouts; and pause your hand for a moment and press until you incise the artery and reach the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to beware of the continuation of the jaw, which is moved during mastication, and that the muscle not be injured and the nerve which moves it, and that spasm does not occur.
And if it is about the guarding and caution of the flow of blood from the artery that is incised, for in its incision there is danger, and especially to him who ignores what he does and is not studious and experienced; and he abandons the operation at the beginning.
And I will come with a remembrance of the treatment of accidental bleeding from the artery according to its manner, in its place in this book.
And if sometimes this illness is not relieved by this cautery and the patient's body is tolerable, cauterize it in the middle of the head as we have recounted, and treat the wound until it is healed.
And if you wish, administer the cautery which we have mentioned in the chapter on the constriction of an artery with a cautery having two blades, and this cauterization is better and more suitable.
Chapter 5.
Chapter 5. On the cauterization of earaches.
When pain occurs in the ear due to cold and is treated with laxatives and with the other treatments that I have mentioned in the section, and the pain is not relieved, then apply the cautery, and it is that which is called "puncta," of which this is the form:
Then apply the point of the cautery, and let it be moved around the entire ear as a wheel is turned, or in the circumference of each part if the pain is located in a specific part. Slowly lengthen the cautery from the root of the ear, after you have marked the spot with a probe.
And if the cautery requires ten points in the whole ear, or whichever ear it may be, then treat those places until they are healed.
Chapter 6.
On the cauterization of a cough.
A cough which is cured with cauterization is only of the types which originate from phlegm, as we have said in the divisions of diseases.
Avoid cauterization of the type of cough that arises from dryness or from spasm of the nerve.
When you treat that type of cough with enemas and purgatives and gargles, and your treatment is not effective, then you should cauterize the patient with three cauterizations:  one at the root of the ear, the second a little below the temple, and the third at the juncture of the two lips.
And place your cautery on the opposite side from the affected part, because softening [or relief] only comes to the part that appears healthy.
And the form of cauterization is that you cauterize it with the cautery at the very tip of the upper ear, a little above the temple, and another in the temple, the length of which should be according to the length of the thumb, and hold your hand with the cautery until the quantity of half the thickness of a goose quill is burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a type of knife, the form of which is shown/depicted above. However, it is more subtle to do it a little like this; and it is necessary to cut away some superfluous thickness.
And then take care of the area with what has been previously described until it is healed.
Chapter 7.
On the cauterization of chronic apoplexy.
When apoplexy is chronic and you treat it with what we have said and your medication is not effective and the patient does not have a fever, then cauterize the patient with four cauteries: on each of the horns of his head; and with a cautery in the middle of the head, as we have said; and with a cautery on the neck. And the manner of cauterization is done as described above.
And it is already cauterized above the mouth of the stomach; and it is further behind; and then let it be treated with what follows.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the cauterization of forgetfulness done by flame.
That large jars and purifying pills be given to the patient at the beginning.
And then shave the entire head of the patient and place on their neck a plaster of cetre described in the treatise on plasters.
And apply that several times, for that is a manner of cautery. And do this according to the same order which I have already said.
And therefore if he is healed by these means, it is good; and if not, then make for him three cauterizations in the back part of his head, which descend from the highest part of the head to the lowest part of the neck. And place between each cautery the width of a finger, and then treat the cautery with what has been prescribed.
But if you see increased swelling and the patient tolerates it, then cauterize in the middle of the affected area; and if you see even greater swelling, then cauterize on both sides.
Then treat that until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive-shaped according to the form previously given.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the cauterization of paralysis and the softening of the whole body.
As you proceed with the purification of the head with leeches and with what we have said; then shave the patient's head, and then cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with a cautery on all the sides (corners) of the head, and with a cautery on the back part of it, and with three cauteries on the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary in the illness of softening of the body to find it and the patient tolerates it and the illness is severe, then cauterize it with four cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and finally with a cautery until it is burned from the tailbone up, and raise your hand. And then treat it until it is healed, and this should be an olive-oil cautery.
Chapter 10.
On the cauterization of epilepsy.
The epileptic is not cauterized unless that epilepsy is due to phlegm.
And then it is necessary that you purify his brain at the beginning with herbs and with the other treatment which we have described in the section when the patient is strong and can tolerate the expulsion of medicines.
And if it is a child who cannot tolerate medicines, then administer gargles and masticatories that purify the brain, before that for many days, with improvement of the diet.
Then shave the head of the patient, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head according to the previous method, and with another cautery in his occiput, and cauterize over each temple. And if the patient is strong and tolerant, then cauterize him with the cauteries which I have mentioned, having paralysis and softening of the body over the vertebrae of the neck and the vertebrae of the back.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped, as has been previously described.
But if the patient is a child, then apply a small cautery according to this method.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the cauterization of melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupt humors and a thick phlegm, then cauterize that with the cauteries which we have mentioned in the treatment of paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is a superfluity tending towards melancholy, and the patient's body is moist, then give him something to drink that cleanses his brain, according to what has preceded in the division (of treatments).
Then shave the patient's head, and then make a decent-sized poultice for him on a circle (of cloth), and then place that in the middle of the head.
And let the patient sit upright, and be held on all sides.
Then apply a liniment; and it is most worthy to use old sheep's fat. And heat that over the fire with a moderate, measured heating, which the said sufferer will feel when it is applied to him. Then pour that into the middle of his head within the circle, and leave it there until it is cooled.
And do this to the patient once a week, along with his other treatment, until he is healed; or if you wish, cauterize it making small punctures according to the need of the patient, but hold your hand with a cautery thus prepared: this is a type of cautery which moistens the brain evenly.
Then apply that cotton soaked in fat or goose grease.
Chapter 12.
Concerning the cauterization of water descending into the eye.
When the beginning of water in the eye is revealed to you by the signs which I have mentioned in the division, then begin and give the patient something to purify his head, and protect it from all humidity.
And make him sweat in a bath for a few days.
And then order him to shave his head, and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head; and then cauterize it with two cauteries on the two temples if the beginning of the flow of water is in both eyes, or on one side if it is the beginning of the water in one eye.
Incise with a cautery all the veins and arteries which are below the hairline; and let the cauteries be at that distance in the width of the temples.
And beware of a flow of blood when it is cauterized; and if you see a flow of blood from it, stop it with any medicine which is possible for you.
And I come with knowledge which is in the extraction of arteries and with the excision of them and the caution of blood flow.
And sometimes it is cauterized in the neck below the two bones with two cauteries placed separately.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the cauterization of old tears (likely referring to chronic sores or ulcers).
When the tears from the eyes are old and persistent, and through the veins and arteries which are in the manifest place of the head outside, and it is verified that this is a superfluity of cold phlegmatic humors, then cauterize that with the cautery which we recounted in the beginning chapter of water falling into the eyes, and with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with two cauteries in the neck below the two temples, and with two cauteries in the neck below the two bones. And if it needs addition (further treatment), then cauterize that in the whole area of the corner of the eye above the end of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter 14.
Concerning the cauterization of nasal odor (or, more accurately, a foul smell from the nose).
When you treat this with what we have said in the division (previous section) and your treatment does not help, then you prepare and give the patient decoctions (or potions) to drink for three nights; and then shave his head, and cauterize it with a medium-sized cautery, with an olive-shaped cautery. And then cauterize that with a clove-shaped cautery, with two cauteries above the two eyebrows, slightly below the hair.
And be careful with the cautery that you do not burn that (area).
And of the clove-shaped cautery, this is the form/shape.
Chapter 15.
Of the cautery for the softening/mollification of the eyelids of the eyes.
When the eyelid is softened by illness or humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with a lunar cautery; and this is its form:
And if you cauterize that (area) above the two eyebrows a little with two cauteries in each part, and they should be elongated/extended from the temples. And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the eyebrow.
And do not press too hard with your hand with the cautery, but only so much that a third part of the eyelid is cauterized/burnt.
Chapter 16.
Concerning cauterization of the eyelid of the eye when the hairs are turned inward and prick the eye.
When the hairs of the eye are turned inward so that they prick the eye, cauterization in these cases is done in two ways, for cauterization is either with fire or with a burning medicine.
If you cauterize it with fire, instruct the patient beforehand to shave his eyelashes as one shaves them, until they are lengthened and aligned; and if it pricks from the root, then you bind the patient's eyes with a bandage so that it is not injured until the hairs are grown.
Then, when they are grown and aligned, place the patient's head in your lap, and then mark on the eyelid of his eyes with a probe, with a mark which is according to the shape of a myrtle leaf, and let the beginning of the mark be near the hairs.
And then place a pledget soaked in egg white or in gum arabic on the eye.
And then heat the cautery, of which this is the form.
And then cauterize over the figure that you have marked, little by little, in several times, until the surface of the cornea, which is like a myrtle leaf, is completely burnt.
And a sign of the success of your work is that you see the eyelid of the eye already contracted and its hairs raised above the puncture of the eye.
Then raise your hand and leave it like that for three days, and then place a pledget with salve on it until the scar is healed. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if some matter returns through the hairs near their root and the eyelid is softened, then apply the cautery again in the same way as you did initially.
But if the hairs are below the eyelid, then cauterize it until it is raised to its natural place and is healed, and the hairs do not touch the eye.
But cauterize with a burning medicine; this is to order the patient to pluck the hairs until they are elongated and healed, and then a plaster of cloth in the shape of a myrtle leaf should be made.
Then take known soap and quicklime and unrefined olive oil, or whatever that is, and mix those ingredients well, and do this quickly so that it does not get cold; then spread this on the plaster which is made in the shape of a myrtle leaf, and place this over one or both eyes; and place under the eye cotton soaked in egg white, and the patient's head should be in your seat (i.e., under your care/supervision).
And place your finger on the medicine and press it slightly and do not remove it until the patient feels the bite of the medicine, for the bite is like fire to him.
And then, when you perceive that he feels the bite, then leave the medicine and remove it with your finger; then, when the bite ceases, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water; and then consider if you see that the eyelid is already raised as it is lifted from constriction with fire or with incision, for then it is well; and if not, return with that medicine to the place where the medicine has not yet been applied and it does not darken until your operation is healed and the eye is contracted (i.e., healed and the eyelid is back in its normal position).
Then place cotton with a bandage on it until the scab from the cauterization is eradicated, and then treat it with healing ointment or with another until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, during your operation, to guard with vigilant diligence that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye, and if the eyelid after some days is softened and some of the hairs are drawn into the eye, then repeat your medicine on that part of the eyelid which is softened, and precisely as you did at the beginning, and then bandage it until it is healed.
And this is the form/method using myrtle leaves.
And know that the eyes of men are diversified according to their size (magnitude and smallness), and therefore your contraction (or treatment, referring to the bandage or adjustment) is made accordingly.
And the path of rectitude will not be hidden from him who studies this art.
Chapter 17.
On the cauterization of a fistula in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula with what we have described in the divisions of diseases and your treatment is ineffective, then you should cauterize it in this manner: instruct the patient to place their head in your lap, and have a minister hold their head between your hands in such a way that their head is not moved or shaken.
Then place a cotton swab dipped in egg white or gum arabic on their eye.
Then heat the cautery, which is shaped like this:
which should be concave, like the shape of a reed or a vulture's feather, at the end with which the cauterization is performed.
And if you wish, let it penetrate to the other end. And if you wish, let it be shaped like a probe.
However, the concave one is better for your work.
Then press the fistula if it is open, and draw out the pus and dry it. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the rottenness from it.
Then place the cautery, which should be well ignited, upon it, and hold your hand a little below the cautery, from the eye to the side of the nose, so that your hand does not waver or the patient be disturbed. Then, place the cautery on the granuloma of the eye and burn it away.
And so, if at the beginning your cautery penetrates the bone, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if necessary; and leave it thus for three days, and then apply a bandage over it with a drying ointment until it is healed.
But if ten days have passed and it is not healed, then apply a sharp, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed and is scraped as is appropriate in its chapter.
And there is another method of cauterizing a fistula which some earlier physicians described:  Identify the location of the fistula and open it, and then place in that fissure a thin tube which is the shape of it. And pour into it the quantity of one ounce of molten lead; and hold your hand with a tube with good retention, and do not move the patient, so that the molten lead does not reach the eye of the patient; and it is necessary that you place over the patient's eye a cloth soaked in egg white or water, because the lead burns the place of the fistula and heals it through a marvelous healing.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said of the cautery and the treatment, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to administer a nasal perforation and reduce the fistula to the body of the nose as it will come to its proper place.
Chapter 18.
Chapter 18. On the cauterization of the fissure of the well (likely referring to a wound or ulcer).
Many times fissures occur on the lips, and especially on the lips of children.
Therefore, if you treat those fissures with what we have said in the previous sections and your treatment is not effective, then apply a small, knife-like cautery according to this form that is shown:
And let its concavity be according to the thinness of a knife.
Then carefully place that instrument in those fissures until the cautery reaches the bottom of the fissures.
And then treat it with cerates until it is healed.
Chapter 19.
On the cauterization of a fistula in the mouth.
When in the root of the uvula, or in the palate, or in the roots of the teeth, an abscess develops and then breaks, and then becomes chronic, and pus flows and a fistula is formed, and you treat it and the treatment is not effective, then you should apply a cautery according to the size of the fistula. Then place that heated cautery into the opening of the fistula, holding it with your hand until the heated iron reaches the bottom of it. Do this once or twice at the back of it; and then treat it with what we have said concerning treatment until it is healed.
And so if the wound is incised and heals, it is good; but if not, then it is necessary to open the area and remove the corrupted bone, as will be explained in its chapter.
Chapter 20.
On the cauterization of teeth and the softening of the gums.
When the gums are softened by humidity and the teeth are loose, and you treat them with medicines and they are not effective, then place the patient's head in your lap, and then heat the cautery, the shape of which will be appropriate for this, so that you place the small spatula against the tooth and quickly insert the heated cautery into it, and hold your hand there briefly, until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the tooth.
And then remove your hand, and then repeat the cauterization several times as needed.
And then let the patient rinse their mouth with salt water and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out, because the loose tooth is now fixed, the softened gum is tightened, and the corrupt humidity is dried out.
Chapter 21.
On the cauterization of toothache.
When toothache is due to coldness, or if there is cold or worms in it, and you cannot cure it with medicine, then cauterize it in one of two ways: either cauterize with a hot iron, or cauterize with fire.
Cauterization with a hot iron is done by taking a hot iron and heating it in a coal fire. Then take a cloth and wrap it around the end of the iron, and then dip it in the boiling iron, and quickly apply it to the painful tooth until it cools.
And then repeat this several times until the heat of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, submerge wool or cloth in the aforementioned hot iron, and place that on the aching tooth, and place a heated iron on top of it until the iron reaches the bottom of the tooth's cavity.
However, cauterization with fire is that you use a small tube of brass or iron, and there should be some thickness in its body so that the intensity of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth.
Then heat the cautery to the desired temperature, and apply it to the tooth, holding your hand there until the cautery cools; and do this several times, because the pain will be reduced either that day or the next.
And it is necessary that, after the application of the cautery, the patient fill their mouth with good hot water and hold it for an hour, and then spit it out; and this is the form of the cautery.
And cauterize with whichever extremity you wish, as is possible for you.
And this is the shape of the cannula:
Chapter 22.
Concerning the cauterization of extrophuli (a likely reference to skin eruptions or lesions).
When extrophuli are caused by phlegm and cold humors and are not responsive to digestion by medicines, and you wish to digest them gently, then you ignite the cautery, whose shape is as follows: having two open ends so that smoke escapes from one end of the cautery and place the ignited end on the abscess once, and again if necessary, until it reaches the depth of the abscess.
And if the abscess is small, then apply the cautery according to the size of the abscess, and then leave it for three days and apply a cloth soaked in butter over it until what the fire has burned is softened.
And then treat it with ointment and bandages until it is healed.
Chapter 23.
On the cauterization of hoarseness and constriction of the throat.
When humidities gather in the lung's passage, and especially when this is accompanied by coldness of constitution, then it is necessary that the patient be evacuated at the beginning with laxative medicines, and afterwards cauterize the area below the root of the throat in the constricted place.
And be careful not to reach the throat with the cautery, nor burn his windpipe, but only a little.
And cauterize that area with another cautery near the junction of the neck at the last vertebra. And his cautery should be a nail, following the previous method.
And afterwards treat it with what has been previously prescribed until it is healed.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the cauterization of lung disease and cough.
When cough and lung disease are due to cold humidity, and the patient has no fever or consumption (tuberculosis) and the illness is chronic, then cauterize with two cauteries on the clavicle, in the previously mentioned hollows and pits; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest between the two nipples; and let the cautery be a nail according to the previous method.
And if you wish, let your cauterization be punctate with the cautery that is called a punctate cautery, the form of which precedes in the punctation of earache.
And let there be points within those (cauterizations); and then treat it with what has been previously described until it is healed.
And let the cautery have three prongs because cauterization is done with this: with one application you can make three cauteries according to this form:
Chapter 25.
Of the cauterization of the testicle, which is the head of the penis.
When the head of the penis is dislocated because of lubricous humors and is not fixed in the moment of its return from its dislocation until it is in the place it is accustomed to return to, and then with a small movement it is dislocated again as it has been observed, then it is necessary that you first reduce the dislocation, and then let the patient be turned onto his back or onto his side; and then elevate the scrotum which is in the inner part of the testicle to the upper parts with the fingers of your left hand, if the joint is dislocated in the inner parts.
And then ignite the cautery having two heads; and this is its form:
And then cauterize the scrotum until it penetrates from one side to the other; and the figure of the cauterization will then be of four cauterized points.
And sometimes it is adjusted according to this count, and therefore there are eight cauteries.
And then place on the cauterized area cooked porridge with salt, and then keep the patient calm, and the limb should not be moved for some time until it is healed.
And if the dislocation is in the upper parts and this is done late, then cauterize over the muscle with a pointed cautery or with many pointed cauteries, because the joint is strengthened, the humidity is removed, and the patient is healed.
Chapter 26.
Concerning the cauterization of the stomach.
When cold and many humors come into the stomach, so that it is drawn from its proper composition and its multiplicity remains in it, and if you continue the methods of treatment and do not cure it, then it is necessary that the patient lie supine on his back, extend his hands and legs, and then cauterize that area with three cauteries: one cautery below the feet, to the extent of one finger, with a pointed cautery, and with two cauteries below on both sides of the cautery, so that a triangular figure of cautery is formed; and space them out so that they are not touching, which would cause putrefaction.
And let the depth of the cautery be two-thirds the thickness of the finger.
And let the figure of the cautery be according to this form without addition. And if you wish, cauterize with a large cautery in the middle of the stomach according to this form; and this should be a cautery according to the form of a circle according to this form:  (Note: This line implies the existence of accompanying diagrams showing the shapes of the cauteries.)
And let the stomach be cauterized, paying attention to what is feared from this cautery: and it is that you mark on the stomach points according to the quantity you desire with a probe, and then cauterize those points with a cautery; and then treat it with the previously described treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 27.
On the cauterization of the cold liver.
When pain arises in the liver due to coldness, or humidity, or excessive flatulence, so that it is thrown from its normal fullness by superfluous egress, and the patient is treated with what we have said and it doesn't help, then it is necessary that the patient be supine upon his neck, and mark with a probe three cauteries according to this form and this same quantity upon the liver under the hypochondria, where the patient's lying down allows. (Again, this implies accompanying diagrams showing the placement and size of the cauteries.)
And let the space which is between each cautery be according to the thickness of a finger, and let the cautery be according to the length of a straight finger.
And do not compress too much with the cautery.
And if the quantity of what is burned is the amount of half the thickness of a finger, and no more is added; and if the patient is standing on his feet; and if he is not standing, then let him be lying down, having already extended his thighs, and raise his arms.
And it is possible for you to cauterize these cauteries with a knife-cautery, when you have subtlety and prudence in the art.
And beware that you do not proceed too much in the cauterization, and let the thickness of a whole finger be burned so that the whole body is burned and it reaches the intestines, because the finger here is subtle; and know this.
Chapter 28.
Of the perforation of an abscess of the liver with a cautery.
When an ulcer or abscess develops in the liver and you want to know if that abscess is in the flesh of the liver or in its tunic, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient finds gravity and pain without sharpness.
And if it is in the tunic of the liver, with the pain there is great sharpness and sometimes it tires the doctors in its treatment.
Therefore, the patient should be positioned on their side; and then mark the location of the abscess with a probe; and then ignite the cautery which resembles a rake, the form of which was shown on the previous page. And cauterize with that cautery until all the pus is consumed, until it reaches the bottom, until healthy blood flows.
And then treat it with the treatment for other abscesses until it is healed.
And it is not fitting that this type of cautery be used except by one whose study in the art of medicine is long and in whose hands many illnesses have been known; but this one, with some experience, may sometimes be bold in this operation, yet let that remain to us, and it is better.
Chapter 29.
On the cauterization of pleurisy.
The ancients said that cautery with the roots of birthwort for cold pleurisy, according to this manner, is effective, and it is this: that you take of the long, dry roots of birthwort a root one finger long that is found, and let it be of the thickness of a finger.
Then submerge that in oil and hold it to the fire, and then cauterize with one cautery in that place which is between the continuation of the clavicle and the neck; and with two small cauteries below the chin, slightly inclined towards the part that is below the beard, and with two cauteries in that which is declining between the fifth rib and the sixth, declining slightly towards the back; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest; and with another above the stomach, and with three cauteries on the back; and with one cautery in that place which is between the two shoulders, and with two cauteries on the two sides of the chest, below the cautery which is between the two shoulders.
And it is not necessary that you grasp your hand with the cautery, but rather let it be in the evident part of the handle in operation.
And some ancients said that some men use a cautery of iron similar to a ray and ignite it, and put it in that place which is between the ribs, until we reach with it that abscess and it is purged of the sickness, just as we have said in the abscess of the liver.
But this perforation with a cautery is not without danger, for one might easily kill the patient in the place, forming a fistula for which there is no cure.
Chapter 30.
On the cauterization of the spleen.
When you treat the disease of the spleen with what we have said of the treatment in the division and it does not improve, then cauterize it in three ways, all of which are proper. One is to make three or four cauteries lengthwise according to the length of the spleen, following the shape of the liver cauteries, the shape of which has been previously described.
And let the width between each cautery be the width of a finger or a little more, and the manner of cauterization should be the same as that which we described for the cauterization of the liver.
And do not press your hand on the cautery; and let the patient be lying on their side.
And another way to cauterize is to ignite the cautery using two points, the reason for which we have explained in the chapter on muscle dislocation, and raise the caul that is in the area of the spleen where the patient's left side lies.
And let your elevation, with which you raise the caul, follow the length of the body, so that cauteries are made according to the width of the body.
And then insert the two ignited points into that space so that with them you penetrate the caul on the other side.
And then withdraw the cautery; and thus four cauteries are made.
And if you wish, cauterize with a cautery having three points.
And then you must tend the cautery until it has ceased bleeding for many days, for this is more important than the other preceding procedures in the treatment.
Chapter 31.
Concerning the cauterization of a dropsical patient.
Cauterization is not applied to all dropsical patients, but only to certain ones specifically.
When you treat a dropsical patient with the methods of treatment which we have described in the division and your treatment is not effective, then it is necessary that you cauterize with four cauteries in a circle around the navel, and with one cautery over the stomach, and with another cautery over the liver, and with another cautery over the spleen, and with two cauteries between the vertebrae of the back, and one cautery on the right foot, and with another cautery over the stomach. And the depth of the cautery should be proportionate to the thickness of the skin.
And then leave the cautery open so that it discharges pus for a long time.
And do not deprive the patient of treatment after cauterization with what is necessary, so that two intentions are combined in him, and he will be hastened to healing with assistance.
The shape of the cautery with which the abdomen is cauterized should be nail-shaped, as its form is described above; and that with which the back is cauterized should be olive-shaped, as its form is described above.
Chapter 32.
Concerning the cauterization of the feet and thighs.
When the feet are inflamed with dropsy and filled with yellowish water, it is necessary to cauterize the feet on the dorsum of the foot, between the little toe and the ring finger. Raise your hand with the cautery and do not twist it; then raise your hand and do not completely return it, for the yellowish water will flow out.
The cautery is according to this form: Then make two cauterizations on the two thighs, and on each thigh a cauterization should be made with the knife-like end of the cautery. And let the cautery be according to the length of the thigh: one below the knee and the other below the middle of the thigh; and on each thigh two cauteries.
And leave the wound open without treatment for a long time so that some water may drain from it.
And then treat it with those things which we have mentioned.
Chapter 33.
On the cauterization of the abdomen.
When the abdomen is cold and damp, weakening its functions – that is, the digestive and retentive functions which are in the stomach and intestines – and is treated with methods of healing and fails to improve, and sometimes the patient suffers a cauterization exhibiting its effectiveness, then cauterize it with a large cautery over the stomach, as was previously described in the cauterization of the stomach, with a circular cautery and four small cauteries around the navel, with a thin awl-shaped cautery; and with a cautery on the hypochondrium, on a large aloe, or with two cauteries.
And if sometimes the humors are excessive and the patient tolerates it, then cauterize it over the pubic region and over the intestines, and quickly apply two cauteries near the stomach and near the groin, for this is a perfect and helpful cure, which you should not miss.
Chapter 34.
On the cauterization of chronic hemorrhoids.
When there are in the lower part of the body chronic hemorrhoids, or those caused by thick, cold humors or corrupted moisture, and they are treated with what we have said in the division and the cure fails, then cauterize with three cauteries over the most inferior part of the spine, small and triangular, and with a cautery above the navel, according to the two aforementioned methods.
And if you suspect that his stomach is cold and his food is undigested, and you see his face inflamed, then cauterize the stomach with a large cautery, as previously described; and with one cautery on the liver, and with another cautery on the spleen using a nail-shaped cautery, and leave the cautery open for some time, and then treat him until he is healed.
Chapter 35.
On the cauterization of a fistula near its excision.
When you excise a fistula, then you ignite the cautery that resembles a ray (or, a spoke). Then insert that ignited cautery into the excised fistula, and control your hand until the cautery reaches the opening of the vein from which the blood comes.
And do this once or twice, for if the fistulas are many, then cauterize each of them as we have recounted, and then treat them with that which you apply to them in ointments until they are healed.
And if you cauterize with a large cautery on something, it is slower in healing.
Chapter 36.
Chapter 36. On the cauterization of fistulas that are formed naturally in the private parts.
When nature does not obey the work which we have recommended in its place, and the patient is frightened by it, let it be healed with cautery.
Therefore, when someone has a fistula in the private parts, and it is old,  it is eating away at the flesh and is filled with corrupt humors. Then what is most appropriate is to probe it with a thin probe, and then ascertain the depth with the aforementioned probe.
Then ignite the cautery which is similar to a ray (presumably meaning a pointed cautery). And then insert that ignited cautery into the fistula, according to the depth determined by the probe.
And repeat the cautery application until those corrupt tissues are burned, once or twice or three times, according to your needs.
And beware of burning nerves and veins; or if the fistula goes towards the bladder or the intestine, then beware of all those areas; and do not do this unless the fistula is in fleshy tissue and you believe it is not penetrating deeply.
And then care for the area until it is healed.
And if the area is sealed, and the matter has been excised from it, and remains so for a long time, then know that it is entirely cured.
And if the matter has not been removed from it, then know that it is penetrating, or the bone itself is corrupted, or it comes from those causes which will be recalled in their place (i.e., discussed later in the text).
Chapter 37.
On the cauterization of sores.
When in sores pain arises from cold or great windiness, and because of this the patient's skin is damaged, then it is necessary that you cauterize that sore with a cautery on each sore, with the pointed cautery of which mention has previously been made.
And quickly cauterize again a little below, so that three linear cauteries will result, and then they will be more helpful in treatment.
Chapter 38.
On the cauterization of the bladder.
When weakness and softening occur in the bladder due to cold and humidity, so that the patient cannot retain urine, then cauterize that area in the lowest part above the bladder, where the hairs of the pubis begin, and cauterize with one cautery on the right side and with another on the left.
And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the walnut; and cauterize that area with a cautery in the lowest part of the back, or with two cauteries if necessary.
And let the cautery be a nail-shaped cautery, as is appropriate.
Chapter 39.
Concerning the cauterization of the breast.
When cold and dampness come to the breast, and therefore conception is prevented, and her menses are corrupted, and their provocation is delayed, and pain occurs with their arrival, then it is fitting that the area be cauterized with three cauteries in a circle around the navel, as we have said in the cauterization of the bladder; and with a cautery on the area below the back, or with two cauteries, and let the cautery be nail-shaped.
Chapter 40.
Concerning the cauterization of a dislocated hip.
When mucous humors are poured into the area of the hip, and are the cause of its displacement, and a sign of this is that the thigh is lengthened compared to the other when they are compared. And the location of the dislocation is found where it protrudes.
Then it is necessary that you cauterize the patient on the affected part of their hip with a circular cautery, after you have marked with a probe the circumference of the affected area as it is swollen, so that the affected area falls within the middle of the circle.
And let the cautery be of the kind used previously in the cauterization of the stomach.
And if this cautery is not at hand, then cauterize it with three cauteries using a large olive-shaped cautery.
And let the cautery be done according to the size of the thickness of the whole rib; and then care for it until it is healed.
Chapter 41.
On the cauterization of the sciatic vein.
When pain comes in the hip area, and it is caused by cold and dampness, the patient is treated with what we have said and do not deviate, and this is ancient practice; then you should purge the patient of thick humors with foul-smelling pills or with artificial pills or similar; and then cauterize that area.
And the manner of cauterization in that case is according to two methods: either cauterization with burning medicines, or cauterization with fire.
If according to the two known methods, one of which is to cauterize that area on the patient's hip with three triangular cauteries according to this form; and make them of good depth. And let the space which is between each cautery be the amount of the thickness of a finger, whether the cautery is olive-shaped or large.
But sometimes let the cautery be in the middle of the place, on the head of the hip, so that there are four cauteries.
And if you wish, cauterize with the circle whose form precedes in the cauterization of the hip dislocation, so that it is a continuous circle and burns the thickness of the whole hip.
And this is the method of cauterization in sciatica when the pain is adherent to the hip and is not extended to the thigh.
But if it is extended to the thigh, then cauterize that with two cauteries on the thigh, on the place to which the patient points as the location of the pain; and with one cautery on the hip, four finger-widths below the left side.
And if the cautery is knife-shaped, let the depth of the cautery be according to the thickness of the hip.
And if the patient indicates that the pain is extended to the area near the toes, then cauterize there where indicated with a cautery for three or four points, or more if needed.
And if the patient indicates that the pain is below the buttock towards the thigh, then cauterize there with a knife-shaped cautery.
And be careful in all cauterizations that you do not use the cautery so much that you burn nerves or cause great injury, and that because of this there is no bad consequence or contraction for the patient.
And I have seen one and another of those whom I cauterized on the ankle and overdid it in the cauterization, and the leg became swollen until the swelling reached the foot, and the whole foot was perforated and corrupted. And then a strong case of diarrhea occurred, and then death.
And if the pain is in two places at once, cauterize each according to this manner.
And the wise ones say concerning the cauterization of the ankle, a cautery of which this is the manner: make a ring of iron, of which the circumference should be half a palm; and let its opening be according to the thickness of a date pit, or a little less.
And within that circle let there be another, a third; and let the space which is between them be the quantity of a walnut; and let those circles be open on two sides; and let the length of those irons be a walnut or two walnuts, and make in those irons a mark properly prepared in the circles; and this is its form:
Then let it be heated in the fire until it glows red and throws off sparks, and then let it be placed on the ankle, the patient lying on his side. And then cauterize that with three round cauteries at once.
Then leave that for three days; then plaster it with a bandage and leave the wound open for many days; and then cure it with ointment until it is healed.
Says Albucasis: O, son, of this type of cautery we have used little, because of the fear of looking at it, and because we have found few who dare look upon it.
But of the good cauteries to him who suffers them, and finds with it the location of it.
But cauterize with adherent medicines so that you make two circles having a perforated armature in which you place the ring of the iron door; let the length of the walls in the height be two nuts or whatever those are.  (Note: This translation is difficult due to the archaic and possibly corrupt nature of the text. The meaning of "armula perforada" and the comparison to nuts is unclear).
And if the spaces which are between each are the quantity of the thickness of the finger; and let them be open below and above, and let them contain themselves between them according to this form: (Again, this is difficult due to ambiguity; the description of the spatial arrangement is unclear).
Then place the patient on their side with the affected part of the hip upwards, and compress your hand on the part of the hip; then take hot water and pour it between the two circles, and let the heat be applied slowly and gradually, and keep it there for about an hour, and let the patient endure the biting and burning of it — for this produces a burning sensation like fire — until the burning ceases.
Then raise your hand with the instrument and wipe the hot water from the whole hip with fresh water.
Then leave it for three days and apply a poultice to it until the blackness of the burn is removed, and leave it for some days, and pus will drain from it; this is later aid/treatment.
Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if the pain extends to the hip or leg, apply a bath to it containing water, in the same way as was done for the hip.
And this is the manner of hot water treatment, and I have already written about this in the treatise on the preparation/refinement of medicines. Take alkali and quicklime, an equal part of each, and crush them and place them in a new pot, the bottom of which is pierced with a small hole, according to the amount that needs to enter the rectum.
And place another inverted pot under the bottom of the pot, and pour over the alkali and quicklime fresh water which covers them by one finger's width, and then when you have compressed them with your hand with a good compression, allow the sharp water to descend from above into the inverted pot below.
And then collect all that water, and pour it over more fresh quicklime and alkali, for then it will be of very strong acidity, which is used in many medical operations and in cauterizing other members, because it works like fire.
And some medicines with which cauterization is also done, such as verdigris and honey mixed with quicklime and soap in such a way that they are mixed together.
And Galen recounted the operation and cure of a man of old times, by which sciatic nerve and hip pain was cured, and greatly praised its effectiveness, and said that no other cure was needed, and because it was healed at once as he recounted, because very quickly that man entered the bath and was already free from it.
And it is that fresh rue should be taken, and if fresh rue is not found, a fresh fig should be taken; and it should be very well crushed with a little grease and placed on the hip where the pain is, or on the thigh, or on the leg, and bound on.
And it should be left on for three hours, or for as long as the patient feels relief from the burning.
Then put him in a bath, and when his body is reddened, expose him to the sun, because the pain ceases and he is healed.
And know that one should not use cautery with these medicines except after the evacuation of the body.
But already Dioscorides said that when the varicose vein is cauterized with goat's dung, it will benefit him very well; and the cautery should be done in this manner: wool should be taken and soaked in old oil, and placed between the toes and the ankle.
Then take dried goat dung and burn it until it becomes charcoal; and then place it on the wool and leave it there until it is extinguished. Then repeat this, and do not cease doing this until the sensation of the cauterization reaches the ankle and relieves the pain.
And this manner of cautery is called cautery of embers (or glowing coals).
Chapter 42.
Of the cauterization of toothache.
Sometimes pain is in the teeth from various causes, such as a fall or a blow, or excessive evacuation, and similar causes, by infusion of cold and humid matter.
And cautery is only suitable in that single instance which is caused by an effusion of cold matter.
Therefore, after evacuating the patient with suitable pills and similar remedies, make three lines on the tooth where the pain is, according to its width, applying a cautery mark to each line, or more, depending on the patient's tolerance and their constitution.
And let the cauterization be done with a pointed cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize with three or four cauteries using a nail-shaped cautery according to this form:
Chapter 43.
On cauterization at the beginning of gibbosity (or humpback).
Many times this illness befalls small children; and a sign of its beginning in children is that it happens to their structure in the wing (shoulder blade area) with elevation and movement.
And you find in the last vertebra of the back and the buttocks that it (the gibbosity) already begins to rise above the other vertebrae.
And therefore, when you see this and want to prevent it, then cauterize that (area) with a cautery that is circular according to this form: so that the cautery takes in all parts of the vertebra equally.
And if you wish, cauterize in the circumference of the vertebra with a pointed cautery two or three lines (meaning, two or three short cauterizations). And let the points be close together.
And then care for the area until it is healed with what we have said.
But beware that cautery is not administered in swelling caused by a spasm of the nerves.
Chapter 44.
Concerning the cauterization of gout and joint pain.
When joint pain is caused by cold humors which are infused into each member of the body, then when pain comes in the feet, it is the custom of doctors to call this gout properly.
And thus, if gout caused by cold is cured with a method of treatment and the pains are not removed, then cautery removes them.
And it is so that you cauterize that place for evacuation in the area of the joint of the foot with many cauteries.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped, of which this is the form:
And if it is necessary that a puncture be made on the surface of the foot, then do it with a pointed cautery. And if the pains rise into the knees or other joints, and this often happens, then cauterize that place on each knee with three or four cauteries on each side with this olive-shaped cautery.
And if many cauteries are necessary, then cauterize that place and do not press too hard with your hand with the cautery beforehand; let it only be around the thickness of a finger.
And if the pains rise into both knees, then apply the cautery which we have described in its chapter.
And if the pains are only in the hands, and it is called a hand-ache, then puncture in the area of the veins, thus as they twist, puncture in those two lines.
And if pain remains in the fingers, then puncture in the area of the veins on each finger and on the palm of the hand.
And if the pains reach the joints or muscles after some days, then cauterize them on every side of the affected area.
And do not neglect the patient's good regimen and reception of medicines, for if a good regimen is followed and the inflammation is evacuated with this cautery, he will be healed, God willing.
Chapter 45.
On the cauterization of fractures.
When a rupture occurs in the groin, pushing the intestines in front of the scrotum, and this is beginning to happen, then it is necessary that you order the patient to stop eating that day and that he use soothing remedies so that the tear of his abdomen subsides.
Then hold the patient between your hands on his back, and command him to hold his breath until the hernia or intestines are expelled or appear.
Then return that [the hernia/intestine] with your fingers and gently manipulate the rupture over the pubic bone, noting that the extremity of the rupture should resemble half a circle in the upper parts of the body.
Then ignite the cautery, whose form is this:
until it is very red and throws off sparks. Then return the intestine or hernia to its cavity, and place your hand over the area so that the intestine does not come out.
And you have already opened the space between the patient's thighs and placed a cushion underneath; and let another servant be on his legs, and another on his feet, who will hold his hands.
Then place the cautery on that mark with your hand, holding the cautery straight, and hold it there until you reach with it the bone of the hip joint in the middle of the thigh; and repeat this another time if you did not reach the bone on the first attempt.
And observe carefully the position of the intestine during your cauterization, so that you do not burn the intestine, lest death or other great harm come upon the patient.
And know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your operation is not complete/successful.
And it is necessary that the cautery for children be small according to their size and measure.
Then care for the cauterized area for three days with a poultice until the burn scar is healed.
And then treat it with other ointments until it is healed.
And the patient should lie on their back for 40 days until the area and wound are sealed/healed.
And it is necessary that you give him nourishment according to the length of his treatment, which will cleanse his belly, and that the intestine does not protrude due to the effort of healing.
And then, when he wants to get up after about 40 days, apply a properly made bandage, and keep it on for another 40 days.
And let him be sparing of work and of abundant food and drink, and of loud voices, for when he uses this regimen of complete healing he will be healed.
And I will describe the treatment of ruptures in other parts of the belly; and if, at the beginning, you want them to not protrude, then cauterize the rupture with a round cautery according to its size, and let what is excised from the hernia be about one-third of it; and then treat it with what we have said, and it is not adjusted. (The last phrase, "and it is not adjusted," is somewhat unclear and may refer to a previous instruction or a need for further adjustment of the treatment.)
And some earlier authorities said that a rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery according to this form, since it is marked with a probe:
and a line is placed across the widest part of the rupture, and another line above it; and place your hand with a pointed cautery, but the first cautery is lighter and better.
Chapter 46.
On the cauterization of torsion (or twisting/strangulation, likely referring to a strangulated hernia).
When pain develops in any limb due to a fall or a blow, and that pain persists for a long time and does not respond to treatment with medicines, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And let the cauterization of each limb be according to its size and smallness, and its strength and weakness, and the location of the torsion and its pain.
Therefore, if it is healed by the first cauterization, it is good; and if not, then repeat the cauterization on it; for it is customary for these pains to be punctuated from one limb to the one next to it, so it is necessary to treat those with cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
On the cauterization of leprosy.
Lepers are sometimes helped greatly by cauterization, especially if the type of leprosy is caused by putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
Therefore, if you wish to cauterize a leper, consider whether the leprosy is spreading and whether it has been treated with what we have said in the section, and whether it does not spread or remain, and we fear that the corruption will affect the whole of their body and their entire complexion, then cauterize them on the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, and a second lower down, toward the forehead at the hairline; and two on the temples; and one behind, on the nape of the neck.
And lastly, with the cautery, press your hand slightly inwards so that it makes a small impression on the bone, and the crusts are dislodged from it, so that the expulsion of thick vapors is relieved from it; and cauterize over the spleen as necessary.
And if the leprosy is already widespread on the patient and manifestly apparent, then you should cauterize them with those cauteries which we have mentioned on the head; and with a cautery on the tip of the nose, and with two cauteries on the two cheekbones, and with two cauteries on two vertebrae of the neck, and with six cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and with one large one on the vertebrae near the elbow, and one on the elbow, and with two on the hips, and one on each hip, and with two on the knees and one on each knee, and with two on the ankles, and with two on the muscles of the calves of their legs; and cauterize on all the joints of the fingers and toes, and on each heel of their feet, and on the wrists of their hands.
And beware of the nerves which are on the heel, that you do not burn them.
And sometimes it is cauterized with one cautery on the pubic bone and another on the stomach opening, and another on the liver.
And know that the more precisely you apply the cauteries, the more helpful and beneficial it is; and know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery, as they do from incisions, because their body is already numbed.
And the cauteries should be large or small according to the limbs and joints, as prescribed by the types of instruments.
Then treat the cautery with cooked barley flour mixed with honey and with other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
On the cauterization of numbness/stupor.
When a limb is numb or asleep and has been treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then you cauterize that numb limb with cauteries according to the size or smallness of the limb.
And let your cautery penetrate a little into the thickness of the skin. And afterwards cure that with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes some numbness is cauterized which extends to the hands and feet, and to the vertebrae of the back near the origin of the nerves which move that limb, and the numbness is relieved.
But do not attempt this except one who is skilled in the anatomy of the limbs and the course of the nerves moving the whole body.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the cauterization of white patches which are morphea (vitiligo).
When the white patches are old and you do not apply any medicinal ointments, then cauterize it with a cautery iron a little, according to the size of the affected area, until the whiteness and its color are relieved, and its color is changed.
Then treat it with flour of barley and lentils with rose oil and with arugula leaves, and with pigeon blood or with swallow blood, each in equal parts and all mixed together, and then apply a linen cloth over the area until it is healed.
Chapter 50.
Concerning the cauterization of cancer.
When the cancer is beginning and you want to stop it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery iron around its circumference as it is swollen.
And the wise men say that it should be cauterized with the pointed cautery iron in the middle; but to me this is not advisable, for I fear that it will be ulcerated, and I have already seen this happen on several occasions.
And it is more appropriate that it be cauterized around its circumference with a circle as we have said, or with several cauteries.
Chapter 50.  (Assuming a numbering system consistent with Roman numerals,  L = 50)
Concerning the cauterization of a boil/abscess. (The word "adubilati" seems to refer to a type of swelling or inflammation;  "boil" or "abscess" are likely modern equivalents).
When a boil/abscess is delayed in maturation, and this is due to the superfluity causing it, or to the age of the patient, because he is old and decrepit, of little blood, or because of the time, and you want to hasten the maturation of the boil/abscess; then cauterize around it with many small, pointed cauteries; and then leave it alone, for it will hasten maturation.
But if you want to perforate it with a cautery, then you heat the cautery, of which this is the shape: and place it in the middle of the boil/abscess until it penetrates the skin.
And if the cautery is in that place which is lower down, so that the body of the arm may be relieved; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 52.
On cauterization with a corrosive.
A corrosive is a corruption moving in the limb, which corrodes it like fire consuming dry wood.
Therefore, if you see this corrosive in a place which has suffered cauterization with fire, then you should ignite very small, hot nails, and large ones as appropriate for the place where the corrosive is.
And then cauterize it completely until you eradicate all the corrosion and none of it remains.
And then leave it for three days, and place crushed sulfur mixed with oil on the cauterized areas until all the scabs are removed and all the corruption is gone.
And then treat it with a flesh-generating ointment.
