But if after three days you see that healthy flesh has grown where there is no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cauterization on whatever remains in the corrupted areas.
And corrosion is sometimes treated with a sharp medicine, for that is a substitute for cauterization. But however, cauterization with fire is of quicker help.
And I have already spoken of its treatment with sharp medicine in the section, and therefore take that when you have need of it.
Chapter 53.
Of the cauterization of wounds and other similar things.
Many times, a disease called a wound occurs in the parts below the foot, and it is a large, penetrating wound which injures the foot; and cauterization in these cases is done in two ways, that is to say cauterization with fire and cauterization with hot water.
But cauterization with fire is to heat the concave cautery, similar to a vulture's beak, which is made of iron according to the size which contains the wound in its entirety, and is of fine edges.
And then place that heated iron on the wound, and then move your hand with the cautery in a circle around the wound until the cautery reaches the depth of the wound. And leave it there for three days until it begins to shed pus. And then plaster it with crushed wild mallows mixed with salt, and leave the plaster on it for one night, for this will eradicate it from its roots.
And then treat the area with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
And if it is not healed, and this often happens on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet, then you must apply cautery with water to these areas.  Take a cannula of bronze or iron or vulture bone, and place it over the wound; then pour a small quantity of hot water into the cannula, and hold your hand, and you move it with compression of your hand entirely, and press the edge of the cannula into the root of the wound, and the hot water will find its way to the root of the wound; and let the patient endure the bite of the hot water for a little while, for about an hour, and then remove it, for the wound with its roots will be eradicated.
And do this to those [wounds] and then to the others until you have treated all those that are in the body.
And then treat those sores, once the roots of the wounds are eradicated, with those ointments which generate flesh.
Chapter 54.
On the cauterization of tremor.
When someone suffers from tremor due to cold in the nerves, or from a fever of a cold origin, or from a similar cause, then you should cauterize them with four or five cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, between each vertebra with one cautery; and with a cautery on their feet, and on their stomach, with an olive-shaped cautery.
And their tremor will subside, and hasten the healing of the cold illness.
Chapter 55.
On the cauterization of accidental blemishes on the body.
When blemishes appear on the body, and they are pustules or fissures caused by thick, corrupt matter; then, at the beginning of their appearance, it is necessary that they be cauterized on the head of the fistula with a thin cautery, with myrtle wood, the end of which is ignited with fire; or with a long root of erestologia; or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And sometimes, a cautery is also applied at the beginning of their expulsion in this manner; and they are not believed [to be effective], and the superfluity is disrupted by doing this, and heals from them.
However, it is necessary that this be done near evacuation with phlebotomy.
Chapter 56.
Chapter 56. On the cauterization of a blood flux accidental to an incision from an arrow.
Many times, there is a flux from an arrow wound that is incised near the wound, which comes from outside, or near the incision of abscesses or cauteries of the limb, and the like; and it is difficult to stop that.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then you press with your hand to the orifice of the arrow wound, and place your finger on it, tightly pressing it many times until the flow of blood is stopped under your finger, and it does not come out.
Then, put small and large olive-wood cauteries in the fire, many in number, and blow on them until they are very hot.
Then take one of those, small or large depending on the location and the wound where the artery is broken,  on that vein quickly, after you have removed your finger, and hold the cautery there until that blood is stopped and restrained.
And if the blood is expelled from the orifice of the artery when you remove your finger and take away the cautery, then take another cautery quickly from among the cauteries that are prepared in the fire, and do not cease to do this one after another until the blood is restrained.
And be careful that you do not burn the nerve that is there, and that another injury does not befall the patient.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, it is not possible to restrain that blood in those cases, and especially when the artery is large, except in one of four ways: either with a cautery as we have said, or with extraction if it is not extracted, for when it is extracted the extremities are better cauterized and the blood is restrained, or that it be tied with thread or with a ligature by strong constriction, or that medicines be placed upon it whose virtue is to restrain blood; and they should be tightened with a cushion with decent constriction.
But that which administers decent restriction with a ligature and with constriction with cloths or by the application of cauterizing agents and similar things, does not work except slowly.
But if this happens to someone and the physician or medicine is not present, then open slowly with the index finger on the orifice of that vein as we have said, and attend to its strong constriction until the blood is stopped from the principal part of the wound, and the finger is not removed from the artery; and over the artery let it be sprinkled with very cold water until the blood is congealed and thickened; and consider further what need there is of cautery or medicine.
Of incisions and perforations and cupping and of phlebotomy and of wounds and their similars.
We have already stated in the first chapter every illness in which cautery with fire is suitable and with burning medicine, and the occasions and causes of it, and the necessary instruments with the forms of the cauteries; and I have ordered the chapters from the head down to the feet.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner, so that the one seeking this may be helped, then on that which he will have asked.
And before I begin this, it is necessary, son, that you learn that in this chapter is what is terrifying in the first chapter on cauterization.
And because it is necessary that there be great caution in it, for in the operation which is in this chapter, many times there happens an evacuation which is with blood with which life is sustained, through the opening of a vein or incision upon an abscess, or opening of a fistula, or purification of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision of a stone, and their like. And with all of these are accompanied fear and trembling, and in most of them death happens.
And I admonish you that this, in which there is hesitation, does not befall you, for sometimes you will handle men with many kinds of illnesses. And of them there is some who are angry with their illness and death is light to them, and this because of the vehemence of what they find in their illness, and because of the length of their illness, and with the illness is a fear which signifies death.
And of those there is one who gives you his possessions and says, you hoping with that for health, and his illness is pernicious.
Therefore it is not fitting that you consent to that which comes to you from which is this handling; and your caution should be greater than covetousness and your rapacity; and do not presume any cause about this except with certain knowledge which verifies this to you, and to which a praiseworthy outcome follows.
And use in the cure of all your patients prognostication and indication of him to whom health is restored, for in that is to you help towards acquisition of fame and memory, of praise and of glory.
O, sons, may God inspire you with his understanding and not hinder your guidance and protection, for that is in his hand.
And I have ordered this chapter, distinguishing it by chapters as preceded in the chapter on cauteries, from the care of the head to the feet, so that this which you ask for and of which you have need may be relieved for you.
Chapter one.
Of the treatment of the water which is contained in the heads of children.
Know that this illness often happens to children at birth and when the nurse binds the child's head too tightly.
And sometimes it happens for a hidden cause which is not known.
But I have only seen this illness in children; and in all those whom I saw, death followed, and thus it is in my view a deadly affliction for them.
And I have seen that a child whose head was filled with water, and his head was always thus enlarged until he could not support himself because of the size of his head, and moreover the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture is either collected between the skull and the bone, or it is collected under the bone on the brain.
For the operation in that case, if the moisture is between the skull and the bone and the abscess is small, then it is necessary that you make a fissure in the middle of the head in width, and the length of the fissure should be about two finger-widths so that the moisture can run out, and this is the form of the spatula:
And therefore if the moisture is more increased and the abscess is larger, then there are two fissures in this manner:
And if the moisture is under the bone, the sign of it is that you see the sutures of the head open on all sides and the water is squeezed out when you compress that with your hand on the inside parts, and this is not hidden from you. It is necessary that you make a fissure in the middle of the head with three fissures in this manner:
And then extract all the moisture from the incision; and then bind the fissures with cloths and soft feathers; and then wash it over with wine and oil until the fifth day, and then untie the bandage and treat the wound with tents and ointments, and bind the head evenly; and let the patient eat dry food with little moisture until the member is strengthened and healed.
And there is another method of incision, that you consider where the magnitude of the abscess and the collection of water appears, for sometimes it is more in the occiput, or in the front part, or on the right, or on the left; and therefore attend with the incision where the abscess and the repletion of water appear, and do it as best you can.
And observe that you do not cut the artery and that a flow of blood ensues and the patient dies from that flow of blood with evacuation of moisture.
Chapter Second (or Chapter Two).
When someone develops a catarrh or rheum in the eyes or nose, and this is chronic, and the cure is not achieved by medications, then the final cure in this case is incision of those two arteries.
And therefore, when you wish to incise them, have the patient raise their head, and thoroughly rub the area with a rough cloth so that the arteries appear, and the patient's neck should be constricted with a cloth; and carefully observe where the vein pulsates; and the location of each [artery], since the locations near the ears are depressed, and are thus hidden in some people.
And then mark the artery with a probe, and incise each one with a sharp lancet, making an incision that reaches to the bone, and let this be done across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the lancet under the artery and cut it at the incisions above.
And let the length of the incision be about two fingers' breadth, for when a vein is incised, the blood will flow out with a pulsatile emission, leaping forth with frequent spurts.
But if the artery does not appear to the touch, then you must measure from the ear a space of three fingers' breadth, and mark it with a probe, and proceed to the bone.
And the quantity of blood to be let is about six ounces, and you should quickly let out more or less, and all this depends on the strength of the patient and the fullness of their artery.
Then examine the wound, and if any cause of infection remains on the bone, incise it so that a hot abscess does not develop.
Then bind the wound tightly with lint and linen bandages, and treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Concerning the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
When someone suffers from chronic migraine or sharp headaches, due to acrimonious humors and a quality in the tissues of the temples, or a similar vehement and chronic condition, and treatment with medicinal methods does not help, then in these illnesses we have proven the extraction of the arteries in the temples, or their cauterization, as we have shown.
And the manner of the operation and extraction of these is that the patient shaves the hair which is on the temples; and attend to the artery apparent in the temple, for it is shown to you by its pulse, and it is hidden only in few men by the force of cold.
And if it is hidden from you, then the neck of the patient should be constricted with some cloth; and then rub the area with a rough cloth or evaporate the area with hot water until the artery appears to you manifestly. And then you take the spatula of which this is the form: And then you scrape the skin with that [spatula] easily until you reach the artery; and then put in it [the artery] a hook and extract it to the superior parts until you extract it from the skin and uncover it from the tunics which are beneath it, from every part.
But if the artery is subtle, then wrap it with the end of the hook, and then incise a part of it according to the quantity by which its ends are lengthened and contracted, and there will be no flow of blood, for if they are not incised, the blood will not rise at all.
And then evacuate from one to three ounces of blood.
And if the artery is large, then it is necessary that you tie it in two places with strong double silk thread or with cords at length, and do not allow it to putrefy before the consolidation of the wound and the occurrence of blood flow.
And then incise the superfluity which is between the two ligatures; and do this at that time or at another time.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery up to the bone with a knife-shaped cautery until its ends are incised, and it will be in place of the same work.
And it is a better procedure unless the patient has a fever or a hot complexion; for cautery is one of those things that helps to dry up humors, and therefore it is better in its application (or better for young people).
And it is necessary after the extraction of the arteries that you clean the place with old cotton, and that properly made powders are placed on it, and then it should be treated with dry medicines that cause the flesh to grow and with bandages until it is healed.
And if, while you are operating another time, a flow of blood from the artery occurs, then you must take care to restrain that blood, either with a cautery, or by filling the place with cotton, and pressing your hand until the bleeding is stopped. And if none of those things are at hand, then place your finger on the area until the blood is congealed, and dab the area with very cold water until the sharpness subsides, and it is constricted as needed.
And what is lighter in the extraction of an artery is that you cauterize the vein with this cautery having two blades, after you have marked the two places with a probe, and place each one, very hot, until they reach the bone and incise the vein in two places, so that what is between the extremities is elongated, because they are not completely consolidated with this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And let the sharpness of the two blades be equal. But however, if there is less sharpness than two knives it is better, because if they are sharp like two knives close to each other, they will not cut the flesh with speed.
Therefore, since in those there is some thickness, the heat of the fire is held in them and the flesh is cut lightly.
And this method is better in every respect and gentler.
And let the space which is between the two knives be the amount of the thickness of a finger.
Chapter Four.
Chapter Four: On the treatment of sharp and acrid tears from the eyes.
When there are acrid tears, and it is the flow from those veins which are on the head, and you find no cause for it in any medicinal treatment, and you see the patient's face redden and feel in their forehead a crawling sensation as if it were the crawling of ants, and their eyes are swollen, wet, so that the hairs around them are matted, and the eyelids are inflamed by the sharpness of the tears, the ancients were skilled in treating these cases with this operation, and they called this treatment "spassa".
And it is that you command the patient to shave the hairs which are on their forehead, and then incise three fissures in the forehead, equally distant according to the length of the forehead. And if the length of the fissure reaches two fingers: one fissure should be equidistant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second according to the length, a little above, of the movement of the muscles which are in the lacrimal duct, and the third on the other side.
And beware of incising the arteries which are on both sides, and keep your hand away from the continuation of both jaws. And if the space of each incision is the quantity of three fingers, adjust it.  (A more natural English phrasing might be: "And ensure that the space between each incision is about three finger-widths.")
And have ready a piece of sponge or dried cloths with which to dry the blood.
Then insert a spatula with two sharp ends, of which this is the form of its incision: which is from the part of the temples to the second incision. Excoriate with that [tool/spatula] the whole chain which is between the two incisions, with the instrument which is on the bone, and then do this from the middle incision to the second incision.  (Note:  "chain" here likely refers to a specific anatomical structure, possibly a vein or muscle group. "Ciphac" appears to be a surgical instrument.) And then remove that spatula and insert into the first incision again another instrument which is called a curette, sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And place the sharp edge of that [curette] against the flesh attached to the chain, and the blunt edge against the bone, and push it in until it reaches the middle fissure, and sever with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes.
But let the incision be continued to that appearance of the chain, and then do this in the middle fissure to the other fissure.
And after a quantity of blood has flowed, press the places of the congealed blood clots.
And then place in each fissure a pledget of old linen, and place upon them powders soaked in wine and oil and vinegar, and see that a hot abscess does not develop.
And on the third day, untie the bandage and administer a large infusion of warm or tepid water, and then treat it with basilicon ointment with rose oil and others until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5.
Chapter 5: On the treatment of tears from cataracts and those descending into the eyes from the inner parts of the head.
When someone suffers from sharp and persistent cataracts, and the patient's eyes appear small and sunken, and their gaze is weak, and the eyelids are ulcerated and their hair falls out, and there is sharp, persistent pain in the forehead and consequent sneezing, then know by these symptoms that the matter and cataracts come from the inner parts of the head.
And the best cure in such cases is this: order the patient to shave their forehead, and then make an incision in the middle of the forehead, a small one in width (beginning the incision from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision be between the bones; and extend your hand from the temples, which are sore due to chewing), until the bone is exposed.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or a dry cloth and then fill the space between the two lips of the incision with old cotton or linen cloths. And then bind it above with powders soaked in wine and oil, so that a hot abscess does not develop.
And when you unbind it and see that the abscess has diminished, then you should scrape the bone until new flesh begins to grow, and then treat it with a drying regimen that promotes the growth of flesh, such as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony and mix them, and make a plaster from them and apply it to these wounds to promote the growth of new flesh.
Chapter 6.
On the treatment of that which falls into the ears.
Everything that falls into the ears is one of four types, for it is either mineral stone or something similar to stone, such as iron and glass, or it is from a plant, such as a thistle or a bean, or it is a fluid substance such as water and sour wine and things similar to them, or some animal.
And so when a stone falls into the ear, or another similar substance from among those things which do not adhere to or grow in the ear, and you expose the ear to the sun, and if you see the stone, then instill into it some violet oil or sesame oil, and then proceed with its extraction, and induce sneezing with a suitable remedy, and block the nostrils to facilitate the occurrence of sneezing. Then place some pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and gently pull the ear upwards, for often it is expelled by these means.
And if it is not expelled, then proceed with its extraction using the instrument called a delicate ear-scoop, of which this is the form:
And if it is expelled with the scoop, good; and if not, then proceed with its extraction using a thin probe with a slight curve.
And therefore, if it is not expelled by this, then make a small tube of brass and insert the end of the tube into the opening of the ear and seal around the tube with wax and softened oil so that nothing is open except the tube. Then extract it with your breath with strong suction, for many times we have recounted this method.
But if it is not expelled in this manner, then take a small amount of lit tinder or birdlime, prepared for catching birds, and place it on the end of a ray [presumably a thin, straight object], and then carefully wrap a suitable bandage around it. Then gently insert that into the opening of the ear, and then dry the ear of its moisture.
And therefore, if it is not expelled by all the methods we have recounted, then you should consider cutting it out before it becomes a hot abscess or causes a spasm.
And the manner of cutting it out is that you first bleed the patient from the cephalic vein and extract the blood according to their strength, and then hold the ear between your hands and turn the ear upward, and make a small incision at the root of the ear below its cartilage, in a well-prepared location. And let the incision be crescent-shaped until the stone is reached; and, once the stone is found, remove it if you can with appropriate instruments; and then quickly close the incision, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the cause lodged in the ear is one of the growths that grows and is inflamed, then administer its treatment and extraction; but if it does not yield to such treatment, then take finely ground bone according to this method: and use that to remove the growth that has fallen into the ear.
And do not do this when you think that it is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, and place it on small weights, and then extract it with tweezers or with a delicate instrument, or with an incision as we have said, for its extraction is thus eased.
But if water has entered the ear, the patient should use sneezing initially, and fill his ear with some old cotton, and lie on the ear into which the water has entered; and if it is expelled by this, it is good; and if not, then take several smooth stones and pebbles, according to the length of the finger, and heat them slightly in the fire, and the patient inserts one of these into the opening of his ear and jumps on one foot on that side, and strikes with another stone upon the stone which is inside the ear. Therefore, do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled.
Therefore, do not cease this with the stone until all the water is expelled. And if the water is not expelled in this way, take some paper or tow, and place one end of it in the ear and hold the other end to a fire until the greater part of it is burned.
And then put in more tow and do this several times until all the water is expelled, and remove it with a cannula, as has been said above of the stone extractor.
But in the extraction of a creature entering that ear, if it is small, like a louse or similar, cure it as we have said in the division; but if it is large, which appears to the sense of sight, then administer its extraction with forceps and hooks, and its cause is the lightest of all other causes that fall into the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we have said in the division and in the treatise on distillations and your treatment does not work, then you should expose the ear to the sun.
And so if any cause of the worms appears there, then extract it with forceps or with fine hooks, but if no cause appears, then take a cannula that is narrow below and wide above, of which this is the form: And insert the narrow end into the ear according to the amount that the patient can tolerate; and then suck with it with strong suction, and do this many times, until all the worms are expelled.
But if it does not obey you in this way, then you fill and close the circumference of the cannula with wax as we have said; then administer distillations which the ancients have already proven in the treatment of worms, and you will find this in the treatise on distillations.
And let your effusion, with which you pour the medicinal oils into the ear, be with this instrument, of which this is the form: And make it of silver or bronze, narrow below, in which there is a small hole, and flat above.
And let it be such that it pushes what is in the cavity of the cannula continuously where it will be necessary. And if you wish, take a ray (presumably a thin rod or instrument) and wrap its end decently with cotton, and then pour oil into the concavity of the ear, or juice of some herb, or whatever you want of these medicines into the cannula—and that is in the ear—and then insert the ray with cotton on top and press with your hand, with moderate compression, until the oil is expelled into the concavity of the hearing and the patient feels the oil inside.
And what is poured into the ear should already be slightly warmed by fire. And take care that the substance which you distill into the ear is not too cold, for ear medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
Concerning the treatment of ear obstruction.
Sometimes children are born from their mothers' wombs without a perforated ear, and this also happens in some adults, an obstruction in the ear canal due to a wound or from flesh growing there; and this obstruction is sometimes in the pre-auricular area which does not allow hearing, and sometimes it is in the outer parts.
And that which happens in the pre-auricular area, which does not allow hearing, is in the greatest disposition of difficult healing.
And in that which allows hearing, it is necessary that you place the patient's ear in the sun and examine it. And then if you see an apparent obstruction, open it with a fine spatula, of which this is the form:
If its extremity is small in width, one side being sharp and the other not; and let the spatula be two finger-widths wide so that it does not injure the ear.
And then if the obstruction is due to flesh grown in the ear or in its meatus, then take it with fine hooks and incise it vigorously until all the flesh is removed.
And if the obstruction is in the preauricular area (the area in front of the ear), then take a fine, thin rod and heat it slightly with fire, and then place it in the ear canal.
And then if you have felt an obstruction with which the patient experiences difficulty, due to the severity of hearing, remove the obstruction gently; and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.
Then place in the ear a linen cloth, according to the size of the opening, which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment; and do this for several days until you are sure of the healing of the wound; or take a wick and soak it in rosewater, and powder it with crushed sage and use it; and if you see that the ear is already festering, then you must remove the wick and replace it with another wick which you will wrap in a plaster made with rose oil, until the abscess is completely healed; and then treat it until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs, then submerge a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use the other treatment which restricts the flow of blood, which is described in the treatise on distillation.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the treatment of a stye which occurs on the eyelids.
Styes which occur on the eyelids are sometimes moist, sometimes dry.
Therefore it is necessary that you hold them with forceps or with fingers, and incise them with a spatula from their roots, and then place a dry dressing on the affected area.
But if pain with bleeding occurs, then cauterize them with a thin, lentil-shaped cautery.
And the best cauterization is near the incisions, and often they return when some cause remains at their roots; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
Treatment of swelling that comes to the eyelids of the eyes.
Often there develops in the eyelids of the eyes some cause which is similar to a swelling in its strength and hardness, and because of this swelling it is called [a swelling].
And it is an accumulation of thick humidity in the eyelid, below and above.
Therefore, the operation on them is that you consider then if the swelling is proceeding in it in that which appears from the eyelid and is spread to every part; and its cause is light.
Then make an incision on it with a lancet across its width, and then peel it away from every part with ease until it comes out, and then suspend it with hooks and cut it off.
And if the growth is large, then you adjust it with sutures; and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, no harm will befall you because of it, for the ointment heals it and it will granulate (heal by forming new tissue).
And if the growth is extending to the inner parts of the eyelid beneath the inner surface, then raise the eyelid and suspend the growth with hooks,  even if you need to make an incision, and press it from every side, and if the eyelid is lengthened by the incision, this does not harm in any way.
And after the incision, wash the eye with salt water; and treat the area with a healing agent until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the treatment of a growth on the upper eyelid.
A growth is a swelling which is generated in the membranes of the upper eyelid, and especially in children; it obscures their eyes and causes them much pain; and they cannot look at the brightness of the sun because of this, for tears gather in their eyes.
And therefore you see them lying on their faces all the time, and sleeping on their faces; and their lower eyelids are damp beneath their eyebrows, which are already swollen, and the appearance of a growth is imminent in the eye; and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the growth beneath them.
And one method of operation is this: the patient places his head in your lap, and you place a cloth on him and make a compress for him, and make a circle according to the size which contains the whole growth, and then place it on the growth and press with your fingers all around so that the growth is gathered in the middle of the circle, and then make an incision in the middle of that swelling with a sharp instrument with a fissure, the fissure being no larger than the incision made in bloodletting.
But in the incision, it is necessary that you cut completely until you reach the growth, for in most cases the growth proceeds by fissure according to the form of a white swelling.
Then extract it with cloths which you have wrapped around your fingers at the outer edges and roll it between your right and left hand until it is removed.
And be careful not to press into the incision so that with the instrument you do not reach the eye and injure it.
But if the growth does not appear at the beginning of the incision, then press gently into the incision until the growth appears; and then extract it as we have said; and then soak cloths in warm wine and water and place them on the area and bind them with powders.
And some crush salt and place it in the cavity of the incision so that it dries up the remaining humidity.
And then care for it until it is healed.
But if a hot abscess occurs in the area, then treat it with soothing plasters until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the ways of touching the eye.
When in the eyelids of the eyes, pustules are formed under the natural hairs and are hardened, then these harm the eye and cause various diseases, such as constant tears and softening of the eyelids and membrane, until this causes destruction of the eye, which is called a tactus (touching, infection) of the eye.  And it is cured in four ways: either by cautery with fire; or with an acute medicine as previously stated in the chapter on cautery; or the tactus is treated by incision and suture; or by cannulas, as I shall explain.
Therefore it is necessary that you place the patient's head on your lap; then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand, and if it is turned, it is good; and if not, introduce a needle into that part where there is pus under the eyelid, and make the needle penetrate with the pus upwards, and this should be— near the margin of the eyelid where the hairs grow— near the hairs, and draw the pus to the upper parts with the eyelid, and turn it with the ray (presumably a surgical instrument or a light source).
Then make an incision in the inner part of the eyelid under the hardened pustules with a lancet from the larger lacrimal duct to the smaller lacrimal duct; and then extract the pus and place small pieces of cotton or cloth under the eyelid, and then bind over the eyelid with a bandage, and let it be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, the shape should be according to the amount of swelling of the eyelid, for this varies in people, because in some it is necessary that a large amount be incised from the eyelid, according to the amount by which the eyelid is softened.
Then make an incision with the lancet along the two lines which you have marked, beginning from the larger lacrimal duct to the smaller lacrimal duct. And if your incision is close to the natural hairs,  as thick as a king's (presumably referring to the thickness of a king's hair, implying a specific size).
Then place the hook in one of the two corners of the wen, and then excise it completely, and then approximate the two lips with a suture of fine wool or silk, and wipe away the blood, and attach what overflows from the wound to the two eyebrows with some sticky substance, if you wish to do so, and if not, do not worry.
Then leave the suture in place for three or four days, and then care for it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without a suture, and treat it with something that dries and constricts, because the eyelid is raised by the sealing of the wound and its granulation; but a suture is better. Therefore, the ancients advised this method of treatment.
However, let there be diligence on the part of the surgeon, and it is a good operation, and let there be no fear in him.
And there is another method of treatment, which is to mark on the eyelid a figure like a myrtle leaf, and then raise the eyelid with three separated and gathered hooks according to this form:
But if it is not possible to use hooks, and this is not suitable, then take a needle with thread and place it in the middle of the figure, and insert the other thread near the larger tear duct, and the second thread near the smaller tear duct, and adjust the threads between your fingers equally, and with these, raising your hand with even elevation, raise the whole area upon which it is marked as we have described.
Then, join the two lips of the wound with a suture, and treat it until it is healed.
But if an abscess develops near your incision or fissure, treat that abscess with a plaster and similar things, and with ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelid that its hairs are inverted; then you should use what we have said about incision; but suture in each case is more skillful.
Bandaging with canes is according to this form: and it is that you should turn back the eyelid and incise the figure that is within, as I have recounted; and then skillfully make two thin canes or splints whose length should be according to the length of the eyelid and whose width should be less than the width of the eyelid. And you have already incised at the extremities of each part, where you have the threads.
Then carefully add each [cane] with subtlety over that which supersedes from the eyelid of the eye, and bind the canes to each part with a strong ligature, and leave it for some days, for the hair, by the constriction, will die and be choked off and will rot until it falls out by itself.
But if it is delayed, then remove it with tweezers, and then cure the wound until it is healed.
Then, when it is solidified, the eyelid is raised and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of two canes which you will make according to this form: and have the quantity of length and width neither more nor less.
Chapter 12.
Chapter 12.  Of the elevation of prickly hairs in the eye with a needle when the hair is stiff.
When unnatural, hard, rough, and prickly hairs are in the eyes, then the operation is that you take a fine needle and place on it a fine silk thread, and then you carefully adjust its ends, and tie each one in a very subtle manner. And let the length of the threads be the quantity of a palm.
And then you attach two other fine threads below it briefly, and tie their ends if you wish.
And then have the patient place their head in your lap, and be near the sun so that your work is illuminated, for it is a subtle and delicate operation.
And then insert the needle into the root of the offending hair, which requires skill, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you extract it above the natural hair.
And then pull the hair upwards and insert it into a [healing] solution, and let the hairs be one or two or three, and no more.
And then pull your hand with it until the hair is pulled out with the [healing] solution in the eyelid, along with the natural hair.
But if the [healing] solution is pulled out and the hair is not removed, you will pull the [healing] solution toward the lower parts with the bile that you have mixed with it, until the [healing] solution is pulled out of the upper opening.
And observe that with the sight of the eye, for then you will remove the [healing] solution and the bile, and gently close the eye, and leave it closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place and good flesh grows over it.
And if the hair is too short, then you attach a long hair from the natural hairs, and it is elevated with that, or you let it be until it is lengthened to some extent, and then you elevate it.
Chapter 13.
Concerning the treatment of a stye which appears on the upper eyelid.
When a stye appears on the eye, and it is a contraction of the eyelid when it is contracted upwards, or to the right or left side, due to poor healing of a wound, incision, or cautery, and it is called lagophthalmos (leporina), and this contraction is either natural or accidental, and is caused by the healing of a wound, incision, or cautery, or by something similar poorly treated.
The method of operation is that you incise that consolidation and separate its lips, and place between them a small piece of linen cloth, and bind them until they are healed.
And it is not fitting that you use treatments that dry or constrict; for if you do this, the stye will return worse than it was before.
Use in this case things that soften, such as fenugreek, and apply it with water in which mallow and linseed have been boiled, or with unguent diaquilum, which should be liquefied with some oil, and soak the cloth in it, and treat it with this.
And the treasure of your treatment is that you prevent it from consolidating in a way different from its initial form, by any means necessary.
And this operation, which we have described, is nothing but a rectification of the stye so that it does not return to its previous form.
Chapter 14.
Of the treatment of a stye which is formed on the lower eyelid.
This stye, which forms on the lower eyelid—which is truly called a stye—has some natural causes, others accidental.
But an accidental stye is caused by a wound, or a cut, or cautery, or other similar things.
And the method of operating on it is that you take a needle with double thread, and insert it into the flesh, passing it from the left lacrimal duct to the right lacrimal duct, until the thread reaches both ends of the affected flesh.
Then stretch the flesh at the upper parts with the needle and sew it up with the needle, and incise it with a wide spatula. And if the eyelid returns to its proper shape, that is good; but if not, take a splint and place it in the split, and with it reshape the eyelid, and sew the two fissures in the lower eyelid.
And let the ends of the two fissures be the two angles of the incision, which you have made so that they meet, and let an angle be formed between them, so that when they are joined, a proper shape is made of them.
And this shape is the Greek letter: lambda (Λ).
Then remove the flesh from that side to the extent that the sharp lower eyelid is, so that it becomes the eyelid.
Then join the separated parts and sew them with woolen thread; and then treat them with what we have mentioned of emollient medicines and poultices until it is healed.
And if it has been excised by fissure, suture, or cautery, then it is necessary that you make a flat incision on the skin of the eyelid as previously described. Then separate [the wound] with a poultice between two lips as we have said.
In summary, concerning the treatment of an excision whether above or below, let the operation proceed according to the preparation of the excision, and this is in the form of many variations.
And the skilled surgeon always prepares the instruments in every possible way so that he may restore the form to its natural shape, or close to it, and that [he prepares] the patient according to what is suitable for him in terms of the operation and instruments in the best conditions.
Chapter 15.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or to the cornea.
When this consolidation happens to many men such that the upper eyelid is solidified with the white of the eye, and its natural movement and function are impeded, then you must insert the tip of a probe under the eyelid and elevate it, or extend it with a probe.
And then you incise the adhesion with a fine spatula, which should not be sharply pointed for incisions, but rather somewhat blunt, so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and move the spatula so that it does not injure the eye with its sharpness.
And let your incision gently scrape away that adhesion until the eyelid is returned to its natural form and the entire adhesion is healed.
And then you put salt water in the eye with which you wash it; or distill into it some honey dissolved in water.
And then separate the eyelid from the eye with a small linen cloth, and place wool infused with egg white on the eye, and then on the third day use consolidative remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the incision of the nail and the fleshy eminence of the tear ducts.
Know that a nail is according to two manners, for it is either nervous, and is similar to a hard, subtle sheath; or it is non-nervous, similar to a congealed white humidity, which when the iron touches it, or it is forced to be taken with forceps, is cut off and the forceps are not fixed into it.
And both types begin only from the major tear duct until it goes little by little to cover the vision and prohibits light and the movement of the eye.
But the manner of working in its incision is that the patient places his head on your lap, and then open his eyes, and elevate the eyelid with your hand, and then grasp the nail with small, double-pronged forceps and extend it to the upper parts. Then take a needle and insert into it a horsehair, or ox hair, or cow hair, or similar, or strong thread, or double the tip of the needle little by little and insert it into the middle of the nail, and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with thread; and extend it to the upper parts, and scrape with the hair the side of the nail which obscures the pupil. And thus you pull it with the hair towards the back of it.
And then you incise the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a fine spatula or with small scissors, and leave the natural flesh of the tear duct so that continuous flow of tears does not result from the incision of it.
And sometimes the nail, with the forceps alone, extended as we have said, and then it should be scraped with a lion spatula, of which this is the form:
And be careful that with the spatula you do not touch the cornea and that a rupture occurs in it, and that the pupil is perforated.
Then, after the incision, put a little crushed salt or a powder made from henbane into the eye, and leave it there until the next day; and then treat it with what is appropriate until it is healed.
And if the nail is not sensitive and you cannot insert the needle, nor is it fixed with forceps, then very often this type of nail is cured with medicines, and is healed as I have recounted in the section.
And if you wish to incise it, then you open the patient's eye and take a thin lion spatula of sharpened iron according to this form: with subtlety.  (or: delicately; skillfully) And if you see that it is rarefied and congested and rooted there, then distill into the eye your own juice, or henbane juice, or rue juice, or crushed salt, and plug the eye until the next day.
And then repeat the operation on it until all that is in it is removed, unless conversely a hot abscess forms in the eye, for then you leave it alone and treat the abscess first until it is healed.
And then repeat your operation on it reasonably until it is healed.
The eminence of the lachrymal flesh, if it is noticed in the eye as a great impediment, should be suspended with hooks; and incise it on one side, and do not extend the incision lest you cut the tear ducts.
And then instill into the lachrymal duct rue juice and henbane juice until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the incision of a staphyloma and of that which is born of fleshy matter in the eye.
Sometimes in the eyes of some, red flesh is born and grows until it covers the pupil, or approaches and presses on the eyelids, and strongly compresses the eyelids on the outside parts, and is similar to the rose of a balustrade.
But the operation is that the patient places his head on your lap; and then open the eyes of that person and collect all that flesh with scissors convenient for that purpose, and hold it with forceps or with tweezers; and then incise the flesh at the beginning until it is all finished with the lancet, and be careful of the eye lest you injure it during your operation.
And let your incision with which you incise those things be with a spatula, which we have recommended in the incision of the nail, or with small scissors with which hair is collected, according to how the form of it will come about in this chapter.
Then when your incision is complete and all that flesh is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt, or instill into it a red powder similar to that of corrosive medicines, and place a cotton compress over the outside of the eye with egg white until it is assured of a hot abscess.
And if some cause of inflammation remains and hot blood is seen, and you fear a hot abscess, then leave the eye and treat that abscess with what heals that abscess, and then return to it with an operation until it is healed, and similarly do in the adjacent flesh which affects the eye of this kind.
But however, you must avoid all treatment of the injured eye by natural or accidental means, working with iron, and do not put your hand to any of these operations until you see that the member is enduring that operation.
Chapter 18.
Concerning the collection of pus in the eye.
Pus is caused by thick veins which are hardened over the eye and prevent the sight of its operation, and they weaken the eye over a long period of days.
Therefore, it is necessary at the beginning that you consider if the eye in which there is pus is strong and if there is no other illness in it besides pus; then you collect that pus.
And it is that you command the patient to place his head on your lap, and then suspend those veins with one or two ounces, according to your judgment; and let that ounce be of a subtle duplication according to this form:
or two ounces gradually in one body according to this form:
And then let those veins be collected with subtle forces, with subtlety, and wipe away the blood little by little, until you see those veins be removed from the eye and be resolved.
And be careful that you do not injure the eye with the ends of the spatula; and if your operation is in the midday before the sun, and you are very firm in your operation that you do not incise except those veins.
And when you have finished, then you distill into the eye verdigris or rue so that with its sharpness it corrodes what is removed from the eyelid.
But if this is not possible for you, collect everything at that time, and then you plaster everything with that which soothes his pain, and leave it for a few days until his pain is soothed and he is assured of a hot abscess.
And then repeat on him according to the same manner until he is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps:
Chapter 19.
On the reduction of nasal dislocations.
When you treat a nasal dislocation with cautery or with a sharp, burning medicine, as described previously, and it is not healed, then there is no benefit in it, but rather incise the abscess before its maturation, and extract all the moisture that is in it and the pus, until the bone is exposed.
And thus, when the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then you scrape it with an instrument of which this is the form:
And it is named a rasp, and it should be made of Indian iron, and its head should be round, like a chisel, which should be pointed by filing or sharpened by subtle incision (as is shown in the following form).
And place that [rasp] on the corrupted part of the bone, and then rotate it between your fingers, and press your hand little by little until you know that the corruption is scraped away.
And this should be done several times, and then restore the area with drying and styptic substances.
Then, if the area is consolidated and good flesh has grown there, and the body of the pus is separated and remains for forty days, and is not renewed, and does not fester, and no accident befalls it, then you will know that it is healed.
And if not, then there is nothing else to do but to reduce the fissure to the shape of the nose, and in this manner: that is, you uncover the underlying bone with an iron instrument or a sharp medicine, and when the bone is uncovered, then you take a trephine in this form:
Let the tip of the iron trephine be triangular, and let its shaft be thin and tapered to the tip, like this [presumably a diagram was present in the original text].
And then place that [trephine] on the bone, and let it be near the lacrimal bone; and extend your hand a little from the eye, and then rotate your hand with the trephine until it penetrates the whole bone, and the patient will feel this because they will feel air escaping when you hold your hand.
Then restore the area with styptic and drying medicines, as has been taught to you.
Then, when the flesh grows and the area hardens, the matter that was flowing to the external parts returns to the nose, therefore this is easier on the patient.
Chapter 20.
Concerning the reduction of the prominence of the eye.
When the eye protrudes completely and there is no accompanying injury, impairment or decrease in vision, then you should leave the patient alone, and carefully observe him, and apply cupping glasses to the neck without scarification and suck by good suction; and then place on the eye an ointment which is made of honey and aloe and incense and sarcocolla, and then firmly apply the plaster to the eye with many powders.
And the manner of the powders is that you take many linen cloths, and each cloth should be folded into four layers; and make of these many powders [or, make many layers/plies].
And then place those one on top of the other according to the amount that the eye needs. And then bind those tightly with a strong cloth with firm pressure; and repeat the cupping with suction without scarification; and then remove the cupping glasses and leave the eye bound day and night.
But if meanwhile the ligament is softened, then it should be bound very tightly.
And then remove the bandage day and night, as we have said.
And if you see the eye has already been reduced, it is good; and if not, then repeat the plaster with the powders and the binding and the cupping until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Concerning the incision of the eminence of the uvea.  (Note:  "uvea" here refers to the uvea of the eye.)
When there is a rupture in the uvea, and it is prominent and protrudes beyond the eyelids like a grape, and its shape is altered, and you wish to incise it, then the operation is as we have recounted: you insert the needle into the root of the uvea from below to above, and then place another needle with double thread on the side of the tear duct, and make it penetrate and leave the first needle according to its position; and then incise the area with the double thread and lift with it the part of the uvea around the needle, and tighten it strongly.
And then remove the needle and place on the eye wool soaked in egg white and allow the thread to remain with the uvea.
And then treat the eye with something that will comfort it until the wound is healed.
And sometimes gather from all sides with a circle of cloth around the eye a large prominent area, and then it should be pierced with a thin spatula that reaches to the pupil of the eye, and drains the albuminous moisture, and the eye descends and enters immediately.
And then tighten it until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
Concerning the treatment of alconati (likely a type of injury or ailment,  requiring further context for a precise translation).
This illness, which is called alconati, is nothing but a swelling that is situated in the eye, similar to descending water, but it is not that.
But the method of working on it is that you make the patient sit on a prepared and straight chair, and then take his head in your hands on both sides and gently manipulate it until you see the swelling descend to the lower parts, and then it should be dried so that it does not descend, and the light should be obstructed, and the patient should see things as he used to see them.
And if it does not descend downwards, know that it is water (referring to the fluid-like nature of the swelling).
But if the descent of the swelling is not prepared with what we have said, then make the patient sit between your hands and then take a thin spatula and use it on the pannicle (eyelids?), carefully around the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea at the limbus (in the empty space), until the swelling is expelled.
Then, when it is expelled, you instill warm water into the eye in which you have mixed honey, or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey.
And then let it be treated with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 22.
On the treatment of the watery discharge from the eye.
We have already discussed the types of watery eye discharge in the division and which ones are appropriate to puncture, as we have clearly stated. Then, this treatment should be done according to the patient's health.
Then it is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands on a square cushion and bind his healthy eye tightly.
And then place your left hand near the eyelid of his eye, if the eye with the watery discharge is the left eye; or with your right hand, if the eye is the right one.
And then with your right hand take an almond, if the eye is left; or with your left hand, if the eye is right.
And then place the tip of the almond near the cornea, along the width of the iris in the white of the eye, on the side of the lacrimal duct, and then gently press the almond and roll your hand with it.
And then penetrate into the white of the eye and feel that the almond has reached the empty space (presumably a cyst or similar).
And it should be that the distance of the almond to the preon (likely referring to a point near the pupil) is the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the end of the blackness (iris), and that is the cornea of the eye; for you will see with that pupil, with the vision of the eye through the clarity of the corneal tunic.
And then place the almond into the place where the water (presumably fluid buildup) is, and then you draw it down one time, then another, and then if the water descends immediately, then show the patient this on which his sight immediately improves, and the almond in his eye.
And then rest a little; then if the water has descended, then you remove it another time before the almond is thrown away.
And then when it is dried and does not descend, then you extract the almond easily, and gently rotate it with your hand, and then dissolve a little clear rock salt in the water and with that wash the inside of the eye; and then place on the outside of the eye cotton or tow or wool infused in rose oil and egg white, and with this the eye will heal.
But we and those of our time place on that (the eye) a compress of egg white.
And if the almond does not obey you upon entering the eye because of its hardness — for there are some men whose eyes are very hard — then it is necessary that you take the instrument which is named alberid, of which this is the form:
Then perforate that conjunctiva with that (instrument), and do not force your hand into the hole, but only make a subtle passage for the almond.
And then introduce the almond as we have said.
And for the completion of your work, let a sleeping place be prepared for the patient, decently made, in which he sleeps on his back in a dark room, and let all movement be prohibited.
And place him in such a position that his body is aligned, and he does not turn his head to the right or left at all.
And let the bandage remain in place according to its disposition until the third day.
And then unbind it in that dark room and expose the injured area to the light, and show him some things, and then reapply the bandage until the seventh day.
And it is not fitting that you do this at the hour of the treatments or immediately after the infusion. Afterwards, it is necessary that you avoid this, because the water might easily escape due to great and vehement movement.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then it is necessary that you unbind the wound before the seventh day, and treat it with what will reduce the abscess.
Then you will cover his injured area and place on his face a poultice by which his vision will be controlled, keeping him for some days in the darkest room.
And then let that matter be thrown away carefully and the cause investigated.
And know that in a cataract, the pupil is not excused from some visions, and then you should be bold in the operation.
And it has come to us from some of those who are in Artaix, and some say that fire is made in Ayrac, a perforated vessel from which the water is sucked.
However, I have not seen anyone in our land who has perfected this, nor have I read of it in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that this is novel.
And these are the forms of the species of vessels so that he who is ignorant of them may know them:
And let them be made of bronze or iron properly, and let their extremities be of this triangulated subtlety, the pointed extremity thus as that is the form.
Chapter 24.
Of the treatment of rotten flesh in the nose.
When in the nose various fleshy growths are born, of which some cause is similar to a very small scorpion, or a polyp fish; and this illness is called polypus because it resembles that fish in appearance. And from it is cancerous flesh, flabby, of a dark color; and from it is what is lion flesh, not of dark color.
Therefore, in that which is in these fleshy growths, neither cancerous nor fraudulent, you must make the patient sit between your hands and place [him] in the sun, and open his nostrils, and prick your ointment into those growths. Then extract that to the outside parts, and then incise what you have grasped of those with a sharp, thin spatula from one side until you know that all the flesh has been removed; that if any cause remains from which excision is possible, then you scrape that with one of the fine instruments easily until nothing remains of it. But if it bleeds profusely or a hot abscess forms, then you apply to it what is necessary; or if it is one of the malignant abscesses, then you cauterize and cut that until the bleeding is stopped and all the cavities are removed.
Then let there be poured into the nose near the incision, strong wine and water or wine. But if the nose is open and humidity proceeds from it into the palate, then know that it is already healed.
But if the humidity does not penetrate as it should, then you know that inside there is rotten flesh in the upper bones of the nose to which excision has not reached.
Therefore, it is necessary that you introduce a thin thread into it, to which some weight is attached, and tie knots in the thread, and place between each knot the quantity of a finger or less, and then carefully insert one end of the thread into the nose with a probe or with any other instrument that is possible for you to use.
And after you have done this, let it draw its odor until it reaches the canals of the nose, and is expelled over their throat, for many times children do something similar in schools, and it is an easy action for anyone who wants to do it.
And then tie the two ends of the thread, one end emerging from the nose and the other remaining inside the nose, and then apply sawdust mixed with the knots that are on the thread, and do this until you know that the flesh is now with the knots of the thread.
And then remove the thread, and apply to the nose, near the extraction of the blood, a warm poultice, and do this for three days or more until the ointment corrodes all that is rotten flesh.
And finally, at the end of the treatment, place a lead cannula in the nose for a few days until it is healed.
But if you need a more drying treatment, use that one.
And this is the form of the instrument, a head-purger, with which oils and medicines are distilled into the nostrils:
And make this of silver or bronze, which should resemble a small open lamp, like a small bowl, and the spout similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula like a tube, and the cannula of the head-purging instrument should be flat and open; to which there should be a handle at the back, with which you hold it when you heat the oil or water, or whatever you want, of the juices and extractable substances.
Chapter 25.
Of the boils that appear at the extremities of the nose.
Many times a boil appears at the extremities of the nose, and it is magnified and increased in a few days, to the point that its appearance is very noticeable.
And for that reason it is necessary that you incise it at the beginning of its appearance, and eradicate all of it. And then place on the area either a cautery or a burning medicine which will stay in the place of it.
But if its incision is delayed until it is magnified, then consider if it is hardened, firm, dark in color and with little sensation; then do not touch it with iron, for it is a cancerous abscess; because many times he who incises this abscess suffers great harm from it, whoever it was that had it.
And if the abscess is of a light color and not dark, and you see that incision is possible in all of it, then evacuate the patient and incise it without fear, and treat the area with what is appropriate and constricting until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Chapter 26. Of the suturing of the nose and of the lips and of the ear when their continuity is broken by a wound or the like.
Know that when a solution of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, you should not attempt that operation except in certain cases.
Therefore, when such a thing happens to someone, consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood, and then you should join the two edges of the wound with sutures, and then treat it until it is healed.
And if the edges of the solution of continuity are already separated, and each is healed, then you must scrape away some part of their apparent skin until blood appears. Then join the two edges with sutures and tighten them, and over that sprinkle dragon's blood and crushed embers, and place over the powder a piece of diachylon plaster, or some other of the consolidating plasters, and leave it bound for two or three days, then unbind it and change the plaster and leave it until the stitches are themselves cut away, and then treat with ointment until it is healed.
But the manner of suturing is that you close the solution of continuity either with needles, as we have described in the suturing of the abdomen, or you close the solution of continuity with thread as has been shown to you.
Chapter 27.
Of the extraction of those things which enter the two lips in the inner parts.
Often there appear in the inner parts of the lips of many men certain hard abscesses resembling birdlime, and some of them are smaller and some are larger.
Therefore, it is necessary that you open the lips and slits around each of those hardnesses on all sides, and then, after the excision, fill the place with crushed sage until the bleeding is stopped, and then it should be washed with sharp wine and the place treated with something styptic until it is healed.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the incision of the flesh grown in the gums.
Very often a fleshy growth appears on the gums which the ancients called an albulus; then it is necessary that you lift that with forceps or hold it with forceps and incise it down to its root, and let the pus or blood flow.
And then place crushed sage or other styptic or drying powders on the area.
And if that flesh returns, then incise it a second time, and cauterize it, for after cauterization it will not return.
Chapter 29.
Concerning the treatment of teeth with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth, both inside and out, and between the gums, rough, decayed, and sometimes blackened crusts are attached, and they become yellowish or greenish, so that the gum is affected by this corruption and the teeth become decayed.
And therefore it is necessary that you make the patient sit between your hands and rest their head on your lap, and scrape the teeth on which those crusts and similar substances appear, resembling sand, until nothing remains of them. And do this to remove the blackness, the yellowness, and the others, until they are cleansed.
Then, if this which is at the beginning of the treatment has been removed, it is good; and if not, then you repeat the treatment on those teeth on the following day and the second and the third until you achieve the desired result.
And know that the teeth require many tools of diverse forms and shapes, according to what is prepared for your operation. And because the tool with which the tooth is scraped from the inside is different from the tool with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to other forms, and these forms of tools should be prepared by you, which you will see immediately:
Chapter 30.
On the extraction of teeth.
So that you treat the tooth's pain with all skill and delay its extraction, for there is no cause that replaces its sight when it is extracted, for it is a sublime substance, for which its extraction is an exception.
But when the patient is forced to have that tooth extracted, you must be firmly certain which tooth is diseased, for many times the pain deceives the patient and he thinks it is in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one, and then the pain does not cease until the diseased and aching tooth is extracted, for we have already seen this through the operations of barbers.
Therefore, when you are certain which tooth is diseased, and you know it well, then you must scarify around the tooth with a spatula in which there is some virtue, until it is loosened from the gum on all sides, and then loosen it with your fingers, or with fine forceps, at first little by little until it is completely loose.
And then apply strong, large forceps for a good grip or firm hold, and place the patient's head between your knees so that you hold him well so that he does not move, and then pull the tooth straight in such a way that you do not break it, nor cause the patient greater pain.
And then, if it is not removed, take one of those instruments, and place it under it on all sides with ease and force its removal in the same way as was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is cracked or eroded, then you must fill that crack with cloth and bind it tightly with a thin strip of linen so that it is not broken at the time of your traction on it with forceps.
And you must carefully open with scarification around all parts of the gum and take great care not to break it and leave a piece behind, and return to the patient due to the harm that might be greater than the first pain.
And beware that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their haste and audacity in extraction, without administering what we have recommended; for many times they cause great illnesses in men which are more difficult to cure, and their roots remain, all or some of them, or that you extract them with some bones of the jaw, as we have testified many times.
And then, after its extraction, the mouth should be washed with hot wine or sour wine with salt.
And if bleeding occurs from this, as often happens, then crush some quantity of sage and sprinkle it on the wound, and if the sage does not work, cauterize it.
The shape of the forceps and the fine tongs with which the tooth is grasped at the beginning should have a long extremity, a short and thick handle, so that they do not bend when you work with them.
And let them be like tongs with a thick handle, so that when you squeeze with them they are not bent, [and] let the extremities be short.
And let them be made of Indian iron, precisely made with straight extremities, and at the extremities of these let there be teeth, of which some interlock with others and tighten by strong constriction, and sometimes the extremities of these are in the shape of the beak of a bird called a *ciconia* (that is, a stork), and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter 31.
Of the extraction of teeth and the removal of bones from fractured jaws.
When you must extract a tooth that is fractured and some part remains, then you must place a cotton compress soaked with a poultice on the area for one or two days until the area is softened; and then insert pliers or forceps, the extremities of which are shaped like the beak of a bird called a *ciconia* or stork, the shape of forceps:
Let their extremities be made inside like a file or rasp.
And if it does not yield to you in this way with these forceps or pliers, then you must dig around the root and remove all the flesh and insert the instrument which is called an *atali*, that is, a small lever, the form of which is this:
And let it be of short extremity, somewhat thick, and not be bent so that it is not broken.
Therefore, if by this the root is extracted, it is good; and if not, help yourself with these other instruments, of which these are the forms: the form of the first instrument is of a triangular extremity, which should have some thickness:
The form of the second instrument is that it be triangular and of a more subtle extremity.
And now be aided by this instrument having two prongs, of which this is the following form: and with other instruments which are used in tooth extraction; for the work is easily done with this instrument which resembles a large hook, and this is its form:
of a triangular, curved or short extremity, which should have some thickness, and not be bent so that it is not broken.
And also know that the instruments for molar teeth are similar to the other instruments.
But the wise and skillful artisan, finds for himself, according to what the operation and the various diseases indicate to him; for there are diseases for which the ancients neither named nor devised a proper instrument, because of the forms of diverse species.
But if the bone of the jaw or of the other bones of the mouth is broken, or if it can be, then it should be treated in its place with what is suitable for it from among the instruments and forceps which we have mentioned in the extraction of tooth roots, and help yourself with a splint, of which this is the form:
And let there be some thickness little by little, and let it be tightened or held with the mouth so that it does not escape or flee until the bone is completely expelled; and then restore the place with medicines suitable for this.
But if there is a small rottenness in the bone, then scrape away that rottenness and its blackness, and cure it until it is healed.
Chapter 32.
On the treatment of teeth that protrude above the others.
When teeth protrude, not following their natural course, and this is especially the case for women, and particularly for venerable old women; then you must consider: if a tooth is next to another tooth, and filing or smoothing is not possible, then extract it; but if it is not attached to another tooth, then you incise it with this instrument, of which this is the form; and it resembles a boar's tusk, and it should be made of Indian iron with a very sharp tip.
And let your incision, with which you incise it, be done in several days because of the hardness of the tooth, and so that no other teeth are damaged.
And if the protruding tooth can be filed, then file it with an Indian iron file of which this is the form:
And let the whole thing be of Indian iron and very fine; and let it have very small points, like a file with which needles are made, with which the tooth is filed little by little over many days easily, so that no other tooth is damaged and broken. Then smooth it at the end, and scrape it with some rasps.
And if it is a tooth of which a part is broken and hurts the tongue, then it is necessary that you file it until the roughness of that fracture is removed and smoothed, and is filled so that the tongue is not injured and speech is not impaired.
Chapter 33.
Of the restoration of damaged teeth with gold or silver wire.
When the front teeth suffer concussion and loosening due to a blow or a fall, and the patient cannot bite on what he eats, and you have treated it with styptic medicines, and your treatment has not been effective, then so that they do not fall out, contrive that they be bound with gold wire and not silver, because silver turns green and almost powders after a few days, but gold is never changed.
And let the wire be of such fineness and thickness according to the amount that can fit between the teeth; and the method of binding is that you take the wire and pass double the length between two healthy teeth, and then let it curve with two turns of the wire between the damaged ones, whether one or several, until the wire reaches a healthy tooth on the other side. And then repeat the weaving back to the point where you began, and tighten it gently and skillfully until it is firm.
And let your tightening, with which you secure the wire, be such that it does not slip from the roots of the teeth.
And then cut off the superfluous ends of the wire with forceps or scissors, and carefully place those ends in each location, and cover them and hide them between the healthy tooth and the damaged tooth, so that they do not injure the tongue. And then leave it thus tightened, remaining as it is.
But if it is loosened or broken, tighten it or them with another wire in the same manner throughout its/their time.
And this is the form of the teeth and the figure of the arrangement of two healthy teeth and two damaged teeth, as you sometimes see these bound:
And when it is done that one or two teeth return to the alignment of each in their places, and are regrown as we have recounted, and that they are remade/repaired.
And only a good and skilled craftsman does this.
And sometimes some material from cow bones is used, and it is shaped like a tooth, and it is placed in the place where the tooth is missing, and it is fastened as we have said, and it remains and serves with that for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Concerning the incision of the ligament which occurs in the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament, which is found under the tongue, is sometimes natural, with which one is born, or accidental and occurs due to a wound which has already healed.
And the operation on him is that you open the patient's mouth, his head being in your lap; and raise his tongue, and then incise that nervous ligament according to its width until the tongue is freed from its restriction.
But if in that [area] there is any hardness or knot, and this is due to the healing of a wound, then you insert an instrument and incise it across its width until you have incised the ligament and the knot is dissolved.
And be careful that no fissure is made in the depth of the flesh and that you do not cut too deeply and cause a flow of blood.
Then wash the site of the incision with warm water or with vinegar or with cold water; and then place under the tongue a linen cloth; that is, a small cloth which the patient keeps under the tongue every night so that it does not heal again.
But if bleeding occurs, place crushed vitriol on the area; but if the bleeding persists, then cauterize the area with a lancet-shaped cautery which is suitable for this. And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
On the extraction of a ranula from under the tongue.
Sometimes, an abscess resembling a small tumor occurs under the tongue, hindering the tongue from its natural function; and it is quickly enlarged until it fills the mouth.
And the operation is that you open the patient's mouth, sitting in front of the sun, and examine the abscess.
And if you see it is dark or black, hard, and the patient feels no pain in it, then do not touch it, for it is cancerous.
