And if it is tending towards white, and moist, then you put an oncia (ounce, or possibly a specific surgical instrument) there, and incise it with a fine scalpel, and cut it completely; and if blood flows during your operation, then you place crushed vitriol on it until the bleeding stops.
Then return to your work until you have completely removed it, until it is washed with sour wine and salt; and treat it with the other appropriate treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 36.
Chapter 36. On the extraction of an abscess from the two ears and that which is located in the throat and other abscesses.
When a goiter develops in the throat, these are glands that are collected together, which protrude outwards and are called two ears; when you treat them with what we have said in the section and it is not healed, then you consider if the abscess is hard, dark in color, and insensitive; then do not touch it with iron (i.e., a surgical instrument).
And if it is red and the root is thick, then do not touch it with iron because of the flow of blood; rather, let it mature, and then you will either extract it, or it will break open by itself.
And if it is white, soft, and its root is thin, then it is one that should be incised.
And the operation on it is that you consider, before your operation, whether the abscess has already matured completely through rest or if it has diminished in any way; and then make the patient sit in front of the sun, and his head should be in your lap, and open his mouth, and the servant, holding it between his hands, presses down the patient's tongue with a very fine instrument of which this is the form:
And let it be made of silver or bronze, and let it be as thin as a knife.
Then, as you press the tongue with this and the abscess is shown to you, and you look carefully at it, then take tweezers and put them in the tonsil, and pull it outwards as much as you can. Furthermore, if you pull out something from the membranes, then immediately incise it with an instrument of which this is the form:
Furthermore, if you pull out something from the membranes, then immediately incise it with an instrument of which this is the form: and it resembles forceps, but its ends are curved, so that the opening of each is opposite the other, very sharp, which should be made of tempered iron.
But if this instrument is not at hand, then incise it with a spatula of which this is the form: And let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other side.
And after you have incised one side, incise the other in the same manner, and immediately after the incision, let the patient gargle with cold water.
But if there is a flow of blood, gargle with a decoction of pomegranate rind or myrtle leaves and similar styptic substances until the flow of blood is restrained, and then treat it until it is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses appear in the throat besides those two [near] the ears, and incise those as I have said in the incision of the two sides equally.
And I myself once treated a woman with an abscess which had formed in her throat, which abscess was tending towards a dark color, with little feeling, which had already closed her throat, and the woman was breathing through a narrow passage, and I had already forbidden her food and drink, and she was near to death if she had remained for a day or two more.
And the abscess had swollen so much that it had formed two openings through which it could escape.
Therefore I treated it with a fistula [incision] and inserted an instrument into one of the openings, and drew it out, and a large portion was removed; then I incised the other opening, and opened its mouth, and compressed its tongue, and then I inserted the instrument into that abscess.
And then I cut away a large part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Therefore the throat of that woman was freed, and she immediately began to drink water, and then she ate food; therefore I did not cease to incise that abscess many times for a long time, and another abscess succeeded in place of that one, which I incised until it was completely eliminated.
Then I took hold of it and cauterized her throat; then I stopped. And afterwards I walked around and did not know what God would do about it then.
Chapter 37.
On the incision of a wen when it is abscessed.
When catarrh descends to the uvula, and it is abscessed and is long, then it is called a perpendicular; and if it is thick and round in the lower part, then it is called a bubo.
But when it is treated with what we have said in the division, and your treatment does not succeed, and you see that the cauterized abscess has already been removed and is thin, then you must incise that which is round and gathered; and if it is of that length, and is bloody, of a dark color or black, and there is no feeling to it, then you must attempt its incision, for in that case there is danger for the patient.
And when you see it according to the manner I have described, its whiteness and length, you should make the patient sit and press their tongue with an instrument which follows its form and then place the hook into the uvula, and draw it down and incise it with one of the two instruments which are described in the incision of the tonsils.
And you must not incise it except what is appropriate to the natural limit without addition, for if you incise more, you will injure their speech.
And after the incision, use what we have recommended in the incision of the tonsils, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the patient is frightened by its incision, you should use ingenuity in its cauterization without fear, and the manner of cauterizing it is only with sharp water, and that you place the patient's head on your lap, and then press their tongue with the instrument which we have mentioned, and then take of the water which we have mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, and apply it with that living coal, and place it neither thick nor thin, and with this fill the concavity of the instrument of which this is the shape:
Let its extremity be a concavity in which the medicine is placed, like the concavity of a spoon, and place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula, and let the patient lie on their side so that the saliva or mucus runs from their mouth because of the caustic medicine, so that nothing descends from it onto their throat and harms them.
And then take your hand with the medicine, and press it on the uvula for the space of half an hour until you see it become blackened and dried by the biting of the medicine.
And if you wish, take cotton and wrap it around the end of the spoon, and submerge the cotton in the medicine, and insert the spoon with the cotton into a sheath so that the cotton adheres to that sheath, and do this as many times as necessary until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of the uvula, and then leave it so that it will dry and fall off after three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it, and after the cauterization, wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in wine, and clean with that what is around it from the medicine, and wash it with cold water, and treat it externally with an embrocation, and internally with gargles until it is healed.
And this kind of treatment is safer than incision and less frightening.
Sometimes the uvula is cured with a method more subtle than cautery or incision, for it is cured by fumigation in this manner: take calamint, hyssop, origanum, rue, Arabic gum, chamomile, and similar herbs, and mix all those ingredients, or some of them, in a pot, and submerge them in sour wine, and boil; and the pot should be well sealed with a suitable lid, and in the middle of the pot there should be a hole, over which a concave brass instrument is placed in this manner; it may be made of silver or brass.
And insert the end into the patient's mouth so that the vapor can reach the uvula through the cannula, until the uvula is sufficiently fumigated.
And then repeat this many times until it is dry, and be careful not to perform this treatment at the beginning of the development of an abscess, for it would increase the abscess too much, and you should not do this, but rather cauterize the abscess when it is apparent.
But if you do not have this instrument at hand, then take a cane and attach an eggshell to the end of it, so that the patient's mouth is covered, because the eggshell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter 38.
Concerning the extraction of thorns and that which adheres to the throat and other causes.
Many times something adheres to the throat, whether a fish bone or some other cause.
Then it is necessary that you extract that which is apparent, upon which your sight falls, after you press the tongue with an instrument in front of the light, so that that which is in the throat may be revealed to you.
And that which does not appear and is hidden from you in the throat, you must make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for very quickly the thing attached to the throat will be expelled by vomiting; or let the patient swallow a piece of turnip or lettuce root or a piece of dry bread; or take a piece of dried sea sponge, tie it firmly with thread, and then let the patient swallow it, for when it arrives at the place where the thorns are, it will extract them and they will be expelled. And moreover, take an eel, well tied with thread, and let it be inserted and quickly withdrawn; or take a leek and quickly insert it and quickly remove it.
But if they are not expelled by those means which we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument according to this form:
And let it be thicker than a small root, and at its extremity let there be a concavity which you introduce into his throat with ease.
And let him have his head raised up, and be careful not to touch his throat bone, so that it may happen to him that he hurts himself, and with that he may dislocate the bone or spine, or that the physician do this with his hand.
But however, it is better that the patient himself introduce it, because of his knowledge of the location of the attached cause and pushes downwards, and pulls his hand with the instrument upwards, all this according to the quantity that is prepared for him until it is expelled.
Chapter 39.
On the extraction of a leech attached to the throat.
When a leech is medicated with what we have said in the division of treatment with medicines and it does not work, then consider putting it in the patient's throat in the sun, after you have compressed his tongue with the instrument which we have recommended to you.
Therefore, if you see the leech, extract it with a small hook or with gentle, appropriate movements.
And if it is not possible for you to extract it with that, then take a concave cannula and insert it into the patient's throat, and then place a heated iron in the concavity of the cannula, and do this repeatedly (until the leech is dead and detaches from the throat), and abstain from giving the patient any water that day.
And then take a vial full of cold water, and open his mouth into it, and wash the mouth with it; and do not swallow any of it and keep stirring the water a little at a time with your hand, because the leech, as soon as it feels the water, falls off.
But if it is not expelled by what we have recommended, then fumigate the patient with cyme (possibly meaning a type of herb or incense) or with asafoetida, using the instrument which we have recommended for the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, so that it will fall out.
And another method of operation in the fumigation of this is that you take a pot in which there are burning coals and the pot is covered with a lid in the middle of which is a hole, and the end of the instrument is placed in that hole; and then let the fumigation come from above; and let the patient put his mouth to the end of the cannula, and close his mouth so that the fumigation is not expelled until you know that the fumigation has reached the leech, for the leech will immediately fall off.
And if it does not fall off, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and abstain the patient from drinking, and eating salty things and garlic, and do not drink water, and thus it is necessary that by this regime it will be expelled.
And this is the form of the instrument with which a leech is extracted from the throat when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps like this, except that there is some curvature in it: which is placed in the throat, and its end is similar to a stork's beak, which is hollow, in which there is a roughness of a file, so that, when it grasps something, it does not let go.
Chapter 40.
In which is the summary of the discourse on the appearance of abscesses and on their incision.
Abscesses are of many species, of many types, according to what remembrance of them is seen, of each one in this book.
And they are diverse in their perforation and incision in two ways, of which one is by the species of abscesses themselves; and by that which we understand of the humors.
And the second way is according to the locations in which they occur in the body, for an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the buttocks; and an abscess in a fleshy place is different from an abscess occurring in a joint; and each of them has its own judgment for operation.
And moreover, there are some abscesses which should not be perforated except near the maturation of the pus in them or their completion.
There are other abscesses which should be opened when they are raw and not matured according to their completion, such as abscesses that are near the joints, because when an abscess is near a joint, its being is prolonged until that which is in its circumference can be purged, and very quickly the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted, wherefore a cause of contraction of that limb is made; or it is an abscess near a principal member, and if you delay its perforation until it is matured, it will harm the principal member; or it is near the buttock, therefore perforate it when unmatured; and it is not fitting that you perforate it raw, of uncompleted digestion, except that which is near the buttock, so that its matter may not spread and be elongated to the inner parts of the buttock, and a fistula be made, or that it be made in such a way that it is not healed. Moreover, you must know the time for the perforation of the abscess, and this is when it is matured.
And know that it is matured when there is a lessening of the pain and a cessation of the fever, and also a decrease in the redness and pulsation, and the acuteness of the head of the abscess, and the other signs.
And because it is necessary that you know the perforation should fall in the lowest place of the abscess and from the coming cure of the eminence.
And the perforation should always be along the length of the flesh or body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands or feet, and in the places of the tendons, of the cords of the nerves, of the arteries; and, finally, in all even places in which there is no reflection; for in those that are reflected, use perforation according to that member.
But when the abscess is in a fleshy place, then it is better that you delay its perforation until its digestion is confirmed according to what has been said; for if you perforate it before that, the pus is prolonged and much filth remains, and its lips are hardened and its depth increased.
But some abscesses are perforated according to the width of the flesh or body due to necessity, or according to what the member requires.
And it is necessary that you administer a small perforation in small abscesses, and in large abscesses a large and wide perforation or many incisions, according to the diversity of the size of the abscesses.
And there are other abscesses in which it is necessary that the skin be curved and incised, when it is made like bread and is ripe, as happens in cases of boils and buboes.
And some of these are incised with a three-angled incision; and others are incised in the shape of a myrtle leaf, like the abscesses of the armpits.
And there are those in which a round incision and a crescent-shaped incision are administered, and similar to those.
But that which is not like the aforementioned superficial abscesses, you should perforate it with a superficial, or only flat, perforation.
And when the abscess is large and has collected much pus, you should perforate it and not worry about removing all the pus at once; having removed a part of it, then bandage the abscess until the next day, and then remove a larger portion, and do this many times gradually until it is all removed.
And especially if the patient is of weak constitution, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an elderly person in decrepitude, because the vital spirit is often dissolved with the egress of pus, and the patient dies very quickly, and you open them.
Therefore, observe this chapter with great caution, and concerning your perforation in which you perforate these abscesses, you should cleanse the wounds and consider whether the wound of the abscess is small, or if it is a flat incision; then you should apply lint or old linen to it; and if the abscess is large, and the fissures of the perforation are many, then you should place a tent in each opening until they join together.
And if in the abscess a part of the skin is cut away or you have hollowed it out around the edges, then you should fill it with old linen or lint made from linen cloth, just as when you have drawn out splinters or roots, without moisture; and bandage it tightly until the third day; and then continue with this and treat it with the ointments that are necessary until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs during your operation, then apply cold water and sour wine in which you have soaked a linen cloth, and apply this to the bleeding area many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then you should administer the regimen and treatment and powders which I have described in many places in this book and in its divisions.
But if it is winter, and the location of the abscess is a place of many nerves, then you should mix powders in warm wine and oil, and apply them to the area.
And if it is summer, and the areas are fleshy, then you should apply powders mixed with water and oil or with wine and oil, and all this should be cold until the third day, as we have said, and then you should open the abscess and cleanse it and administer to its treatment what is appropriate for it until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses universally, but according to the way of distinction, I have already spoken of every abscess and in what manner is the way of its treatment in particular chapters.
Chapter 41.
Concerning the incision of abscesses which occur in the back of the head.
In the back of the head, small abscesses are found, a type of node containing tunics which are attached to their surfaces; and their types are many.
And among them are some that contain a humidity similar to slime. And among them are those that contain a humidity resembling dust, similar to them; and among them are those that are stony, hard.
And in the incision of all, and in the extraction of those, there is no fear, since the extraction of those does not cause contrary effects.
And the operation in the incision of these is that you probe them with an instrument whose shape is appropriate for this, which is called an *entromes* (a type of probe), until you know what they contain.
For if what it contains is humidity, then you incise it with a cut along its length, a superficial incision according to this shape:
And begin the incision from line B to line G.  Then, when the humidity is evacuated, you scrape that skin which contained the humidity, and do not leave any of it at all, because much that is evacuated returns if some of it remains.
And then immerse cotton in Egyptian ointment, if it is present, and if not, in water and salt; and fill the wound with it and leave it there until the next day, for with this you corrode all that remains of that pellicle (thin skin).
And then repeat on that cotton with Egyptian ointment a second and third time if it is needed, until you are confident that there is no remainder of that pellicle; then cure the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty nodules, then you incise it according to this form: with an incision having the form of a cross, and insert forceps between the two lips of the incision and scrape it from all parts, and exert your strength in the extraction of the tunic which contains it.
But if the arrow causes resistance, then do what I told you first.
And similarly, treat a lapified abscess with incision and cure as we have told you; and the incision on a lapified abscess is easier, because it has little blood and little humidity.
And sometimes some of these abscesses are in the head of some men, in which there is no humidity at all; the reason is that I incised an abscess on the head of an old woman and found the abscess like a hard, rough, white stone, which could not be broken, and if anyone were struck with it, it would hurt them.
But in other accidental abscesses in the head other than this, this is not the chapter, such as the abscesses that appear on the heads of children and behind the roots of the ears, but the incision of all of these should be a superficial incision.
And make the perforation of those from the lowest part of them so that the body is relieved of the pus in the lowest parts.
And then treat those with what is appropriate for their cure.
Chapter 42.
Chapter 42. Of the incision of scrofulas which happen in the neck.
Often these abscesses occur in the neck and in the armpits and in the groin; and sometimes they happen in other parts of the body.
And those which happen in the neck, and many which are generated among them, and every scrofula of these is within its own proper tunic, and just as in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have recounted.
And the species of these scrofulas are many, for some of them are stony, and some of them contain humidity, and some of them are malignant which do not obey cure.
Therefore, in that which you see of good disposition to the touch, of which the color that is manifest is near to the color of the flesh, and is softened in every part, and is not attached to the nerves of the neck, nor to the veins, nor to the artery, and is not deep, it behooves you to incise it with a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body of the line G. within the line B., and scrape it from every part, and extend the lips of the flesh with hooks, or with many hooks if you have need of them, as we have said in the abscesses of the head, and extract it little by little.
And it is most cautious that the vein or nerve not be incised.
And let the instrument not be very sharp, so that it not hurt you with the incision, or that it trouble the patient, and that you incise only what is necessary to incise; for if you should incise a vein or artery and be hindered in your operation, then place on the wound crushed vitriol or other powders which stop the flow of blood, and bind the wound, and leave it until the acuteness of the abscess subsides and the wound is softened and begins to be putrefied. When the blood is cut off.
Then return to your operation until you have completed it.
And then investigate with your index finger if there is any cause of the other small scrofula; then incise those and cleanse them.
And if there is a large vein at the root of the scrofula, then you must not incise that scrofula at its root. Afterwards, it is necessary to tie it with strong doubled thread and to leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old linen which you have already submerged in Egyptian ointment, and then care for it.
And if you incise the whole scrofula, then you must adjust the two lips of the wound and stitch it immediately as soon as no superfluity remains.
But if you see that superfluity remains because of the size of the scrofula, it is necessary that you understand how to incise its root, and extract it, and administer suture and what has been said.
And that of the scrofulas which contains humidity, perforate it with a superficial perforation where the place of its maturation appears to you, and place the perforation so that it is the lowest part of the body, as we have said.
Then administer a perforation, perhaps with ointment and similar things, so that it corrodes what remains of the corruption. And then, when you see the wound already cleansed, then you cure it with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
Chapter 43.
Concerning the incision of the core of the abscess accidentally occurring within the throat.
The ancients described this incision in the core, and no one in our land has been seen to have performed it.
And this is the description/writing of the speech/discourse of those (ancients).
But in men having a rupture (hernia), the extraction of the core should be avoided, because no benefit is done thereby, since all the veins and the major ones of the patient are there.
But in those who have abscesses located in the mouth or throat or both ears, when the illness is not in the windpipe, it is necessary to use the incision of the core to avoid the harm which is caused by perforation.
Therefore, when we want to do this, we should incise the core below three circles of the circles of the core, or four, with a small incision in width in that which is between two circles in quantity, so that the incision is in the skin, not in the cartilage, and this place is suitable for incision, because it is devoid of flesh and its blood vessels are distant.
And if the physician is timid/fearful, then he should extend the throat's membrane with a hook, and then split the membrane until he reaches the canal, but he should avoid the blood vessels if he sees any of them.
And then incise the skin as we have recommended; and the incision of the windpipe is indicated by the inflammation which comes from it, by that which is ruptured from it, and by the loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
Therefore, when the time has arrived in which perforation is feared, bring together the two lips of the wound's membrane, and sew it only without the cartilage.
And then administer medicines that cause the flesh to grow until it is healed.
And Albucasis says that the sum of the discourse which we have recounted, the explanation is only that you see that some of these abscesses have already obstructed the gullet of the patient, and the patient has come close to death, and his breath is cut off, and some go to incise the epiglottis so that the patient breathes over the site of the wound for some respiration, and is saved from death.
And for this reason I order to leave the wound open until the malignancy of the disease is consumed; and its malignancy lasts for three days; and then I order to sew the wound, and cure it until it is healed.
And this which I have seen myself, is that a servant took a knife and put it in her throat and incised with it a part of the lung's tube, and I was called to her cure, and I found the mucus as it is coughed up by the dying, and I uncovered the wound and found the blood which had come out of the small wound.
Then I was confessed that she had not incised a vein or artery, and the wind came out of the wound, and I cured it until it was healed.
And nothing happened to the servant except hoarseness in her voice, and after some days she returned to a better state of health.
And now I say that in the dissection of the epileptic there is no fear.
Chapter 44.
Chapter 44. Of the incision concerning an abscess which occurs in the throat externally, and is called a throat hernia.
This abscess called throat hernia is a large abscess according to the complexion of the body, and is very common in women.
And it is according to two manners, for it is either natural or accidental.
And the natural one is not something to be cured.
And the accidental one is of two kinds, of which one is similar to fatty nodes, and the other kind is similar to an abscess which is caused by inflammation of an artery, and in that there is danger. And it is not fitting to apply iron to it except in the case of one that is small. And you probe it and investigate it with a probe, and if you find it similar to a fatty node, and it is not connected to any of the veins, then you incise it as you would nodes, and extract it with what contains it from the skin and bursicle; and if not, then its entire nature is examined, and then treat the area with what is necessary for treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 45.
Concerning the incision of the types of nodes.
There are many types of nodes, and I have already described all their types in the division (of chapters/sections).
And it is necessary that I recount here the difference between a node and an abscess, lest there be doubt.
Therefore I say that with an abscess there is heat and fever and vehement pain until the boiling of the superfluity is sedated; and putrefaction is complete, at which point the fever, pain, and heat cease.
But with a node there is neither heat nor fever nor pain, and it contains that certain humor which is in its own sac.
And it is according to the color of the body, and its beginning is like a swelling and is made like a blister, whether larger or smaller, and they are of two types, for either they are greasy and contain humidity, and the colors of the humidity are many, as I said in the division (of chapters/sections).
Therefore it is necessary, when you arrive at the cure of a node, that you expel that and inquire about that at the beginning with an instrument which is called an intromes, according to what its form will become in the chapter which is near this one.
And the manner of inquiry into abscesses and all nodes is that you take this instrument and introduce it into the most humid place which you find in the abscess, and you will revolve your fingers with that little by little until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the pus.
And then extend your hand according to the measure of the size of the abscess, and then extract the intromes and consider that which has come out in its track.
And if the running humidity, of whatever color it may be, is expelled, then you incise it with a superficial section as I have said in the other abscesses.
And if from the track of the intromes you find no humidity, then know that it is fatty (or thick/dense).
And dry it up with an incision having the form of a cross, according to this form: Thus as has been taught to you, and suspend it with hooks, and flay the skin from all sides easily, and keep the skin or sac, if you can, over it and extract that matter with the node (or swelling).
But if it is broken in the operation, it is not possible to extract that matter, and this often happens, and then you extract it in pieces until no cause remains there.
For if any cause, little or much, remains there, the swelling will return according to the measure (or degree).
And if you see that some small part remains, then fill the wound with some sharp corrosive powders and tighten the wound, and place over it a hot poultice, and treat it with another treatment until it is healed.
And if it is a large swelling, then you sew the two lips of it and treat it with that which causes it to granulate (heal by forming new tissue).
But if a pulsating vein is against it, or a non-pulsating one, then you consider and fill the place with crushed alum, which is vitriol, and leave it tight for two or three days until the wound dries and the boiling of the blood subsides; and then return to incise what is rotten of the swelling.

Chapter 47.
Of the treatment of the breasts of men which resemble those of women.
Sometimes the breasts of some men are inflamed due to excessive sleep, and then the breasts become inflamed and resemble the breasts of women, and remain as festering, white sores.
And for one who has this, it is necessary to incise it with a lunar incision according to this form, from line B to line G, and then scrape away all the thickness, and then fill the wound with incarnative medication, and then join the two edges of the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the breast sags downwards and is softened by its size, as it happens in women, then let it be split in its two upper parts, with two incisions similar to a lunar shape, each one contained within the other at the end of each, so that the larger line contains the smaller according to this form, from line B and line G. And then scrape the skin which is between the two edges of the wound, and remove the fat, and administer that which we have said of the suture and the powders, which is necessary in the cure until it is healed.
And if you cannot complete the incision of what needs to be incised because of the patient's discomfort, and because of the flow of blood, then you must fill the wound with cotton soaked in corrosive ointment and leave it until it corrodes the remaining fat, and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
Of the perforation of abscesses that appear below the nipple.
These abscesses that appear below the nipple are a type of scrofula, hard, and containing a sac-like skin.
And some of these contain humidity; and those that contain humidity should be incised with a lunate incision according to this figure: from line G to line B. And when all the pus in the abscess has been evacuated, then you fill it with old cotton and leave it there until the next day.
Then the cotton should be removed and it should be treated with a suitable ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess is of the type of nodes and scrofula, then you should incise it as we have said in the section on scrofula above.
But if the wound is so extensive that it causes putrefaction, then apply cautery as we have previously described.
Chapter 49.
Concerning the incision of abscesses that occur from an arrow wound or from a vein.
When an arrow is broken, and the remaining portion embedded in the wound is consolidated, then often an abscess occurs.  The signs by which it is known that inflammation and abscess are from an artery or vein are these: for when the abscess is from an arrow, it is long and extends deeply into the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel a sort of grating sound within it.
And that which is from a vein is round and superficial on the body.
And incision of this abscess is harmful, especially that which is under the armpits, and in the groin, and in the neck, and in many places of the body; and it is very great, therefore its treatment with iron should be avoided.  And that which is in the extremities, or in the head, should be avoided as well.
But concerning that which is there from the inflammation of the arrow wound opening, make a lengthwise incision in the remaining portion of the arrow; and then widen the incision with forceps and around the arrow, and free it from the tissues until it is uncovered.
And then place a needle on it and make it penetrate to the other side, and tie the arrow with double thread in two places — which procedure I have shown you in the extraction of two arrows which were in the temples.
And then puncture the place which is between two ligaments with a spatula until all the blood which is there comes out, and the abscess is dissolved.
And then administer treatment in which powder is applied until the ligaments fall away; and then treat that with ointments suitable for this until it is healed.
And if it is an abscess caused by a severed vein, then it is necessary that you hold with your hand as much of the abscess as you can with the remainder, and then insert the needle below the place which you have held under your hand, and make it penetrate; and let there be double thread on it so that you extract it from the other side, and then tie the abscess with good ligature as I have told you in the ligation of a grape, in two parts with two ends of the thread.
But if you fear that the thread will be pulled out, then insert the thread with that needle under the whole abscess below the incision of the first needle, and tighten the your threads in four places.
Then dry the abscess in the middle of it until, when that which is in it has come out, then you incise the superfluity of the remainder, and leave that which is bound to it.
And then place on it a powder which you have submerged in wine and oil, and then administer the treatment which is done with poultices and ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 1 of the abscess which happens through the twisting of nerves.
Just as abscesses occur in arteries and veins, similarly abscesses occur in the nerves when they are struck or injured by excessive work, and similarly.
And it is formed most in the places of the joints or of the heel, or in any place where the joints are weakened.
And the abscess is serious, and is similar in color to the other body parts, and it is in the worst dispositions of them and when it is tightly constricted with vehemence, the patient feels a similar numbness.
And the abscess is not located in the front of the body, and therefore it is below the ribs, and it is weakened in every part and does not extend to the front or back parts.
Therefore, that which is in the joints should not be incised with iron, for contraction will quickly follow.
But that which is in the head or forehead, incise it with a spatula. But if it is a small abscess, then grasp it with forceps and excise it by the roots.
And if it is large, then you should suspend it with a hook, and flay it, and then remove it; and close the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 51.
Concerning the incision of an abscess which occurs in the groin, which happens to the belly.
It happens to some men many times in their bellies and in other parts of their bodies that there are swellings which are called fungals by similarity to fungi, of which the root is subtle and the head is large; and the lips of these are turned inward, and some are small, and some are very large.
And I have seen one, and in his belly were two swellings which were very similar to fungi, and there was no difference between them, white, with slender roots.
And the lips of these were inverted and split open, and humidity constantly flowed from them.
Therefore I incised them and found in one eighteen ounces of pus, and in the other six ounces.
And the operation in their incision is that you consider then if the patient is humid, and the color of the swelling is white, humid, with a subtle root; then you incise it with a broad spatula, and let cauteries be present in the fire, because many times after the section much blood is expelled; and if the blood overcomes you, cauterize it.
And if you see the patient fearful and timid, beware of incision with iron; but then take freshly made lead plaster, and bind the swelling with it, in the manner which we have described; and leave it for two days.
And then adjust the tightness of the lead thread; therefore, do not cease to tighten the lead thread strongly until it is cut off and falls by itself without labor.
But if the swelling has a thick root, then in its incision there is some cause for fear and dread, and especially that which is in the abdomen.
But it is possible that you incise half of it, and then cauterize it so that it does not return.
And then care for it until the wound is healed.
And beware that you do not presume to incise a swelling which is of a dark color, of little feeling, and of a bad appearance, for that is an abscess of cancer.
And I come to the remembrance of cancer with this.
Chapter 52.
On the treatment of a swelling of the umbilicus (navel).
A swelling of the umbilicus is caused by many things; either by fissures of the diaphragm which is above the belly, and from it the membrane or intestine, according to what happens in other ruptures; or by blood which flows from a vein or artery, as has been said before; or by wind constricted there.
But if it is due to a fissure of the diaphragm and protrusion of the membrane, then the color of the abscess is similar to the color of the body; and it is soft without pain, and appears in various locations. And if it is due to the protrusion of the intestine, then its location is as we have recounted, with even more significant variation, and when you press it with your fingers it is hidden, and returns; and very quickly it is there again, and is greatly increased by the entry of a bath and strenuous work.
But if it is due to humidity (fluid), then it is soft and is not hidden when you press it with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor does it grow.
And if it is due to blood, then the abscess appears with these signs, tending towards blackness.
But if it is due to raw flesh (a wound), then the abscess is severe, large and hard, and is located according to a certain amount (or quantity/size).
But if it is due to flatulence (wind), then its touch is soft.
And the operation in it is that you consider then if the prominence of the umbilicus is due to arterial blood, or venous blood, or due to flatulence; then it is necessary that you are prohibited from the treatment of it, because in that there is fear and danger as I have taught you in the chapter in which I have described abscesses from arteries and veins.
And if the prominence of the umbilicus is due to the intestine or the membrane (peritoneum/hernia sac), then it is necessary that you command the patient to hold their breath and be lying down extended; and then mark with a probe around the whole umbilicus, and then command him to lie between your hands on his back, and then surround with a wide bandage around the umbilicus over the place which you have marked with the probe; and then extend half of the abscess upwards with a large inch; and then bind around it with strong thread or with a bandage and in a decent manner of binding.
And then open the middle of the extended abscess over the ligature and introduce your finger into that space and seek the intestine; and if you have found it and held it by the ligature, then you pull gently and push the intestine back into the inside of the abdomen.
And if you have found the membrane (hernia sac), then grasp it with forceps; and incise its superfluous parts.
But if it is close to an artery or vein, then surround that area carefully, and then return to your operation; and take two needles and thread strong thread into them and insert the two needles into the circumference which you have made around the abscess in the manner of a cross, making them penetrate; and then tighten the abscess into four parts over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the place so that the extended flesh may suppurate and fall away of its own accord; or incise it when it is thinned and suppurates, and then treat it with whatever ointments are necessary until it is healed.
And if the prominence of the umbilicus is swollen with flesh or by humidity, then it is necessary that you open the abscess as we have said and draw out the blood or the humidity which you find there, and then treat it with what is necessary and which will consolidate the area.
Chapter 53.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have already spoken, in the division, of the types of cancer and in what manner it is possible to treat it with medicines, and that it is advisable to avoid treating it with iron so that it does not become ulcerated; and we have discussed cancer and the caution and care of its treatment.
And the ancients said that sometimes cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible, such as cancer which is in the breasts or in the groin and similarly located members, to extract it all, especially when it is small and beginning.
But when it is old and large, one should not attempt to treat it oneself, for I have neither been able to cure anyone of it nor have I seen anyone else who has succeeded in this.
And the operation on it, when it is located as we have said, is that you should proceed and purge the patient and remove some black bile at times, and then bleed them if a manifest plethora is found.
And then prepare the patient with a preparation in which the location for the operation is situated, and then apply to the wound ointments which are suitable for it; and then scrape it thoroughly with a sharp curette until no part of its roots remain. And let the blood flow, and do not stanch it lightly, and press the areas, and wipe away the thick blood entirely with your hand or with any instrument which is available to you.
But if you find an excessive flow of thick blood from an incision of a vein or artery, then cauterize it until the flow of blood ceases, and then treat it with the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 44.  (Assuming a standard chapter numbering system)
On the treatment of dropsy.
We have already recounted, in the division, the types of dropsy, and the signs of each type, and their treatment with medicines.
And that which is cured with iron is only the species of ascites, and iron is not applied to the species of tympanites or the species of anasarca, because that is fatal.
And when you have treated this type of tympanitic dropsy with medicines and your treatment is not effective, then consider this: if the patient has already developed weakness, or suffers from another illness alongside this dropsy, such as a cough or abdominal pain, and similar conditions, then beware of treating them with iron, for there is danger in that.
But if you see a patient with exuberant strength, and with no other illness but dropsy alone, and neither a child nor an old person, then the method of operating is that you lift the patient between your hands, and the assistant gently presses their abdomen with his hand, driving the water towards the area below the pubic bone, and then take a sharp, pointed instrument in this manner:
Let it be two-edged, long, with a sharp point like a lancet; however, let there be some shortness in it, little by little, so that in operating with it you do not penetrate the intestine and wound it.
And then consider if the origin of the dropsy is from the intestines, for then it is necessary that you, with the incision, move away from the navel by the amount of three fingers towards the lower parts, in a straight line above the pubic bone.
But if the origin of the dropsy is due to liver disease, then your incision should be three fingers to the left of the navel.
And if its origin is from the spleen, then your incision should be on the right.
And it is not fitting that your incision be on the side on which the patient wants to lie, lest the superfluities run to that fragile place.
And then perforate the entire sheath with the instrument, and then insert it into that incision, and raise your hand with the spatula between the sheath and the peritoneum, and you should scrape that; and if it is the amount of a fingernail or thereabouts.
And then perforate the peritoneum until the spatula reaches the empty space, which is the location of the water. And then extract that and insert an instrument of which this is the form: and it is similar to a fine silver cannula; it is made of silver or bronze or latten, which at its lowest part has a small hole, and on its sides there are three holes: two on one side and one on the other; and its incised extremity is shaped like a feather, like this:
And at its uppermost extremity is a ring, because when the instrument reaches the water, then that immediately settles on the instrument, therefore evacuating the water in a moderate amount, because if you evacuate more than is necessary at that time, the patient will quickly die from the dissolution of their animal spirit, or a syncope occurs which is close to death.
But evacuate according to the quantity of its virtue and according to what the patient's disposition signifies to you, the virtue of their pulse and their well-being.
And then extract the instrument, and retain the water, the reason for which is that it is immediately retained by the chain which retains the hole that is on the cup, of which I have told you that you should perforate it in that manner.
And then repeat the procedure with the instrument on another day if you see the patient tolerating it, and extract a small quantity of water, and do this for several days cautiously, and beware of error so that only a little water remains.
But if you are afraid for the patient, and leave a large quantity of water, then treat him with burial in warm sand, and induce sweating in a bath, and expose him to the sun for a long time; and treat him with drying medicines until he is healed.
And cauterize the stomach, spleen, and liver after the extraction of the water, as I have recounted in what precedes.
Chapter 55.
Concerning the treatment of children born with imperforate urinary tracts.
Sometimes a child is born from its mother's womb and the head of its penis is not perforated (or the opening is narrow or not in its proper place).
Therefore, it is necessary to treat it with perforation as soon as it is born, using a very fine instrument, according to this method:
And then place a thin lead pin in the opening and leave it there for three or four days.
And when he wants to urinate, let him be helped and he will urinate; and then remove the pin.
And if you do not place the lead pin, it is possible that the urine, which flows over the area, will not allow it to close.
But the one whose opening is narrow should be treated with a lead pin, as we have said, for several days until it is widened.
But in those in whom the opening is not in its proper place, the cause is that the opening is at the end of the penis, so that he cannot urinate unless he lifts his penis upwards with his hand; and he is infertile, because the sperm cannot reach the womb in a straight line, and this is a very long-lasting illness.
And the manner of the operation is that the patient should be placed on his back, and then extend the head of his penis with your left hand with a firm pull, and incise the head of the penis at the location of the foreskin with a sharp scalpel, like a feather incision, or that you shave a small part, so that the middle of it becomes similar to the head of the penis and the opening falls in the middle as needed.
And be careful in your operation of the flow of blood, for this often happens; and stop the bleeding with what you have, and treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 56.
Of the whitlow which occurs in the prepuce at the head of the penis, and of the corruption, blackness, and adhesion of the prepuce to the head of the penis.
Many times this whitlow occurs on the penis and is a swelling of foul flesh.
And of these, one is malignant and the other is not malignant.
For the non-malignant one, you should suspend it with a fine thread and excise it until you have completely cleaned it; then apply to that wound an ointment soaked in Egyptian ointment; and after that, treat it with diafinicon ointment until it is healed.
And if the whitlow is malignant, of a dark color, then you should apply cautery around the incision and its root.
But if the whitlow is in the prepuce of the uncircumcised penis, and one part of the whitlow is inside the prepuce and the other part is outside, then you should remove the whitlow which is inside first until it is healed; and then you treat the one outside, because when you treat both at the same time the prepuce is at risk of being damaged.
And if you find in the genitals and prepuce blackness and corruption, then you should remove all that which is already blackened and which is beginning to corrupt or is already corrupted; and then apply to it a mixture of honey, pounded with the rind of a pomegranate and crushed and sifted herbs.
And then treat it with another [appropriate] treatment until it is healed.
But if there is a flow of blood, then apply lunar cautery according to this method:
because cautery is helpful in two conditions at once, that is to say, to a flow of blood and to a wound when it is corrupted.
And if the head of the penis is corroded and completely destroyed, then you should insert into the meatus of the prepuce a lead cannula so that the patient urinates through it.
In the adhesion of the prepuce to the head of the penis (and this adhesion does not happen except in one whose prepuce is healthy, and circumcision is not required on him; and when adhesion happens due to a wound or an abscess), it is necessary that you scrape with a blunt spatula until the adhesion is dissolved and the head of the penis is freed from all parts.
And if it is difficult to separate these delicately, then you should scrape with a spatula some part of the head of the penis and of the prepuce, and this is because the prepuce, when it is thin, is very easily perforated by its slight thinness.
Then separate the prepuce from the head of the penis with a very thin linen cloth, which has already been moistened in cold water so that they do not adhere again.
And then let it be treated with styptic wine until it is consolidated (healed).
Chapter 57.
Concerning the cleansing of children and the treatment of what happens to those who are circumcised.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity like other wounds; however, since it is of our operation and our will, and since it is done primarily on infants, it is necessary that we assign to it the best operation and the easiest path leading to health.
Therefore I say that the ancients did not discuss circumcision in any of their books, because they did not use it in their laws, and it is only because we have acquired it through experience.  The reason is that I have found most surgeons and barbers use circumcision and cleansing with a lancet and with forceps; and they use ligature with thread and incision with the nail.
And I have tried all these methods and I find no better cleansing with forceps, with ligature with thread, than cleansing with the nail, because many times the skin peels back from above, because the foreskin has two layers, and the lower layer remains, and it is easily forced to another incision and sure pain. And with cleansing which is done with an awl, and is digital, which cobblers use, the incision of the tip of the penis is not safe, because it easily enters the hole.
But in the operation with a nail, it easily becomes rusty and corrupts your operation. And the foreskin of the child is short by nature, and many times it is born in such a way that it has no need of cleansing, and I have seen this.
But of the cleansing that is done with forceps and ligatures with thread, the goodness, experience has shown me, because the forceps are of proportional incision, because the sharpness that is above is the same as the sharpness that is below; and thus as you press your hand, there are two proportional sharpnesses, and it cuts according to a comparison at one time; and compression is made of the thread with a shape containing the foreskin of the penis from every part, with which no error happens at all.
And the manner of the first operation is that you make the child stand, especially if it is one of those that understands a little, that you do nothing but tie the thread to its penis only, and let it be until the next day.
And then do what you can to encourage it in whatever way that it can understand. And then hold the child steadily between your hands, and let it not be restless; and hide the forceps in your hand or under your foot, so that the child does not see them or any other of the instruments.
And then place your hand on his penis and blow into the foreskin, and pull it until the head of the penis is exposed, and then clean it of whatever dirt is accumulated there.
The shape of the forceps for circumcision, this is the shape:
Let one end be non-cutting and the other cutting, so that the nail's shaft is not exposed, and let the length of the two pointed ends be equal to the length of two handles of equal size.
But the error in circumcision is that you too quickly remove the entire inner foreskin, or a part of it, from below the incision.
Therefore, it is necessary that you stretch that [skin] with your fingernail before the suppuration of the area and that you incise it evenly.
And if you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then pull it with forceps and incise it.
But if three days have passed, and what is swollen remains under the suppurated area, then you should leave it until the hot abscess subsides, and then carefully incise it and cut it as you see fit.
And be careful of the head of the penis, for this will harm him.
And then treat it with that which consolidates the wound, from the powders which we have recommended in the treatise on powders.
And if it is incised beyond measure from the scrotum and reaches the parts above, this will not harm him.
And furthermore, treat it with what we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 58.
Concerning the treatment of urine retained in the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder is caused by obstruction from a stone, or congealed blood, or pus, or flesh, and similar to those things we mentioned concerning the methods of treatment in the division.
And if it is not dissolved by what we have said, and the cause is vehement in the patient, then it is necessary to administer extraction with an instrument which is called a syringe, of which this is the form:  Let it be of silver and be a slender, concave tube, like a bird's quill, with the subtlety of a long ray, about a palm and a half in length, which has a narrow tube at its tip.
And the manner of drawing out the urine with this is that you take a doubled bladder and tie wool or cotton at its end with a good binding; and introduce the ends of the bladder into the lower part of the syringe, and cut with scissors if there is any excess wool, but introduce it into the cannula forcefully. Then anoint the syringe with butter and with egg white.
And make the patient sit on a chair, and rub their bladder and penis with moist oils, olive oil and rosewater.
Then gently insert the syringe until it reaches the root of the penis; then bend the penis upwards towards the navel; and then push the syringe inside that; and then push it until it reaches the bladder, and the patient will feel that it has reached the void; and this is not done in a straight line, but the passage goes in a tortuous way.
Then gradually extend the bladder with the wool, because the urine will wet the wool.
Then remove it and treat the urine, and use a syringe; do not cease doing this and using the syringe until the bladder is emptied and the patient finds relief.
Chapter 59.
Concerning the method of clysterizing the bladder with a catheter and similar instruments suitable for this purpose.
When an ulceration or abscess occurs in the bladder, or congealed blood is present in it, or putrefaction is constricted there, and you wish to instill waters and medicines into it, then it should be done with an instrument which is called a catheter, and this is its form:
except that its extremity is blunt, that is, closed. And in that [extremity] are three holes: two on one side, thus it is made.
In the beginning, it is concave, in which is what is pushed in, and it should be according to the quantity that fills that [cavity], without addition, until when any matter is drawn from the humors with it, it is drawn out; and when it is expelled, with that [the instrument] it is drawn, and when expelled long, as is done by a catheter with which naphtha is poured into the ships in sea battles.
Therefore, when you want to draw out humors from the bladder, introduce the extremity of the catheter into the humor, and draw with it that which is pushed to the upper parts, because the humor is drawn into the concavity of the catheter.
And then introduce the extremity of the catheter into the urethra as we have described in the syringe; and then push the humor with what you push, because that humor comes to the bladder immediately, and the patient feels it.
The form of the fine catheter with which the bladder is catheterized, and this is the form:
And let it be made of silver or tin, of which the upper end should resemble a small feather, and below it a smooth, narrow tube.
And then take a goatskin bladder, and place in it the humor you wish to instill into the bladder, and then tie it tightly between two ends with strong double thread, and warm that humor over the fire.
And then introduce the end of the catheter into the penis (urethra).
And then squeeze your hand on the bladder with the liquid, pressing gently, until the patient says that the liquid has reached the bladder.
But if you do not have a bladder at hand, then take a piece of parchment and make a circle from it; and perforate it all around its circumference; and then insert strong thread into those holes, so that it is tightly bound; and then attach the circle in such a way that it is well sealed, after you place in it what you want of the humors, and oils, and waters.
And then you tie that to the instrument and do with that as you did with the bladder, and express with your hand until the liquid reaches the bladder.
Chapter 60.
On the extraction of a stone.
We have already discussed in the division the types of stones and their treatment with medicines, and the difference between stones generated in the kidneys and stones generated in the bladder, and their symptoms.
And we have discussed the stone in which the operation is done by hand and incision.
And the stone generated in the bladder properly is that which is lodged in the bladder.
And I am narrating that with clear exposition and brevity.
Therefore I say that stones generated in the bladder mostly affect children; and among its symptoms is that the urine that comes from the bladder is similar to water in its thinness, and sand appears in it; and the patient rubs his genitals, and often cries, and is restless; and with this, pus comes out of the anus in many of them.
And the healing of children from this is easier until they reach fourteen years; and it is difficult to cure in the elderly. But in young people it is easier because of this, since it is among them.  (meaning: it's easier to cure in young people because the condition is common among them.)
And of that (disease) in which the stone is larger, the cure is easier; and if it is small, it is the opposite.
Therefore, to arrive at the cure of this, it is necessary at the beginning that we cleanse the patient with a clyster, which removes all the filth that is in his bowels; for this filth sometimes prevents the expulsion of the stone through its search.
Then let the patient be taken with his feet up and shaken and rubbed gently so that the stone descends to the bottom of the bladder, or that it jumps from its place sometimes.
Then make that small incision between your hands, prepared, and his hands should be under his thighs so that the whole bladder is inclined downwards.
And then search for it and all that is outside.
Therefore, if you feel the stone in the area, then immediately attend to it with an incision.
. And therefore, push the stone until it rests under your finger. And return it little by little into the neck of the bladder.
And then press on that with your finger, and expel it outwards, away from the place where you made the incision.
And instruct the assistant to press on the bladder with her hand; and instruct another assistant to extend the genitals upwards with her right hand, and with her other hand to support the perineum which is below the genitals, near the location of the incision.
And then insert a well-sharpened spatula, of which this is the form: and cut what is between the anus and the genitals, not in the middle, but towards the left thigh; and let the incision be over the stone, and your finger be in the anus, and let a transverse incision be made, so that the external incision is wide and the internal narrow, according to the size that allows the removal of the stone, and no larger, because easily you can compress with your finger which is in the anus, the stone against the incision, and it is expelled without difficulty.
And know that some stones are angular and rough, which makes their removal difficult because of that.
And of those, that which resembles a gland, and is round, is easily removed.
Therefore, if it is one that is angular and rough, adjust it in the fissure.
And if it is not expelled in this way, then you must push on it, or press on it with a suitably shaped instrument, the tip of which is like a file with which you press against the stone, and it does not escape; or introduce under it a thin instrument with a curved tip.
But if you cannot reach it, then you enlarge the opening a little, and if you see a flow of blood, staunch it with crushed vitriol.
But if the stone is more than one, then initially you apply your hand to the opening of the bladder, and then incise it; and then gently push it; and similarly do if there are many more than two.
And if the stone is very large, then it is unwise to incise it with a large incision, for two things may happen to the patient: either he dies or he suffers from continuous urinary incontinence, because the wound is not completely healed.
But administer its expulsion until it is expelled, or work on breaking it up with forceps until you extract it in pieces.
Then, when you have completed your operation, fill the wound with aloe and dragon's blood powder, and bind it tightly. And place over that a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or in rose oil and cold water, so that a hot abscess may be calmed.
Then lay the patient on his back, and do not untie the bandage until the third day.
And when you have untied it, wash the area with water and plenty of oil, and then treat it with diachylon ointment and basilicon ointment until it is healed.
But if in the wound there appears a hot, burnt, and corrosive abscess, and it resembles blood frozen in the bladder, and because of it urine is prevented (and this is known by the passage of blood with urine), then insert your finger into the wound and extract that blood, for if it remains it provokes corruption of the bladder and putrefaction therein.
Then wash the wound with sour wine, water, and salt, and with any other suitable remedy, doing what is appropriate for its healing until it is healed.
And it is necessary during all the treatment that you bind both thighs and adjust the medicines which are placed on the area.
And if the stone is small and is in the meatus of the penis, and is lodged there, and the flow of urine is obstructed, then treat it with what I have recommended before you resort to incision, because many times because of this treatment incision is required; and I have proved this, and it is that you take a polished iron perforator, of which this is the form:
of triangular, sharp, pointed end, fixed in wood.
And then take bile, and bind the penis with it below the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
And then insert the iron into the penis, perforating easily until the perforator reaches the stone. And revolve the perforator with your hand on that stone little by little, and you will enlarge the perforation until you make it penetrate from the other side; for the urine will immediately be released.
And then press your hand on what remains of the stone on the outside of the penis, because that is fragmented, and it is expelled with the urine, and the patient is healed.
But if this cure is not suitable for you due to some reason that prohibits it, then bind one bandage below the stone, and another bandage above the stone. And then incise upon the stone in that penis between the two ligatures. And then extract it. And then untie the bandages and cleanse the congealed blood which is formed in the wound.
And it is not necessary to bandage below the stone except so that it does not return to the bladder, and the other bandage above only because when the bandage is untied near the extraction of the stone, the urine returns to its place and heals the wound.
And for this reason it is necessary for you, when you tie the bandage above, that you elevate the urine to the upper parts, or that it return to its proper place, and the wound heals as we have said.
Chapter 61.
On the extraction of a stone from women.
Stones are rarely generated in women.
But if a stone is found in one of them, then its cure is difficult, and is prohibited in many ways.
One reason is that women are very easily made virgins (implying the difficulty of procedures due to the hymen).
And the second is that you will not find a chaste woman, or a married woman, who willingly reveals herself to the physician.
The third is that you will not find a woman who is well-skilled in this art, especially the operation by hand.
And the fourth is that the location of the incision for a stone in women is far from the location of the stone itself, wherefore a deep incision is needed, and in that there is danger.
But if necessity compels you to this, then you must obtain a female physician who is skilled, and few such are to be found.
But if you are deprived of that, then seek a cautious and subtle physician, or present a matron well-versed in the matters of women, or a woman who knows some part of this art.
Therefore, present that woman, and command her to do all that you instruct, from the initial examination for the stone; which means that she should determine if the woman is a virgin, then she should insert her finger into her anus and seek the stone.
Then, if she finds it, let her grasp it with her finger, and then command her to incise over it.
But if she is not a virgin, then command the matron to insert her finger into the vulva of the patient and search for the stone, while placing her left hand on the bladder, and compress it with a good compression.
Then, if she finds it, she should gently pull it from the opening of the bladder downwards with the sum of her strength until it reaches the root of the thing.
And then incise upon it near the midpoint of the vulva below the root of the thing, wherever it is stopped, and center the stone in that area, and her finger should not be removed from the stone while grasping it.
And the incision should be small at the beginning; and then introduce the instrument into that incision, and if the stone is felt, then adjust the incision according to the size that she knows the stone will be removed from it.
And know that the types of stones are many: for some are small and large, and smooth and rough, and long and round, and having branches.
Therefore you should know how many types there are of it so that you may indicate what you want.
And if you see a flow of blood, apply crushed vitriol to the place and keep it there for an hour until the bleeding stops, and then return to your operation until the stone is removed.
And make sure you prepare the instruments which are mentioned in the extraction of stones in men, so that you may be aided by them in your operation.
And if you see a flow of blood, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from the artery that has been incised, then place powder on the area and compress it with powders and a descending bandage, and do not repeat this; and leave the stone and do not extract it, for the patient will very quickly die. And then take care of the wound.
Then, when the sharpness of the bleeding has subsided after a few days, and the area has healed, then you return to your operation until the stone is removed.
Chapter 62.
Concerning the incision of a strangulated hernia.
A watery hernia is nothing but an accumulation of moisture in the white scrotum, which is below the genitals, containing the testicle, and is called scrotum.
And sometimes it is in the proper pouch which nature prepared for the testicle, so that it is believed that it is another testicle; and it is the cord of the genital between the white scrotum which we have mentioned, and this is only a swelling.
And the generation of this hernia is due to weakness which affects the genitals, so that this matter is poured into them, and sometimes it happens due to a blow inflicted on the genitals.
And this moisture has many colors, for its color is either tending towards yellow or bloody, or red, or it is black and foul-smelling, or it is a watery white, and this is the most common.
And the signs by which it is known where this accumulation of water is are these: for if it is in the white scrotum which we have mentioned, then the swelling is round, slightly elongated, like the shape of an egg, and it surrounds the testicle, for the moisture contains it on all sides.
And if the moisture is in the proper pouch, then the swelling is round near the testicle and therefore it is believed to be another testicle.
And if the moisture is between the root of the genitals and the white scrotum, then it falls below the feeling/sensation.
And when you want to know the color of the moisture, then you test the color of the moisture in the swelling with a probe, from which the shape is determined; and according to what comes out on the probe, judge what is inside.
Therefore, when we come to its cure with iron (cautery), then it is necessary that we order the patient to be phlebotomized if that is possible for you, and see his body full (of blood).
Then let him be supine on his back on something slightly raised, and place many cloths under him.
And then sit on his left side, and command the assistant to stand on his right, who should extend his rod (probe or similar instrument) on one of the two sides, to the root of the genitals and the region of the navel.
And then take a broad spatula and split the perineum in the middle lengthwise almost to the anus.
And let the incision be made straight and equally distant from the line where you should separate the skin of the genitals, until it reaches the white fatty tissue (included/comprehended).
And scrape that [fatty tissue], and observe, and be careful not to cut it; and your scraping, with which you scrape it, should be suspended [gentle] and subtly investigated with the scraping according to the quantity possible for you.
And then perforate the water-filled cyst with a wide perforation, and extract all the water.
And then separate the two lips of the incision with hooks, and extend the cyst to the parts above; and do not touch the perineum, and incise the cyst in the way that is possible for you. And incise it in its entirety or in pieces, and especially its thin layer, for if you do not meticulously investigate its incision, there is no guarantee that the water will not return.
But if the egg [testicle] protrudes from its sheath during your operation, then when you have completed the incision of the cyst, return it inside, and then join the two lips of the perineum with suture, and then cure it as you would other wounds, until it is healed.
And if you find the testicle corrupted by another disease, then it is necessary that you tie the vessels which are on the spermatic cord, and remove the testicle, and then treat it as we have said.
But if the fluid is collected in two parts together, then know that there are two hernias; therefore incise that part in the same way as you did the other; and if it seems to you that it is one operation, then you perform it.
And then you wipe away the blood, and insert into the incisions wool soaked in oil or rose oil; and place outside other wool which you have soaked in wine and oil, and spread this over the perineum and the area of the abdomen; and place on top folded cloths and powders, and bind these on top with a bandage which is according to this form:
And then let two cloths be taken and filled with carded wool in this manner; and let the ends be made with which it is bound of cotton or damp wool, and let them be fastened with a tight binding in the waistband of the breeches, and one of the ends should reach from the buttocks to the front, and the second reach to the part of the groin, and two ends over two cushions below and two other ends over two knees, and the whole binding should be tightened in the waistband of the breeches.
And sometimes this hernia is treated artificially with cautery instead of incision with an iron; and it is that you take a thin, knife-like cautery and that you incise with it the skin of the perineum, and it is heated as we have recounted until the white pus containing the fluid is discovered, and then you take a cautery according to this form: and it resembles the Greek letter.
And then pierce with that cautery, and it is heated until all the moisture is expelled.
And then extend the abscess with an unguent, and scrape with a thin, heated cautery; and incise it as much as possible until you eradicate it all; and be careful that the fire does not touch the egg (presumably referring to the testicle).
And know that this operation is safer and involves less blood loss, and better than operation with incision.
But if an abscess hinders you during the incision or cauterization, or blood flow, then you must treat whatever is hindering you, with the treatment that is appropriate to it, as has been described.
But if the patient is fearful and does not expect a second operation because of its horror, then administer perforation as I have recommended; and that is when you are certain, from what we have said about the signs, that the hernia is watery.
Then you should make the patient sit on some elevated seat, then perforate the abscess in its lowest part with a wide spatula, and position the perforation according to the length of the body (likely referring to the length of the abscess).
And let the opening be wide enough so that all the water flows out.
And then place a bandage on the area and tie it, and leave it so that the remaining water drains. And then treat it until the wound is healed.
But if there is an obstruction to the drainage of the water, then you should perforate it: and this is done only because the white pannicle in the opening of the wound creates an obstruction and prevents the outflow of water; then you must put into the wound a probe or instrument with which the water is drawn out from dropsies, the form of which has been described previously; or apply gentle pressure to the wound.
And know that if the water returns and is collected again within six months or thereabouts; and when it is collected, then it should be perforated in the same manner, for the patient will thus pass their days.
Chapter 63.
Concerning the incision of a fleshy hernia and its treatment.
Know that in the incision of this hernia there is a fear of loss leading to death in the most favorable circumstances; therefore, in my view, its removal and saving by other means is better.
And I am recalling the operation on that and the types of abscesses.
Therefore, I say that abscesses arise in the genitals which are composed of these things and this is caused by many things: either by an acute, acrid superfluity which is poured into the genitals, or by percussion (a blow).
And the color of the abscess follows the color of the body, and no pain is found in it, and it is a serious abscess.
And very quickly is a stony abscess formed, of which the color is dark, and there is no feeling in it.
And sometimes it is caused by a knotting of the arteries, and by an inflammation of them such that it is perceived, or by inflammation of the veins.
