But that which is from inflammation of the arteries is known by the dispersion of the abscess when we compress it with our fingers; and it should not be touched.
But that which is caused by inflammation of a vein is not dispersed by any means upon compression with the fingers.
And it is possible that an incision should be made upon this type [of abscess] and on the other flesh; and it is that you incise the skin of the genitals, and then extend the testicle upwards, and extract it from the white sheath, and free the suspensory ligament, and tie it, and incise the suspensory ligament after you have freed it from every part of the parts of the testicle.
Therefore, if the testicle is already consolidated with those inflamed tissues, then it is necessary that you extract the testicle and incise it.
And if there is adhesion between any part of the sheath, or between that which is between the vessels, then it is necessary that you free all that adhesion and incise it with a circular incision.
But if there is a growth of flesh in the place of the adhesion which is behind, then it is necessary that you incise all that which is there, and extract the testicle as we have said.
Therefore, when your operation is complete, then fill the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and wine; and then treat it with other treatments until it is healed.
Chapter 64.
Of the treatment of a hernia that is of the scrotum (or, a scrotal hernia).
A scrotal hernia is an abscess enveloped by some covering similar to a pouch, with softening of the testicles, and it makes movement, exercise, and walking difficult for the patient.
And in the treatment of this illness, it is necessary to fear that which preceded it (i.e., the cause of the hernia).
However, we must describe the operation on it as the first physicians did.
And I say that you must have the patient sit on a raised seat; then gently push the suspensory ligament of the testicles downwards; then hold the scrotum with your fingers, along with the vessels that are near the penis; and let another assistant hold those, extending them with firm pressure.
And then incise with a sharp, broad scalpel in a transverse section to the right of the vessels, until the vessels are exposed.
And then flay the entire area as we have told you in the extraction of arrowheads that are lodged in the temples.
And then insert into them a double-threaded needle, and tie it at the first point where you made the incision, and tie it again at the other end. And then incise those in the middle lengthwise with a straight incision, and extract what has gathered of foul and corrupt humors.
And then treat the wound with the same treatment as other wounds in which you wish healing to be generated, so that the vessels which nourish one of the two genitalia do not rupture, for this is not harmful.
But if you injure all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles be removed or that the testicles be ligated along with the vessels, so that they are deprived of all nourishment by the incision of the vessels, and that they wither, or that they be tied off so that they receive no nourishment.
Chapter 65.
Concerning the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a tear that happens in the peritoneum extended over the abdomen, on the side of the inguinal rings of the abdominal muscle, causing the intestines to protrude through that rupture.
And this rupture is either of the peritoneum or an extension of it, and these two types occur for many reasons: percussion, jumping, shouting, lifting something heavy, or similar.
And the sign of this, when it is due to an extension of the peritoneum, is that it occurs gradually over a long time, and not suddenly, and it is an inguinal abscess near the groin because the peritoneum compresses the intestine.
And the sign of it, when it is due to a tear in the peritoneum, is that at the beginning it causes sudden, intense pain; and it is an abscess that appears differently under the skin of the groin, and this is due to the protrusion of the intestine, and its body is outside the peritoneum.
And sometimes the omentum is expelled along with the intestine, and this hernia is called an intestinal zirbal hernia.  (Note: "zirbal" is likely a term specific to this text and its meaning may need further investigation within the context of medieval medical terminology.)
And sometimes it is formed with flatulence, and sometimes a tear occurs in the intestine, and the air is retained there, and this causes great harm to the patient, and sometimes it causes severe pain, especially when pressed.
And in the treatment of this type of illness, iron (or cautery) is dangerous, therefore its use should be avoided in this case.
And the method of operating on this is that you instruct the patient to return the intestine with his hand to its cavity if it yields to repositioning; then, he should be positioned on his side between your hands, and raise his legs.
Then stretch the scrotum, which is the sac of the testicles, upwards, and incise the scrotum lengthwise; then insert two hooks into the lips of the incision, as you need to open the incision with them.
And if possible, according to the amount you can manage, let the egg (presumably referring to a hernia or some similar swelling) be expelled from the incision.
Then scrape away the skin which is under the scrotum until the hard white tissue is uncovered on each side.
Then insert your finger into what is the testicle, into what is between the white tissue, which is under the scrotum, and the second tissue layer, and break up with it the adhesion that is behind the testicle.
Then gently manipulate the scrotum upwards with your hand, and with this stretch the white tissue upwards with your left hand, and elevate the testicle with the tissue towards the incision; and instruct the assistant to lift the testicle upwards while you completely dissolve the adhesions behind it.
And search with your fingers to ensure that there is no intestinal matter enveloped in the hard white tissue. But if you find any such matter, then push it back down into the abdomen.
And then incise in the area below the scrotum, through an incision from which blood and pus will flow, as we have recounted in what we have said before.
Then apply wool soaked in oil and place it on the wound; and apply the ligature which we have mentioned (and sometimes the white tissue is cauterized, which we have said is near the incision to prevent bleeding, because this often happens) and leave the ligature until it falls off by itself.
But if its falling off is delayed, then let it be bathed with warm water, and keep it there until it falls off as we have said.
Then treat the wound with the other treatments for wounds until it is healed.
Chapter 66.
Of a ventosity hernia (or Windy Hernia).
In this hernia, I have not seen anyone who has dared to treat it with iron.
And the first ones said that it is done as we have recounted in the hernia that is with wine; which is that you tie the vessels near the incision on that with facility; and then dry the middle area around the abscess with what opens it, until the vessels fall away.
And cure the wound as we have said until it is healed.
Chapter 67.
Of the disruption that is made in the inguinal region.
Sometimes a rupture occurs in the groin as we have said, and it swells the area.
And do not allow the genitals any cause of the intestines in the genitals; and if they do, it is little; and it returns in all hours.  (This is a somewhat awkward translation reflecting the awkwardness of the original.  A better paraphrase might be:  "And don't let the intestines protrude into the genitals; if some do, it's a minor issue, and it will retract on its own.")
But if the time with this is prolonged, a fissure is adjusted in the scrotum until the intestine descends into the scrotum; and this happens because of the extension of the scrotum which is in the groin as we have said, which cause is because the scrotum is extended, and then it is softened and the area is inflamed and swollen.
And its cure is with cautery, as its narration has preceded. (Meaning:  And it is treated with cautery, as described previously.)
And sometimes it is cured with iron according to this manner: that the patient lies on his back between your hands, and then dry the area of the rupture of the swollen abscesses with incision across the width according to the amount of three fingers.
And then tighten the white scrotum that is under the perineum until it is uncovered. (A more natural phrasing might be: "And then pull taut the scrotum under the perineum until it is exposed.")
And then take the ray and place that on the prominent area of the scrotum, and press that to the abdomen.  (The word "ray" here likely refers to a surgical instrument.  A more accurate translation might be "...take the instrument and place it...")
And then sew the two prominent places on the extremity/end of the scrotum's ray (instrument), and join one of those to the other with a suture.
And then remove the end of the instrument, and do not incise the scrotum completely, nor extend the wound, nor do anything else like I will let you know in the treatment of intestinal hernia.
And then treat that with the treatment for wounds, and as you incise the fibers, cleanse it and restore the wound until it is consolidated; because the scrotum is constricted and the prominence of the rupture is not adjusted.
And cautery is better in this place, because it is closer to health. (This implies cautery promotes healing in this specific location).
Chapter 68.
Concerning the softening of the swelling of the testicles.
Many times the swelling of the testicles in some men is softened because the flesh that is between them is softened, and its appearance is spoiled.
Then it is necessary for the one who is charged with his cure to make the patient lie on his back, and to incise all the swelling which is softened over the natural swelling; and then that he join the two lips with a suture.
And if you wish, sew in the beginning the superfluity of the softened swelling with three or four stitches and with a firm suture.
And then incise between the sutures; and then care for it with the treatment of other wounds until it is healed and the threads fall out.
Chapter 69.
Of castration.
Know that castration is forbidden in our law, but because of necessity I am compelled to mention it in this book; and its mention is necessary only in two ways, one of which is so that the physician, when questioned about it, knows its treatment should it occur; and the other way is that we often have need of the castration of other animals for our benefit, such as horses, goats, pigs, and similar animals.
And therefore I say that castration is done in two ways: either by abrasion, or by incision.
But in that which is done by abrasion, the method of operation is that we cause the animal to be bathed in warm water until its genitals are softened and relaxed and hang down. Then grasp them with your hand until they are resolved and do not appear beneath the touch.
But in castration done by incision, it is necessary that you hold the animal and press the cord of its testicles with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament, and incise over each testicle with a single incision until both testicles are exposed.
Then incise them after you have flayed each one. And do not leave on those of the cyphus any cause, but only the subtle cyphus which is over the vessels.
And this manner of castration is better which is done by crushing, because forceful crushing leaves little cause of the testicles by which the animal desires copulation.
And then care for the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
On the treatment of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men is according to two manners: one is that which appears as if there were a space in the scrotum, in that which is between two testicles, a shape like a woman's vulva in which there is hair.
And sometimes urine flows from that which is in the scrotum.
But in women, there is one type: and it is that the vulva of the woman above the pubis is like two testicles of a man, somewhat smaller, externally similar to a man's prepuce and two testicles.
And the cure is of three types: two for men and one type for women. And it is necessary that you incise those fleshy growths until the tumor is removed, and then that you treat them with the treatment for other wounds.
And in the second type in men, from which urine flows that is in the scrotum, there is no cure or operation for it.
Chapter 21.
Concerning swelling and fleshy growths due to causes in women.
A lentigo (freckle or a similar skin growth) is easily formed from natural causes until it is irritated and becomes noticeable; and sometimes it is enlarged in some women to such an extent that it is spread like in men and extends to the genitals.
Then you should hold the excess of the lentigo with your hand or with forceps and incise it.
And do not overdo the incision, especially near the root, so that a flow of blood does not result.
And then treat it with wound care until it is healed.
Excess flesh (a fleshy growth) is flesh that grows in the womb of the mother until it fills it, and it quickly protrudes outwardly resembling a tail (and for this reason some first called this "tailed disease"); therefore, we must incise it as we incise a lentigo, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 72.
On the treatment of gout.
Gout is when the vulva of the woman is not perforated, or the opening is small. And this is either natural or accidental.
Accidental [gout] is due to a preceding illness, and that is caused either by improperly formed flesh or by a subtle or thick membrane.
And it is formed either in the front of the mother, or in her sides, or in her upper part, or in her lower part, and it prohibits conception and pregnancy and childbirth, and strongly inhibits menstruation.
And the diagnosis of this is made by observing the womb; if the obstruction is apparent, [it is] profound; but if it is not apparent, then you seek it with your fingers or with a probe.
But if the obstruction is due to a thin membrane, and it is close to the two labia, then you cauterize and break it up.
And you should place powders on the two labia, and then place your index fingers of both hands together.
And the woman should be on her back, her thighs spread, and then spread the two labia forcefully until that thin membrane is broken and the obstruction is opened.
Then take wool and soak it in oil and place it on the area, and keep it moist throughout the day so that the incised area does not close.
But if the membrane is thick and dense, then you should incise it with a broad spatula or with a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is due to fleshy growth, then lift it with a hook, and incise it.
And have with you medicines that stop the flow of blood without biting, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and enses (possibly referring to a type of plant or herb), mixed with egg white.
Then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not close prematurely.
And keep it there for some days and apply a poultice of dry herbs.
And then it should be treated with the other healing method for consolidation until it is healed.
And sometimes it happens in the mother's birth of other flesh (likely referring to a difficult birth or a complication), and then it is necessary to incise according to this manner. But if this is due to a cranial abscess,  the cranial abscess which is in the mother should not be treated with iron in any way.
Chapter 73.
Concerning the treatment of hemorrhoids and of a boil or red tumor that occurs in the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids are a swelling of the orifices of many veins, from which much blood continuously flows; and when hemorrhoids are old, they become boils (or abscesses).
And I have already mentioned in the division the types of these and their signs, and I will say thus which ones receive treatment and which ones do not receive treatment.
Therefore I say that hemorrhoids and boils, when they are in the womb of the mother and do not appear externally, then in those cases no treatment should be done with iron (i.e., cautery or surgical instruments).
But those which are at the orifice of the womb, upon which sensation is felt, are those that are treated.
And it is necessary that you place the woman in a cool place and then stretch the boil with forceps, or with a rough cloth, and incise it at its roots.
And then sprinkle on those wounds the flow of blood with one of the incisive powders without biting [i.e., without causing further damage], such as acacia and similar things.
And then bathe her feet for an hour, and then place them in cold water.
And if the flow of blood persists, then make her sit in a decoction of sumat (possibly a type of herb) and of pomegranate bark and of  woad and similar things.
And then take barley flour and mix it with honey and sour wine and make a plaster with this and apply it to his back.
And if the blood is removed, it is good; and if not, apply two cupping glasses, to the breasts without scarification.
And then take wool and soak it in coriander juice or in comfrey juice or in henbane juice with red wine and apply it to the area; and then treat the area with ointment until it is healed.
And treat that until it is healed.
Chapter 74.
Of the perforation of the bladder accidentally occurring in the mother.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the mother, such as cancer, and stony abscesses, and ulcerations, and buboes, and erysipelas, and fistulas, hemorrhoids, and fissures, and boils, and other abscesses. And I have already discussed these illnesses, and their types, and their symptoms, and their treatments, in the division (or section).
And it is fitting that I should speak in this treatise of a hot abscess which occurs in the mother when it is one of the abscesses that collects pus; and if you wish to know in what manner its operation is performed with iron (a cautery).
Then you must consider if the pain of the abscess at its beginning is acute with throbbing, phlegm, and fever, and sometimes redness, and if you feel it, then do not attempt its perforation, but treat it with what aids maturation until the acuteness of the symptoms subsides.
And then you must make the woman sit on a chair that has two seats; and she should recline on her back and adjust her feet to the part just below her abdomen, and her thighs should be spread, and she should place her arms under her knees, and tie each with a suitable ligature.
And then the midwife follows from the right side and provides the instrument with which the orifice of the womb is opened, the form of which will come in those things that are done next.
And it is necessary, when you wish to apply this instrument, that you place the point of the instrument on the cervix of the woman's womb, and that what is entered into the womb by the instrument should not exceed the cervix of the womb and hurt the patient. But if the instrument is larger than the cervix of the womb, then it is necessary that you place powders on the texture of the cervix of the womb so that the entry of any instrument into the cervix of the womb is prevented.
And it is necessary that you place the turning mechanism on the instrument from the upper side and that the person administering it hold the instrument and turn the turning mechanism until the orifice of the womb is opened.
And when the wound appears, and it is touched with the hand and is supple and moist, then it is necessary that you dry that wound with a linen cloth.
And when the pus is evacuated, then it is necessary that you place in the wound a wick soaked in rose oil or green oil in which there is some astringency; and place the compress on the section in the cervix of the womb.
And place outside the womb and over the vulva clean wool soaked in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And then let it be treated for about two days with an ointment which is suitable for this until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and the wound are washed in such a way that it is rinsed with water and honey, and then with water in which the root of lily or of birthwort has been boiled, and then returns to the ointment.
And if the wound is deep inside the womb, then you must be prohibited from treating it with iron,  instead treating it with what we have said in the previous section.
Chapter 75.
To teach midwives in what manner live embryos should be treated when they are expelled in an unnatural way.
It is necessary that the midwife know at the beginning the nature of a natural childbirth by its signs, and it is that when you see the woman straining in the lower parts and desiring to expel air, and on top of this she is relieved by what is above her from the birth, and hastens the expulsion of the embryo, then know that this birth is according to the natural form, and it is the expulsion with its head first, and the afterbirth with it, or suspended or hanging from the umbilicus.
Therefore, when you see those signs, then it is necessary that her belly be compressed so that the embryo descends easily because when it descends head first, the afterbirth descends with it, and it is cleaned of those superfluities with a good cleansing.
But that which is otherwise is an unnatural and unfavorable birth, for when the embryo is expelled feet first and its body is expelled before its head and feet, and one of its hands, or its foot, or its head and hand, or its belly, is expelled, and quickly it is turned upon its neck, and similar to these unfavorable presentations.
Therefore it is necessary for the midwife to be wise and skillful in all these matters, and to guard herself against error and deception.
And I am showing all the forms of these, and what skill is needed for them so that it may be understood and acted upon accordingly.
When the fetus is expelled head first by natural exertion, and this is strenuous for the woman and the birth is difficult for her, and sometimes her strength is already weakened, then have her sit up, or have the women support her, and evaporate her genitals with cooked fenugreek and warm oils.
Then the midwife inserts a small, flat spatula between her labia and with it she breaks the secundines (afterbirth), or scrapes it with her fingernail until the moisture within it is removed. And press the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
But if it does not descend, then the woman should be given a clyster of mallow and fenugreek mucilage with honey.
Then order her, after the clyster, to be compressed and make her sneeze with a sternutatory; and hold her mouth and nose for an hour, for the fetus will soon be expelled.
And if the two hands of the fetus are expelled at the beginning, then you must gently reduce them little by little. But if it does not obey the manipulation, then place the woman on a chair and raise her legs, and then shake the chair on the ground; and the woman should be held so that she does not fall from the shaking.
And if the hands are not returned inside and the fetus dies, then incise them and extract the remaining part of the fetus, or tie the body to its hand with a cloth and extract it evenly, because that will be expelled.
When the fetus is presented feet first, then you must push it upwards, and then gently turn the fetus, and then grasp it, and then take one of its feet and gently extend it.
But when it has entered the birth canal, then you order her to strain, and make her sneeze forcefully, for it will be expelled if God wills it.
And if it is not expelled by what we have recommended, then you gently return the fetus until you place it in its natural position, for then it will be expelled with ease.
And if it is prevented by all that we have recommended, then take mallow and dissolved gum mucilage, and thoroughly pound everything in a mortar, and then apply this to the woman's vulva, and the lowest part of her abdomen. And then make her sit in lukewarm water, until it reaches her hypochondria (the region of the body below the ribs).
And when you see that the lower parts are softened, then give her a fumigation of myrtle and apply it to her. And when she has had the fumigation for an hour, then make her sit on a chair, and then make her sneeze and hold her mouth, and lift her up, and press the lower part of her abdomen with gentle compression, for the fetus will then be expelled immediately.
Thus of the fetus, laterally, holding one of its hands. Let it be managed by reduction of her hand; but if you cannot reduce it, then raise the woman and make her walk.
And if she cannot walk, then make her lie on the bed, and shake her thighs with a vigorous shaking, and then administer bandages which I have recommended of mucilages.
And if childbirth has arrived, then make her sit on a chair, and then work on the reduction of the hand, and grasp the fetus according to its natural shape, and make the woman sneeze and command her to strain until it is expelled.
Thus of the fetus, upon the neck, its hands spread out, and its face is towards its mother. It is necessary that the midwife hold the hand of the fetus and then turn it little by little, and that this be done easily.
And when it is grasped, then place upon its head that which we have recommended of oil and mucilages.
And then command her to cough little by little, and when you have done this, then make her sit on a chair and command her to lean to the right side, and then command her to strain; and make her sneeze with a [suitable] concoction/mixture. The prepared fetus exits sideways.
When the horror of the mother ceases according to this manner, consider, and then you incise the afterbirth with your fingernail until it is evacuated of its moisture.
And if the fetus is suspended over its face and neck, then you incise it at the navel so that it is not retained and dies.
And then gently push the fetus inwards until you place it as it should be, and tell the woman to strain a few times, and it will be expelled easily in this way, if God wills it.
The exit of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and sometimes three or four, but this is strange and rarely happens; but there are reasons for it in nature, and they do not live.
And count those, even if they are numerous, as one birth, and they are separated by a membrane, distinguishing them through the ligament in the umbilicus of each one. And sometimes they are expelled as if it were one fetus by easy natural expulsion, and are expelled sometimes by less desirable means as we have recounted.
And if some of their births are difficult and come to that which we have said of unnatural presentations, then you treat those as we have recounted.
And if the midwife is skillful and does this with ease and skill, and with patience, and is ingenious according to every presentation, as is prepared for her by the skill leading to health.
A remembrance of that which is formed in the womb from embryos and is aborted.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten are formed in the mother, and it has been verified by me that one woman aborted or expelled six, and another ten, all formed, which cause was determined by the measurement of glorious wise men.
And all this must be in the knowledge of the midwife, because it quickly seems similar to her, and therefore she opposes herself to it with what is necessary.
Chapter 76.
Concerning the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you treat a dead fetus with what we have said in the division of medication and it is not yet expelled, and you are forced to use an operation with your hand, then you must consider if the woman is of sound constitution and is not afflicted with any of the accidents by which loss is feared for her.
Then the woman should be positioned on the bed on her side, with her head inclined downwards and her thighs raised.
And the assistant should hold her from both sides, or she should be tied to the bed so that her body does not extend during the extraction of the fetus.
And then you moisten the mother's vulva with moist oils and with a mucilage of mallow, fenugreek, and linseed.
And then let the midwife anoint her hand with those oils and mucilages, and gently insert her hand into the mother, and then search with her hand for the place where the umbilical cord is attached to the fetus.
And convenient places for this: then you consider if the fetus is positioned to descend head first, and then you grasp the umbilical cord at its eyes, or neck, or mouth, or palate, or under the chin, or at its clavicle, or in the appropriate places near the ribs, or below the hypochondria.
But if the descent is feet first, then at the groin, or in the middle of the back.
And it is necessary to hold the umbilical cord with the right hand, and place its curvature in the space between the fingers of the left hand, and insert the hand gently and grasp the umbilical cord in one of those places which we have mentioned, until you reach the empty cavity (presumably the vagina).
And then place and attach another umbilical cord, or a third if necessary, so that there is equal traction, and it is not extended only along its straight length.
Rather, it should be pulled evenly from all sides, so that its extraction is eased, as is done in the extraction of a tooth.
And it is necessary in this that which is between them (presumably the birth canal) that the extension be softened; and if any part is retained, then it is necessary that the midwife lubricate some of her fingers with oil and skillfully insert them and loosen that which is retained.
Therefore, if the part of the fetus is expelled, then the umbilical cords should be moved to a higher position; and do this until the entire fetus is expelled.
But if one hand is delivered before the other, and it is not possible to reduce it because of its constriction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it; and then extract it until all is expelled, and incise it either at the joint of the hand or at the elbow.
And thus you should do with the other hand and with both feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or a constriction occurs during its expulsion, or there is accumulated water on its head, then it is necessary to insert a pointed, spatulate instrument between the fingers and perforate the head and extract the water, or to uncover it with an instrument which is named "alundat" (a word that seems to be specific to the text), the form of which will be shown with the forms of other instruments in the chapter which is near this one.
And similarly proceed if the fetus has a naturally large head: and then it is necessary to incise it with a scalpel, or to grasp it with the alunda as we have said; and then extract the bones with forceps.
And if the head is expelled but constricted by the clavicles (furcula), then let it be incised so that the fluid which is in the feet may be poured out.
But if it is not complicated, nor does it yield to extraction, then you should incise the head immediately as much as is possible for you.
And if the abdomen of the [fetus/patient, unclear from the context] is abscessed or dropsical, then it is necessary that you perforate it so that the fluid that is within it drains out.
But if the fetus is positioned on its feet, then its extraction is easy, and its guidance to the mother's birth canal is easy.
But if it is constricted around the abdomen and feet, then you must extract it with a cloth that you will have wrapped around your hand, and that you incise the abdomen and feet until what is in them flows out.
But if the other limbs are obstructed and the head is turned, then the midwife inserts her left hand; but if the mother's birth canal is open, then she inserts her hand into the vagina, and with it seeks the head, and draws it with her fingers towards the mother's birth canal, and then inserts into it one or two hooks, and pulls it out with them.
And if the mother's birth canal is constricted by a hot abscess which harms her, then it must not be disturbed; instead, it is necessary to administer a perfusion of humid, oily substances. And administer plasters; and bathe the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the fetus is on its side, then if possible, it should be turned: and what we have said should be administered in the case of a live fetus.
And if that is not possible, then let it be cut into pieces, and extracted.
And it is necessary that you leave no part of the afterbirth inside.
And when you have completed all your work, then administer what we have said concerning the treatment of hot abscesses which may befall the mother.
And if a flow of blood occurs, then bathe in styptic waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have seen a woman in whose womb was a dead fetus, and soon after that, she became pregnant again, and soon after that, the fetus died also. It happened that for a long time there was an abscess in his navel; and it swelled until it burst open, and began to discharge pus.
And I was called to his care, and I treated him for a long time, and the wound did not heal.
Then I applied plasters of strong attraction to him, and I extracted a bone from him; and after a few days, I extracted another bone; and I was very surprised, since there should be no bone in the belly.
And then I considered that they were bones from the dead fetus.
Then I cleansed the wound, and extracted many bones from him, the woman being in her menses, and she lived in this way for a long time,  a little pus still draining from him.
And I did not consider this to be strange, but because in this there is science and help, so that the skilled physician with his hand administers the cure.
Of the forms of the instruments that are necessary to extract the fetus.
Form of incision by which the mother is opened:
This is the form of a tourniquet in which the limbs are bound, to which binding there are two incisions at the two ends of two poles. However, this incision should be more subtle than a constricting [instrument], and be made of ivory or boxwood.
And let the width of each piece of wood be about two fingers, and the thickness of each about one finger, and the length of each is a palm and a half; and in the middle space between the two pieces of wood there are two fittings of the same wood which are already fixed, of which the length is half a palm and a little more, and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mouth of the mother so that she is opened with them when you turn the two prescribed poles.
Another form similar to that one, but more subtle and lighter:
Let it be made of ivory or boxwood, according to the likeness of forceps. However, at its extremity there are two additions like those you see, and the length of these additions is about one palm, and the width of each is two fingers.
And when you want to open the mother with those, then you make the woman lie on the bed with her legs hanging down, open what is between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument, just as you do with scissors, evenly according to the amount you want to open the mouth of the womb until the midwife does what you want.
The form of vertigo which the ancients called:
The form of the instrument with which the fetus is expelled:
The form of the instrument with which the large head is held back (or restrained).  The "2" likely indicates a variation or a second type of instrument.
And it resembles forceps which have teeth at the end, like a saw, and sometimes it is made long like forceps according to this form, like a saw, which has teeth like the teeth of a saw with which it is incised and held back (or restrained). The form of a small instrument:
The form of a hook having a spine (or point).
The form of a hook having two spines (or points).
The form of a spatula having two ends with which the head is severed.
The form of two wide spatulas.
All these instruments are exercised, and when the types of these are more multiplied and prepared by the artisans, then it is easier in its operation and more valued by men because of its quantity (or number).
And do not neglect those instruments, whatever they may be, that you have prepared, for the necessity of those is not excusable.
Of the extraction of the afterbirth.
When the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, then you should order the patient to hold her breath, and then make her sneeze forcefully.  Hold your hand over her mouth and nose, and if it is expelled by this, it is good.
And if not, then take a pot and pierce a hole in the lid, and place herbs in it, opening the mother's mouth; thus with [herbs such as]  calendula, rue, anise, chamomile, dried Arabic gum, cassia wood, and centaury. All these herbs, or some of them, should be submerged in water in the pot, and place that over the fire.
Then place a reed cannula in the mother's mouth, and hold it there so that the vapor reaches the mother's womb.
And then make her sneeze as we have said, and the afterbirth will be expelled quickly.
But if this treatment remains and it is not expelled, then order the midwife to submerge her left hand in sesame oil or mallow poultice, and then insert it into the vagina and search for the afterbirth with it; and when it is found, then take it and extract it little by little. But if it is attached to the womb of the mother, then insert her hand as we have recounted, and when she has found the afterbirth, extract it little by little as instructed, so that the mother does not collapse from the vehement traction. Furthermore, it should be turned to the right and left sides with ease.
And if the mouth of the mother is constricted, we have already recommended treatment with sneezing and medication of the oil with herbs.
But if it is not expelled by all the means we have recommended, then be careful not to tire yourself with repeated attempts at extraction; however, what has come out from her to the mouth of the mother should be tied to the woman's thigh, and then cauterize it with ointment and tetrapharmacon; for that will cause it to rot in a few days and it will dissolve and be expelled.
But when it rots, a foul odor rises to her head and stomach, and this harms the patient; and then fumigations suitable for this should be administered.
But some of the Ancients already proved the efficacy of fumigation with hellebore and dried figs.
Method of instrument with which a woman is fumigated for the retention of menses and the secundines (afterbirth):
Let it be made of glass or brass.
And let the thin end be placed in the vagina and the wide end over the fire, and the fumigation be placed on the embers of the fire.
And let her hold that until that fumigation is consumed and then another.
Concerning the treatment of an imperforate anus in a child.
Many children are born and their anuses are not perforated, which are very narrow and obstructed. Then it is necessary for the midwife to perforate that narrow passage with her finger; and if not, perforate it with a sharp instrument, and be careful not to touch the rectum.
Then place wool soaked in wine and oil on it; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is obstructed, then place a cannula in the opening for several days, and remove it when the child wishes to defecate.
And sometimes anal obstruction occurs due to the consolidation of a wound or abscess; then it is necessary that this consolidation be incised, and then treated with what we have said about the treatment and the lead [likely referring to lead-based ointments].
Concerning the treatment of fistulas that occur below. (This is a heading or title, indicating the start of a new section on the treatment of fistulas.)
The fistulas that occur in the lowest part below are knotty and thick, which develop near the anus externally or in the space between one part [i.e., between parts of the anus].
And it is one fistula or several.
And when that knot is old and discharges a watery white moisture or fine pus, and sometimes some of these fistulas are penetrating.
And those penetrating ones are known because what comes out of them is a discharge and flatulence; and sometimes the patient has a discharge, and worms quickly come out of them.
And some of these fistulas sometimes penetrate into the space [between the anus and genitals], into the bladder, and into the meatus of the penis; and some penetrate into the hip joint and into the buttock.
And what distinguishes a fistula penetrating into the anus from one that is not penetrating, is that you put your finger into the anus, and insert a thin probe of bronze or iron into the fistula when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in it, then insert into it a thin probe of lead or horsehair until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
And if you do not feel it at all, nor does anything come out of the opening, neither discharge nor worms nor flatulence, as we have said, then know that it is non-penetrating, and then attend to its cure.
And when the fistula penetrates into the bladder or into the meatus of the penis, then the sign of it is the incontinence of urine from it and the prohibition against that so that the place with medicine may be consolidated [healed].
But if it penetrates into the joint of the thigh or into the bone and causes pain in the circumference, then the sign of it is that the probe enters there if there is no tortuosity in it into the bone, and causes pain in the circumference of the part; and that from it comes purulence constantly; and that medication is of no use to it, nor is it consolidated with ointment.
And in all those penetrating fistulas there is no certain cure, and the cure of those is the labor and vanity of foolish physicians, upon which they are fixed [insist].
But in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing with iron is hoped for, according to what I will recommend to you, and this which I have already proven.
And it is that the patient lies between your hands on his back and raises his legs up, his thighs inclined towards his belly, and then introduce a lead or bronze probe, if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know the place where the probe penetrates.
And if the patient feels it around the anus, then it is necessary that you introduce your finger into the anus.
And if you feel with your finger the probe already by itself discovered penetrating, besides what you feel between your finger and that suppuration or flesh, then you know certainly that that is penetrating; and then do not work on that, for there is no healing in that, as we have said.
But however, some say that it is healed in some men late.
And of the medication by which help is hoped for him is that you ignite a thin cautery according to the width of the fistula, as is previously done, and introduce that ignited cautery into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat that two or three times until you know that all those fleshy fistula parts resembling the quill of a bird's feather are cauterized.
And then treat that with warm poultices until all those cauterized tissues slough away, and then treat that with medicines that generate and consolidate flesh, and if it is healed, it is good; and if not, then it is not healed at any time by another treatment.
And if the probe enters and does not penetrate beyond your finger which is in the anus, and between that and the probe there is a noticeable thickness of flesh or suppuration, and you see the fistula in that which is the surface of the skin, then dry the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you will see it dry with the probe, and be in the fistula until the incision reaches the place where the end of the probe arrives, and through the probe it is freed and cut.
And then cleanse those fistula tissues which resemble a bird's quill and let no part of them remain.
But if you see a flow of blood come between you and your operation when you cauterize those tissues, then the best and easiest instrument is to cauterize with fire or with a sharp medicine, because cauterization with fire achieves two good effects: that is, cauterization of those affected tissues and staunching of the blood and drying of the humors.
And then treat it with poultices wrapped in linen or crushed flax until the place is free from pus, and then treat the wound with ointments that generate and consolidate firm flesh.
And these are medicines in which there is stypticity and desiccation; when it is thus restored, it is healed and we do not fear recurrence.
But if the fistula penetrates to the rectum and extends from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the rectum and investigate with it.
And so if you feel the fistula and between you and it there is a layer of fat or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then it is not an operation on it, except by way of trust and hope.
Which requires one of three methods: either cauterize it as we have said; or dry it until you reach its cavity so that you can introduce the poultices and the cure is close; and do not proceed much with incision so that you do not cut the muscle containing the rectum, and that this may happen to the patient without their will; and then treat it with what we have said, for it will heal very quickly; or lengthen that layer and clean it of pus after drying it near the anus, as we have said, either with a probe or other instrument with a sharp tip, and then clean those fistulous tissues which are in the incision as far as you can with scraping; and then strive to effect the restoration of the whole wound with incision, and if the upper orifice is not closed, and the fistula remains open in the part of the anus, it will be easier for the patient. And sometimes the fistula is located in this way: when you insert the probe into the fistula, and it is in the side of the anus near the surface of the body with the tail and in the margin of the anus, then take a probe with a perforated end, which is like a needle for preparing skins in this way:
and insert it into that [fistula] wrapped in five threads or around them; and then insert a probe with a thread into the fistula until you reach its cavity.
But if it penetrates into the margin of the anus further inside, then you pull the thread out of that hole so that you can insert your finger into the anus; and pull out the end of the thread and join the two ends together, and tighten each one, and leave it for a day or two, and when you cut the thread into the flesh, tighten it well until those tissues are cut which are between the two ends of the thread and fall away.
And then treat the wound until it is healed and consolidated.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate, in whatever way is prepared for you, except that it is very close to the rectum, because it is not up to you to do that so that you do not cut the muscle; and then treat it until it is healed.
The shape of the pointed probe with which the fistula is opened: its curvature is very sharp and the other part is not sharp, so that you do not cut what is not necessary, as sometimes happens.
On the suturing of hemorrhoids from which blood flows during their incision.
Hemorrhoids are of two types, for they are either between the buttocks similar to coals, or they are like bunches of grapes. And of these there are small and large ones, and blood flows from them constantly, and there is one, and there are many, and they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those that are outside the anus are usually of little moisture, from which flows citrine water or a little blood with continuous flow, and it is according to the complexion of the body.
And the treatment of that which is inside the anus is that you command the patient to sit and strain until the anus protrudes and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you carefully grasp and lift them with hooks or hold them with your fingernail, and then incise them to their roots.
But if the hooks cannot grasp them because of their moisture and softness, then grasp them with rough cloth or cotton or a pledget of wool, and extract them with your fingers, and then incise them and sprinkle on them some hot powders near the incision, so that they act there in place of cautery; or cauterize them as is described in the chapter on cauterization. And then treat them with the other treatments until they are healed.
And if the anus does not readily allow for protrusion, then  stimulate the patient with a mild irritant, and let it be washed with the liquid that is in the anus and that easily permits protrusion when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then their cause is slight, and it is that you grasp them with your fingernail, or that you grasp them with a hook, and incise them, and then treat them with what we have said until they are healed.
And that which you have incised, you must suture as follows: take waxed thread and insert it into a needle and then bring the hemorrhoids together from their upper parts, and make the needle penetrate at the root of the other part, and wrap the two ends of the thread in the lower part of the needle, and this should be laterally; and tighten the hemorrhoid with a strong ligature, and then knot the thread and remove the needle.
And do this to all of them, and leave one of them untied, so that the excess blood may flow from it.
And then place on the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil or cerate; and place that on the anus and instruct the patient to rest. And then leave it until it falls off; and when it has fallen off, treat them with ointments or with other treatment until it is healed.
And since they are old and that medicine is not effective, then you must scrape them with the point of a spatula or with your fingernail until they are moistened and the outer layer above is removed, which prevents their consolidation; and then treat them until they are consolidated.
Then you remove them with more force than the first time until they bleed a lot and are excoriated; and then treat them until they are healed.
Know that a felon, that is, a whitlow, is nothing but a red nodule according to the color of the body, similar to a carnation head, and it is found throughout the body and especially in the parts below the feet and fingers, and because of this pain you must treat it.
And then it is necessary that what is in the circumference of the whitlow be incised, and held with forceps or tweezers, and eradicated from its root; and then treat that.
And if you wish, cauterize it according to what is described in its chapter.
Furthermore, a wart is a small, rough, fistulated, round eminence on the surface of the body, and often appears on the hands of children.
And its cure is that you extend and incise it, or that you bind it tightly with thread or a hair so that it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize that, as we have said, with fire or with a sharp medicine (caustic substance).
Furthermore, an anthill (or possibly a specific type of growth resembling an anthill) is a small, rough or fistulated, large eminence on the surface of the body, spreading widely in the preon (possibly a specific area or the pubic region), and when friction encounters it, it is felt like the bite of an ant, and it is formed around the body (or perhaps "all around the body").
And its cure is that you take a cannula made from a goose quill or a vulture quill, and let it be strong, and place that over the anthill until the circle of the cannula contains its parts; and then rotate your hand with the cannula until you incise what is in the circumference of the anthill, and place that in the depth of the flesh, and so you eradicate the anthill from its root.
And if you wish, do this with a brass or iron cannula, according to this method:
Let the upper part of the cannula be drawn out to a thin point, blunt (that is, non-perforated), wrapped so that the grip on it is eased and its rotation facilitated.
And if you wish, cauterize the anthill as was previously described in the cauterization of athel in the chapter on cautery.
Concerning the instruments that are used to administer a clyster.
Sometimes a clyster is made of silver, or tin, or melted or hammered brass; and sometimes it is made of these instruments, small and large, according to those to whom they are administered, for that which is administered in the treatment of small children should be small; and for those whose buttocks are fragile or painful, the clysters for them should be small.
The form of a large and a small clyster:
The head of its cannula, to which the bag is attached, should be wide in this manner; and let there be a distinction where the bag is tied, as you see.
And the lower extremity, which is inserted into the anus, should be blunt (that is, not open) very smooth, tapering to a point; and on one side of it let there be two holes, and on the other side one hole, and let the size of the hole be according to the thickness of the rays and a little larger.
And let the bag, in which the medicines are placed, be of animal bladder or sheepskin, and let its length be one palm.
And let it be perforated with many holes, as it is rolled up; and let the space between the holes be the thickness of a finger.
Then insert into those holes strong, raw flax threads, about ten of them, or thereabouts; and tie the bag with them; and when the bag is placed in position, tie it tightly at the head of the clyster with two distinct strong ligatures, and then cauterize each hole with it: But the manner of cauterizing with it is that you fill the bag with the medicine, which should be warmed according to the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is neither too hot nor too cold.
Then let the patient lie supine on his back, and raise his legs and place under him a cushion on which are placed men or a linen cloth; and then anoint the end of the clyster with oil or egg white or fenugreek mucilage or the like; and then insert it into the anus easily, and then the attendant should take the bag and squeeze it with both hands and vigorously until all that is in the clyster is evacuated into the bowel; and then remove the clyster and the patient lowers his legs to the ground.
And if the medicine forces him to defecate, hold it as long as possible; and if he sleeps with it all night, it is more beneficial.
But if you cauterize it in cases of diarrhea and the evacuation troubles him, then you repeat the clyster until he is healed.
And if there are hemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the anus, then the clyster should be very fine and soft, of the kind with which infants are clysterized.
On the treatment of wounds.
I have already said in the division on wound treatment what was appropriate to the book, and I say thus the treatment of these with hand and medicines according to their needs.
Therefore I say that wounds are diversified according to what causes the wounds and according to the location where the wounds occur.
And the causes by which wounds are often made are thus:  a stone throw, or an incision from a sword or knife, or a blow with a lance, or with a stick, or with an arrow, or similar things from many causes.
But wounds that are in the locations of the body are such as a wound which falls on the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the belly, or the thigh, and similar to those of the other limbs.
And I am recounting the treatment of some wounds so that you may apply that reasoning and rule to other wounds.
And I will begin with simple head wounds, for the discussion of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore I say that when a simple wound occurs on the head and there is no fracture, then consider: if it is from a stone throw or the like, and it has only wounded the scalp and is a large wound, and you fear the occurrence of a hot abscess in that patient, then you should immediately take care of it and phlebotomize the cephalic vein, and do not delay this; and extract blood from it according to the patient's strength, unless some other cause prohibits phlebotomy, especially if the blood that has flowed from the wound is slight.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side from the wounded part; and guard the patient from repletion and from wine and rich foods.
But if some cause prohibits bloodletting, then let his diet be reduced and let him not approach meat nor wine.
And place on the wound, if a hot abscess occurs in him, a cotton cloth soaked in plain rose oil or in wine in which a styptic has been dissolved.
But if it is certain that a hot abscess will form, then place on the fresh wound its own blood, if the air has not altered it; and the powder which I shall shortly describe.
And if the air has already altered it, then apply some ointment until it is healed.
But if the wound is large, or is caused by a sword or similar instrument, and its edges are not joined by themselves, then join them with sutures as I shall describe in the section on sutures.
But if the wound has exposed the bone and hangs open, and there is no retention of it but only a small suspension, then incise it and then treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh gradually in place of the bone, and instruct the patient to eat foods that are coarse:  heads and cooked vegetables and the like, until it is mended.
And if you find putrefaction in the bone and it does not adhere to the bone, then you shall incise all that which is rotten and remove it, and then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which a flow of blood may occur, and it is not stopped by medicines, then you shall investigate the artery, and if you find it uncut, then you shall cut it with a spatula, or tie it; and if necessity compels you to do what we have said is not appropriate, then you shall cauterize until the bleeding is stopped.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then their cause is slight; therefore you shall sprinkle this powder on them, for it consolidates them before the air changes them.
And the manner of the powder is this: take olive oil, one part, dragon's blood, two parts, and unslaked lime, three parts; let all be finely ground and sifted, and with it let the wound be filled, and let it be bound with a good bandage until it adheres well to it, and is coagulated with the blood.
And then bind it above with powders and leave it, and do not unbind it while it is thus, that it is not suppurated, for the medicine is not removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes this is done only with lime, when you do not have olive oil and dragon's blood at hand.
And do this same operation on large wounds when it is necessary to apply it to them and they are fresh and with their blood, which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, and especially when it is summer time, then this powder is not suitable, but rather treat them with what we have said, that is to say, apply digestive ointments to them, or apply barley flour powder with water and honey until they stop festering; and then treat them with other cures until they are healed.
But if a fracture in the bone occurs with the wound, and it is small, then you extract it with tweezers.
And I will describe the treatment of head fractures in what follows.
Of the wounds of the neck.
But if the wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference in treatment between it and a simple wound of the head.
But if the nerves of the neck or an artery are cut, then if it is a nerve, there is no other remedy than to avoid treating it with something that causes strong astringency, such as vitriol or similar substances, because they harm the nerves and constrict them. And do not apply anything cold to the wound, because the substance of the nerves is cold, and they are connected to the brain, which is the highest of all the members.
And if it is cured with mild remedies, such as washing with fresh water, sometimes infused with rose oil or plain oil, and afterward washing with fresh water and other suitable ointments in this manner, and moist unguents.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures, or draw the edges together, and then treat it with powders until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep or there is pus in the lowest part, then perforate it in the lowest point.
But if an artery is incised in the wound and bleeding results, then separate or cut it, or tie it off, or cauterize it if necessity demands it.
But if the vertebrae of the neck or all of them have been incised, and the veins of the neck have been saved, then you should approximate the edges of the wound with sutures over the cervical spine and do not touch that; first equalize it and restore it to its natural shape, and then bind it with a suitable dressing, and leave it for some days; and then treat it with ointments which have astringent and stimulating properties until it is healed.
But if the wound has damaged the bone, then cauterize it, and if there are bone fragments, extract all those that you can; and what you cannot manage, leave it until the wound suppurates, and is separated and the injury is relieved.
And if the wound is fresh, then sprinkle on it the powder which has been recommended to you. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with that which will be mentioned in its chapter.
If it is a wound from a lance or knife, and you see its depth, then consider further if there is a venting of air from the wound when the patient breathes; then know that the wound is mortal.
And if it is not deep and the wound is fresh with its blood, then you should put powder on it in the first hour, and do not bind it tightly so that the blood is not retained in its depth and returns to the heart and kills the patient, but put attractive ointment on it, and unbind it twice a day.
And if you don't have ointment at hand, then place old linen on the opening of the wound so that it draws out the humidity; and make the patient sleep on the wound so that what is within it flows out.
But if three days or more have passed since the wound and the patient has not suffered spasms, or tremors of the heart, or constriction of the throat, and you see his other conditions are good, then know that the wound is safe; and then treat it with cauterization and other treatments until it is healed.
But if its healing is delayed, and it remains open for a long time, then know that a fistula has formed; therefore, treat it according to its chapter [i.e., according to the instructions given elsewhere in the text for treating fistulas].
And if the wound is from an incision by a sword or knife, and is simple on the surface of the foot or back, then treat it with the previously described suture if it is large, or with powder if it is small.
But if a piece of bone has been broken, and large pieces have been detached from it, then you should enlarge the wound and try to extract those pieces if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then leave them until the wound suppurates, so that their extraction is facilitated.
And for other accidental wounds in other limbs, the treatment is similar to what we have already described.
Concerning wounds of the abdomen.
The indisposition which happens in the belly is either great, or small, or moderate.
And a small rupture is made difficult in another way, and it is so that their reduction is more difficult if you do not take care of their replacement in their place immediately, because it opens and swells, wherefore its entry is made difficult.
Therefore, the moderate rupture is best, because in that one the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is in the two previously mentioned types.
And know that the suturing of the belly is according to four methods, but two sutures are common, which are suitable for suturing other wounds of the body.
And two other sutures are proper to other wounds, but however they are more proper in the suturing of the belly.
And I recount to you each one with exposition and explanation.
Therefore I say that when the wound is small and some part of the intestine has come out through it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then this is in one of two ways: either because of the smallness of the rupture as we have said, or when the intestine has become inflamed by the coldness of the air.
And therefore when this is so, then it is necessary that you warm it in such a way that you submerge a sponge or cloth moistened in lukewarm water alone, or that you boil in water squinant, cyperus, and spikenard, and bathe the intestine with this until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes this is done with something which has astringent properties, and it is better than water alone in resolving the inflammation.
And so when you see the inflammation to be resolved, then you moisten the intestine with water in which mallow and althea have been boiled, for by this the reduction of it is aided with little difficulty.
But if the reduction is hindered despite this treatment, then incise the rupture a little with an instrument with which hemorrhoids are incised.
And the first treatment is better, when it is appropriate that there is no incision, and there is no need to dry due to necessity and weakness.
Of the instrument, this is its form:
One part of it is sharply curved, and the other part is not sharp, and the end is thin, and not as thin as a spatula, rather it should be slightly blunt.
And thus, when the wound is enlarged and the intestine protrudes, then its reduction should follow its natural form, to its proper place, if possible in this way, for it is better.
But when the rupture is large, and is in the lower part of the abdomen, then you should make the patient lie on their back, and raise their legs higher than their head; and if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, then you should raise their head and feet higher than the lower parts.
And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two sides of the abdomen, then always direct your attention and consideration to ensuring that the side where the wound is is higher than the other.
And thus you should administer this in large wounds and in medium-sized wounds.
And in small wounds, place what you have prepared, and then have the assistant gently place their hands between your hands, who will hold the entire rupture with their hand and adjust its lips, and then discover from this to that which might be suitable for the suture, something else nearby.
And this is the common method of suturing: first you take the needle, or a number of needles according to the size of the wound; then leave the extremity of the rupture of the ring finger, and tighten one needle, provided that you do not put thread in the two extremities of the suture, with two extremities of the knot that is under the suture of the inner parts, until you make it penetrate that part; and you have already joined two extremities of the suture and two extremities of the knot, and thus four orders are made.  (Note:  This is a very difficult sentence, the medical terminology and the construction are quite complex.  The translation attempts to convey the meaning, but minor ambiguities may remain due to the archaic and technical language.)
Then tighten with double thread in the circuit of the needle, and sometimes from two sides together, and then join the two lips and the wound with a decent joining; and then leave the width of a finger and place another needle in that space, and then tie it with thread just as you did with the first needle; and do not cease to do this if necessary with the needles until you have completed the entire closure of the wound.
And let the needles be of medium size between thickness and thinness, because thin needles easily injure the flesh; and thick ones are difficult to insert into the suture, and therefore they should be medium in thinness and thickness.
And it is not necessary that the needle be inserted too deeply into the two extremities of the suture, so that the flesh is not lightly injured and the wound does not open before its consolidation.
And the sutures should not be too long, lest the wound be hindered from healing.
Then cut off the ends of the needles so that they do not injure the patient during their sleep; and you will place on the suture powdered herbs from all sides, which will hold the ends of the needles; and leave them until you know that the wound is healed.
And this type of suture with needles is most convenient in small wounds, because one or two needles or thereabouts suffice for their closure.
But the second suture is common: and it is that you stitch together the four extremities of the wound and two extremities of the lip, at once, with a needle on which thread is wrapped equally in thinness and thickness.
Then, when you have penetrated these four extremities with the needle, return the needle to the same point from which you began, so that the drawn thread lies from the highest part to the wound, so that the suture is like the stitching of cloth with which cloths are tightened.
And place between each suture the thickness of the little finger.
And this suture is known to men.
And with such a suture I sewed up the wound which had happened to a man in his belly, which had been wounded with a knife, and the disruption of the wound was more than a palm, and two palms of intestine had come out from the middle of the wound, and the disruption was in the middle of the member; and then I reduced it after the intestine had been outside the wound for 24 hours.
And the wound was consolidated in the space of fifteen days or thereabouts, and I cured him and he is healed; and after that he lived for many years, and remained in good health.
And the other physicians had judged that he would not be healed; and it was by a miracle, because I did not cure him with ointment, for it was in a place where no ointment or any medicine was found, but I put old linen on the wound twice a day and washed it frequently with honey water until it was healed.
But I shall describe two kinds of sutures, properly, according to the account of Paraula. (Note: "Paraula" likely refers to a person or text the author is referencing.)
And it is that you begin with the suture of the cord, and insert the needle from the outside to the inside.
And so when the needle penetrates the cord and that same laceration, according to its width and length across the whole body, leave the end of the thread in which you have inserted the needle; and make the needle penetrate at the other end from the inside to the outside at the other extremity of the wound.
And when you have made that penetration, then you make it penetrate again at that same extremity of the wound from the outside to the inside; and leave the end of the thread that is at this point, and make the needle penetrate at the other extremity from the inside to the outside.
And make the penetration with that [needle] with which you make the penetration in the thread at the extremity of the wound which is on his side until all of it penetrates.
And then begin from that same side and sew that [wound] with the end of the thread on the other side, and extract the needle from the wound which it penetrated, and then return it into that wound and sew the end of the thread which is on the other side with this extremity of the wound; and extract it from the wound which is on his side.
And do this once and again, and do this one time then another time until you sew up everything that is outside according to that example.
And the summary of this manner of suture is that you sew with a suture of skins, with which you sew skins, in such a way that you sew the thread one time from the part of the extremity of the wound, and that you leave it another time until you have completed it.
But the second proper kind of suture, which I call "Gualia," and this is spoken of by him and recounted by him, is that you sew it according to the similarity with which certain medicants sew it, in such a way that you join each part to its corresponding part conveniently for nature; and you apply the end of one to the other end and the end of the wound to the other end, and this is done as I have recounted to you.
So that you place the needle at the extremity of the wound closest to you, on the outer part, and make it penetrate to the inner part in that sole place, and leave two ends of the thread; and then turn the needle and make it penetrate from outside to inside through two ends of the thread.
And then turn them each, and make each penetrate from the inside part to the outside part through two other ends of the wound that are on the opposite side.
And this manner of suture is better than the common, light suture, and it is the suture of which I have previously made mention.
This is the discourse of/on Gualia.
And moreover, some say the suture of the belly is rectified with two needles, that is, that you insert a thread into each of these, and begin with the needle insertion from the side nearest to you, and make it penetrate to the other side according to the suture of those who sew the hearts similarly.
And know that when the rupture is in the middle of the belly, then its suture is more difficult than in other places of the belly.
But the judgment of the medication of a wound is the judgment of other wounds.
The reason is that when you treat a fresh wound with its own blood before the air alters it, and you reduce the intestine and sew it and dress it properly, then place incarnative powder on it.
And if the air has already altered the wound, then apply some ointments that are present to you until they form a hard scab and shed the threads, and are consolidated by cyprus and myrrh; and then treat it as you would treat other wounds until it is healed.
But if you do not have the medicines at hand, then immediately apply, as soon as it begins to suppurate, old linen, and change that twice a day, as you have been taught, until it is healed, for you do not need other medication in many cases when the wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound will spread infection to the principal members, then you should submerge lion wool in oil of good quality, or in rose oil, and place it in the area around the places that are between the armpits and below the shoulders.
And if you sense pain or putrefaction in the intestines, because this often happens, then cauterize it with lukewarm black styptic wine, especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestine and has made a penetrating exit into its cavity.
And know that that which is in the large intestine is of easier healing than that which is in the small intestines.
And it is of difficult healing because the intestine which is called "jejunum" does not receive healing from a wound that falls upon it at all; and this is because of the great multitude of veins that are in it and because of the magnitude of those that are in it, and because of the subtlety of the body of that, and because of the proximity of that to the nature of the nerves.
But if that which proceeds from the wound is the web (likely referring to a membrane or tissue), and you obtain that recently, reduce it according to your method with which you reduce the intestines equally.
But if it has passed to the space and has already become green or black, then bind it with gall over the location that is blackened by it, so that a flow of blood comes, because in the web are veins and arteries.
And then incise that which is beyond that ligament, and place two ends of the gall hanging from the part below the wound outside it, so that the attraction of it towards the falling of the web and the emission of the arm of the wound is relieved for you.
A remembrance/reminder of wounds when they happen in the intestine.
When a rupture occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it is possible that it may be restored in some men, because of this, for I saw one who had been wounded in his belly with a lance thrust and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was old and a fistula had formed, and from it came gesticulation (movement) and flatulence.
Then I pursued his cure, although I was not confident in his healing, but I continued to refine the treatment until it was healed and the place was consolidated (closed).
And I, after I saw the place consolidated, feared for the patient that some accident of illness might befall him in his belly; and no such ill accident happened, and he remained in good condition eating and drinking, and then entered a bath and exercised himself in his movements/actions.
And some experienced people have said that when a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it should be tied in this manner:  that you take ants with large heads; and then adjust the two lips of the wound and place one of those ants, with its mouth open, over the two lips of the wound; then when it seizes upon those and clenches its mouth, cut off its head, for it will adhere and not be dissolved.
And then place another ant next to the first, and do not cease doing this with ants, one next to the other, according to the size of the whole wound.
And then reduce that and sew the wound; and let those attached heads remain in the intestine until the intestine is healed; and let no harm come to the patient.
And it is also possible that the intestine be tied with fine thread which is made from the gut of an animal, attached with it, since it is passed through a needle; and it is that you take the end of these gut threads, and it should be very clean, and then tie its end with its thread, wrapped, and then insert that thread in needles and sew with it the intestine, and reduce it to the disruption/rupture.
And this method of suturing with ants is only a matter of trust and hope.
But if the wound is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, for then it is only a thinning, and not a complete healing of it.
Concerning the treatment of fistula and abscess.
Know that every wound and abscess, when it ages and becomes old, and becomes an ulcer, and is not consolidated and discharges pus constantly and is not excised, then, in whatever limb it may be, is universally called a fistula, and we call it that *azukem* (likely a regional variation or misspelling).
And a fistula, in truth, is a hard, white, knotted formation, without pain, and which has a concavity like the concavity of a bird's claw; and for this reason it is called a knotted ulcer, that is, claw-like.
And in some men it is moist, which discharges pus; and very quickly the moisture is excised in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is thick, and sometimes it is thin, and sometimes it is abundant, and sometimes it is scant.
I have already shown all those causes, and their signs, and their treatment with medicines in the division (section).
And it is fitting that I make known to you the treatment of fistula, which is done by means of surgery with the hand.
And know that fistulas occur in all the members of the body; for there are fistulas that reach the large veins and arteries, or the nerves, or the diaphragm, or the intestine, or the bladder, or one of the ribs, or one of the vertebrae of the back, or one of the joints composed by composition, such as those of the hand and foot; and those which cause damage to the member nearby and similar ones.
Therefore, that one of these fistulas which is according to this manner is of the illnesses of difficult healing, and does not receive treatment, and occupation with it is labor and ignorance.
