And if there is one of the fistulas whose depth does not reach those places that I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its healing, and wish to arrive at knowledge of it, then take a probe of bronze or iron, and if the fistula goes straight, and search for it with it.
But if the fistula is tortuous, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead, for lead, with the lightness of its body, is easily bent along the tortuosity.
And if the fistula has many horrible twists, it is not possible for you to explore it with a probe.
Then, irrigate the orifice through its openings, for the liquid with which you irrigate it will go through the other openings and clean them.
And then inquire diligently, in whatever way it is possible for you to know if there are bones or nerves there, and if the depth of the fistula is far or near, or if it is a fistula which has many openings, and attend to all this with the sum of your strength, with your questioning, with which you question the patient, and the discovery of the pain through your compression with your hand on the place, and similar indications and accidental causes of the abscess.
And when you have considered all these causes with true judgment, then proceed to the cure with confidence: and it is that you consider then if it is a fistula readily apparent and close, or in a safe place, far from a joint, or from a nerve, or from an artery, or from a vein, or from one of the places which I have told you.
Then you dry the fistula as previously described in the narration, and remove what is in that infiltration and of corrupted flesh, and that which has grown in the opening of the flesh, or of skin and similar things, and cure it until it is healed.
But if the fistula is of great depth, and is straight, then it is necessary that you incise it as far as is possible for you, and then clean it of all its corrupted flesh; and then apply cauteries wrapped in sharp medicines, and introduce them into the depth of the fistula, which you have grasped with an instrument.
And do this several times until the sharp medicine corrodes all that is rotten in the depth of the fistula from the corruption.
And then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then you cauterize it as we have recounted.
But if the cause of the fistula is bone and you are certain of this, then you should investigate it and dry it as we have recounted, if you do not prohibit any cause from a vein or nerve or principal member as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed to you and there is some corruption and blackness in it, then you scrape it until all the corruption is removed; and then treat it with something that will cause it to grow flesh until it is healed.
And if it is not healed and throws off pus as it did before, then know that you have not achieved the complete eradication of the corruption; and then uncover it again and meticulously perform the scraping and cleansing of it with your utmost strength, and then restore it.
And so if it is healed, good; and if not, then know that that corruption is in the distant depths of the body, which you have not reached, and then for you in that case there is nothing to do but leave it.
And if the corrupted bone is small, or it is possible for you to remove it, then remove it with fine forceps which are suitable for this.
And if there are many bones, then meticulously perform the extraction of all of them, and of those do not leave any part behind with your strength.
But if some matter of the humors hinders you, and in their incision there is no fear nor error, then incise those ligaments or flesh if they are there, and free the bones.
But if you are not prepared to remove the bones at this time, then place on the wound something that will suppurate the wound, and leave it until it has reached the area of those bones, flesh and ligaments, and watch the wound so that it does not close, and keep constricted the incision which you made in your operation.
Then, if you fear, place on it a cloth dipped in crushed rue with oil, or a cloth dipped in Egyptian ointment, or in any of the green ointments, for when those ligaments suppurate, and the bones are separated from them, the removal and extraction of them is eased.
But if it is a large bone, such as a leg bone or thigh bone or similar, and only its surface is corrupted, then scrape it with a rasp only superficially until that blackness and corruption is removed, and then restore the wound.
But if the corrupted part is large, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to close that and to completely incise it to the place where the corruption has reached, and then treat it until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his thigh, so that you may use this as an example and aid in your operation.
There was a young man, around thirty years of age, who suffered pain in his thigh, and from the inner parts, a swelling arose, with matter forming within the body until the matter reached the thigh; it was the matter of an abscess, a large abscess, and there was no external cause for it.
And then time was extended with that [illness] through the error of the physicians, until the abscess was opened, and much matter flowed from it; and its healing was delayed until the leg was fistulated, and many orifices were made in it, all of which discharged pus and the humors of the body.
And then many physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no improvement in his condition, until I was called.
And I saw his leg and the matter running from those orifices in great quantity; and already the body of that man was completely weakened and his complexion was yellowish.
And then I inserted a probe into one of those orifices and the probe reached the bone.
And then I quickly examined all the orifices and found that they reached to all parts of the leg.
And then I considered and incised one of those orifices until I uncovered a part of the bone, and I found it corrupted, and it was already earthy and rotten and blackened; and a part of the leg was already corrupted, so that it had penetrated to the marrow.
And then I sawed off what was exposed to me and prepared the exposed bone.
And I thought that in the bone there was only that corruption which I cut away and sawed, and I had already eradicated it.
And then I applied the restoration of the wound with consolidating medicine for a long time, and it is not consolidated.
And then I returned and uncovered the bone a second time on top of the first uncovering, and I found continuing corruption in the bone.
And I sawed away what appeared to me of that corruption, and then I was unable to perform its cure and restoration, and it is not restored.
And then I uncovered it again and did not cease to incise the bone little by little, and I forced its restoration, and it was not restored until I incised about a palm's breadth of it, and that I extracted with its marrow, and then I restored that with medicines, and it is lightly consolidated and healed.
And iteration in its operation and section is not necessary except for the disposition of its fragility and for the little tolerance it has and its fear of death, because syncope afflicted it at all hours due to superfluity of evacuation.
And therefore it is healed by complete healing, and nothing is in place of the hard bone flesh; and its disposition in its body is made good and its virtue returned; and it is conserved in its dispositions and did not cause it contrariety in walking nor harm in any matter.
But if the bone is prominent in the place of the flesh already taken away, then it is necessary that you treat that according to this manner: that you take a ligature and bind that at the extremity of that prominent bone, and command someone to extend that upwards; and place that wool ligature thicker than the first ligature; and then tie that upon the flesh which is beneath the bone, and extend the extremity of it so that you draw the flesh to the parts below, and you uncover the flesh upon the place which you want to close so that the closure does not injure the flesh.
And place a piece of wood or a board decently under the bone, for when you do this, the saw is not prohibited from cutting the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be done on the corrupted place little by little, for if the corruption is in the concavity of the bone and does not appear in its appearance to the senses, then force the sawing of it another time.
And if the bone is corrupted and not prominent, in that one part is continuous with the other, and the corruption is in the middle place or in part of it, then you uncover the flesh from all parts completely; and then place wood underneath, and then saw that from the first part where the corruption is until the sawing is continuous to the other part.
And let the sawing be according to the place of the corruption, little by little, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, incise that corrupted joint, and saw the bone where it is continuous with the other part.
And if the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no reason but ingenuity [or skill/cleverness] in it.
And if the corruption is in the breast of the hand or foot, it is very difficult; however, it is necessary that you investigate the corruption in whatever manner appears to you, and that you open that, and clean that according to each disposition which is possible for you, and with each ingenuity it should be rectified for you, when it does not oppose you vein or nerve.
And know that the incisors and saws to incise these bones are many according to the location of the bones, and the preparation of those, and the thickness of those, and the subtlety and the size and the smallness of those, and the hardness and their rarity; therefore it is necessary that you prepare for every kind of operation an instrument convenient to that operation.
And know that this operation signifies to you the kind of instrument of which you have need; when with you is great study and long and knowledgeable experience of the manners of this art, and witness of many illnesses, for then you will already have for yourself that which is convenient in instruments for every illness.
And I am forming for you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments which you will place with yourself, with which you will proceed to your prohibition which you will experiment on others.  (The "prohibition" likely refers to a surgical procedure or treatment.) Form of saw:

Concerning the incision of extremities and the sawing of bones.
Extremities may be affected either by an intrinsic or extrinsic cause. If you have treated that corruption with the appropriate medicines and the treatment is ineffective, or if you see the corruption spreading through the limb without any apparent cause, then you must incise that limb to the extent the corruption reaches, so that the patient escapes death or the greater harm of limb loss.
And the sign of this condition is that the limb turns black as if burned by fire, or the blackness spreads until the decay reaches the limb, threatening the whole body; therefore, treat it with an incision.
Similarly, if the cause of the corruption is the impact of some poisonous creature, such as a sea scorpion, viper, or spider, and similar things.
And if the corruption or injury is in the extremity of a finger, then you should incise the finger and not allow the corruption to spread to the hand.
Similarly, if it affects the hand, then you should incise it at the root of the hand, and not allow the corruption to spread to the forearm.
And if it affects the arm, then incise the arm up to the elbow joint.
And if the corruption passes beyond and you see it around the elbow, then in that case the patient will die; and to administer any other treatment besides this is pointless.
Similarly, with the foot: if the corruption affects a toe, then you should incise it up to one of the ankle bones.
And if it takes hold in the heel of the foot, then you should incise the entire foot. But if it ascends to the knee, then you should incise the leg up to the knee joint.
And if the corruption has already reached the knee, then there is no remedy for him, only dismissal, and to leave the patient to death.
And the manner of incising or cutting the limb is that you should tighten a ligature on the part below which you wish to incise, and tighten another ligature above the location; and let the assistant extend one ligature to the lower parts, and let the assistant extend another ligature above; and you separate the flesh which is between the two ligatures in such a way that all the flesh is exposed; and then it should be incised or cut.
And it is necessary that a linen cloth be placed on all parts so that it does not touch the healthy area, and that the patient receives a proper dressing and a hot poultice.
But if you encounter a flow of blood while operating, then you should carefully cauterize the affected area, or apply some blood-stanching powders; and then return to your treatment until it is completed.
And then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage, and treat it until it is healed.
And I recount to you what happened to a man in his foot, this same accident which I have recounted to you, which is that a blackness occurred in his foot with a burning that resembled a burn from fire.
And that corruption occurred first in his toe before it spread to the whole foot.
And then that man prepared himself, for he saw the cure proceeding to the limb with such vehemence that he was overcome by pain and burning, and he incised that foot up to the joint, and it was healed.
For a long time he suffered that same corruption in the index finger of his hand.
And then he came to me, and I tried to repel that superfluity with what I applied to his hand by means of medicines, namely the purification of his body; and the superfluity was not repelled and began to spread to another finger before it began in the hand.
And he asked me to incise his hand, but I did not want to do that, hoping for the repulsion of that superfluity, and more so fearing lest the incision of his hand prove fatal, because the patient's strength was failing.
And after he departed from me, he returned to his own land, and it was reported to me that that man prepared and incised his whole hand, and he was healed.
And this narrative is included only to be of help in the case of this illness, and to be a sign by which it may be recognized and according to which it may be treated.
Concerning the treatment of sinuses and the nature of clysterization with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in any fleshy member and the space of the abscess is extended until it forms a collection of pus, and then it is broken and perforated, and the pus has come out of it, and the place remains empty, almost as if it were a vessel, and the skin that is over it is like thin cloth, and it has not reached further corruption, and it does not impinge on bone, or nerve, or ligament, it is called a sinus, and not a fistula.
However, if the space is extended to the point that the corruption affects any member, then it is called a fistula and not a sinus.
And its treatment is that you incise that skin entirely as it is swollen, especially when it is thinned and made like cloth, and ensure that it does not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
But if you suspect that the skin adheres and that the corruption has not reached further, and there is a thickening of the skin, then you treat that with a clyster.
And it is that you consider the sinus, and then if it is large, and the arm that is there has a foul odor, then you should clysterize it with an Egyptian medicine which is of oil and sharp wine and resin, taking equal parts, and they should be mixed in a vessel, and boiled over the fire until the medicine becomes thick and reaches the consistency of honey.
Then you take what is necessary from it and dissolve it with water and honey; and clysterize the sinus with that, and tighten its orifice, and leave the medicine inside for two hours.
And then extract that with a syringe; and do this for as many days until the sinus is cleansed and the foul odor is removed.
And sometimes it is clysterized with lye water, when this medicine is not present to you; and it is that you take the ashes of vines, and put water over them; and then filter that and clysterize with it until you are sure that the sinus is clean.
And if the patient does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the lye water, then clysterize it with water and honey and to which crushed resin is added, and clysterize with wine and honey mixed, for it is the property of honey to cleanse and wash, and the wine makes it adhere to the sinus, and especially when the wine has an excess of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this several times and are sure that the sinus is clean, then clysterize it with something that will cause flesh to grow there.
And it is that you take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or that you clysterize it with other medicines and ointments that have the properties which I have already written in the treatise on ointments.
And if the opening of the sinus is narrow, such that the clyster cannula cannot be inserted, then you should widen it little by little with a small instrument, and place in it a small, warm object wrapped in Egyptian ointment or basilicon ointment until it is widened.
And similarly, if the opening is wide, then you should approximate its edges with a suture and leave an opening sufficient for the clyster to enter without addition.
And if the opening from which the pus flows is situated higher than the surrounding area, then drain it at the lowest point so that the pus flows downwards, for when the pus is contained within the depth of the sinus, flesh will not grow there.
And if it is not possible for you to drain the sinus into the lowest point of the surrounding area as you wish, then you should endeavor to prepare the limb with a preparation by which the pus flows lightly as it is possible for you, and that no pus remains within.
But the cloth that is over the opening of the sinus, change that every hour so that what is collected there of the pus may come out. And so that you may know if the pus that flows from it is much or little, or is digested, or is undigested.
And with this, observe the location of that sinus, whether the patient feels pain there or not, and if there is an abscess there.
And as your treatment continues in this manner, and you see that the matter discharged onto the opening of the sinus has already adhered, or tends to adhere, then after three days you should loosen it, and when you loosen the dressing, then change the cloth for another and renew the ointment.
But if near the sinus there is a subtle poison, then do not be surprised by its adherence, for its healing is approaching, for there are some men in whom the generation of flesh in their wounds is slow, and there are those in whom the opposite occurs.
And if undigested pus has flowed from the sinus for many days, then know that the sinus will not adhere; and if the adherence of the sinus is delayed and the cause is prolonged, then you should adjust the drying quality of the medicines, and if the medicines are moist in their substance and in their virtue, they are dry, such as diafinicon ointment, when there is an added superfluity of  zegi in it.
And the best final remedy with which it is cured is that you take diafinicon ointment and dissolve it in rose oil, and let old wine be added to it, equal in substance to the ointment, and mix it well and use this.
And it acts similarly in that case, when there is no pus: diafinicon ointment, honey cooked until thickened, and use this; or take myrrh, aloes, and incense, grind everything and powder it over honey while it is on the fire, then spread this on a cloth and bind it over the sinus; and spread this cooked, thickened honey, and powder it on a cloth and bind it.
And sometimes long birthwort and roots of lily of the valley (and it is lily of the valley) and flour of rye and centaury are administered with it, and use these simples or compounded, grind them, sieve them, and powder them over the honey which has been mentioned; and use this.
And if the sinus is prolonged, and is not healed by this treatment, then know that the corruption is in its depth, and it has already affected the bones or other hard parts or the ligaments; then you treat it with fistula treatment, as described above.
Concerning the treatment of paronychia, and of a worn nail, and of an added incision of the finger, and of a section (of the finger).
Paronychia is an abscess which arises under the nail of the finger of the hand or foot, and easily spreads to other fingers, and as it is prolonged and its treatment is less valued, or it is suppurated from a hot abscess, or it is corrupted, and it throws out pus until it corrodes the root of the nail, and easily corrupts the whole nail, and easily spreads its corruption to the bone until there is a foul odor, and the extremity of the finger becomes swollen, and its color becomes dark.
And therefore, when you have treated it with what we have said in the division and your treatment does not succeed, then it is necessary that you incise with an iron all the superfluous matter that remains of the nail and then cauterize the wound, for a hot cautery is very helpful in this.
But if the bone is healthy and the nail is healthy, and the additions of the nail, already emptied, compress the flesh at the sides, and begin to prick and hurt it, then it is necessary that you place a thin file under the angle of the nail which pricks the flesh, and push it up and incise that flesh easily, and apply to what remains of the flesh corrosive medicines until it is all consumed; and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already penetrated the bone, then it is necessary that you incise that bone and extract it, for a bone that is corrupted will never heal.
And if you see the corrosion and corruption spreading in the finger, then you should incise it at one of the joints, as its cure requires, and then treat it until it is healed.
But if the nail suffers from percussion or abrasion, and there is vehement pain in it, then you should first bleed the patient, and then cut the nail with a sharp instrument with a transverse section from top to bottom, and be careful that the cut does not reach the flesh which is under the nail, for you will cause the patient vehement pain, and it will be a cause for the generation of superfluous flesh in the area; and then treat the area with something that relieves pain.
But a finger, which is born in the hands of some men, is quickly all fleshy, and quickly becomes bony, or quickly becomes a nail.
And some of them are born at the root of a joint, or their growth is in some of the joints of the fingers. But that which is born in some joint of the finger does not hurt, and that which is born in the joints is quickly afflicted.
And of those that are born fleshy, the section is easy, and it is that you incise that below its root with a broad instrument.
But of that which is born at the root of a joint, the cure is difficult; and therefore beware of its excision.
But in that which is born on a finger between the joints, it is necessary that you first incise its flesh with a circular incision down to the bone, and then saw it with one of those saws suitable for this, and then cure it until it is healed.
But consolidation which happens to the fingers between their many joints, whether by the nature of the man who is born thus, or by the consolidation of a wound, or by burning with fire, or similarly, then it is necessary that you separate that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form.
And then place between them cloths soaked in rose oil so that they are not lightly consolidated, and separate them, or place between them a thin lead plate until it is consolidated as is necessary.
And similarly, if consolidation occurs in some fingers with the palm, then you separate that consolidation according to what is prepared and appropriate to the shape of the limb.
Of incisions of vices, that is varices (varicose veins).
Varices are veins, swollen and thick, full of melancholic superfluities which appear in many parts of the body, and most often in the legs, and especially in the legs of travelers, laborers, and burden-bearers.
Then it is necessary that you administer a purging of the body of black bile several times with strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilica vein.
But the cure of them with iron is according to two methods: one is that they be incised and that black blood be drawn out, and the other method is that the vein be raised and entirely extracted.
The incision is done in this manner: first bathe the leg with very hot water until the thick, feculent blood of the leg is resolved.  Then bind the patient's leg tightly from the upper part of the thigh to the area below the knee with a bandage. Then incise the vein in one, two, or three places with a wide incision, and then draw the black blood upwards with your hand from the lower part of the leg to the upper, and from the upper to the lower, until you have drawn out the quantity you desire and which you consider sufficient, and which will remove the patient's ailment.
And then bind it up, and instruct the patient to abstain from foods that engender black bile.
And repeat the treatment by phlebotomy when the veins are full and this harms the patient.
But their elevation is according to this manner: you who shave the patient's leg, if there is much hair on it, and then place him in a bath and bathe his leg with hot water until it reddens and the veins are distended; or, if a bath is not available, have him engage in strenuous exercise until the limb is warmed.
Then incise the vein in a lengthwise cut along the vein itself, either behind the knee, or at the lowest point below the heel.
And then open the vein with an incision and scrape the vein on all sides until it feels sensitive, and it is by its appearance that you will see that redness turning to blackness.
And then, when you have opened the vein, you will see that white which is like a cord.
Then you place a leech below it so that it is raised and drawn from the vein, and suspend it with a gold or lead weight.
Then make another incision near that incision for three fingers' breadth, and scrape the vein from the area above the vein until it appears.
Then raise it and incise it at the back incision near the heel, and then extract it and raise it until it comes out through the second incision, and then extract it from the incision that is above it.
And do this until you extract it from the incision that is highest or most superior of all the incisions, until all of it is out; and then incise it.
And if it does not obey you to extract and elevate it, then insert a double-pointed needle with strong thread and tie it and extract it, or insert a leech below it and move your hand everywhere until it is drawn out. And be careful that it is not incised; for if it is broken, it is made difficult for you to elevate it much and it has become harmful to the patient because of that damage.
And when it is all removed, then place on the wound site wool soaked in rose wine and un-rose-flavored olive oil, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the vein is constricted, having tortuosity to which there is constriction in parts, and it is not straight as we have said, then it is necessary that you incise it at every part of its tortuosity and at the places of constriction, and then suspend it with hooks until you elevate it completely.
And be careful in your incision that you do not cut the vein and that you do not injure it, for it will be difficult for you to elevate it; and use your prudence in this.
Form of the incision with which the vein is cut:
It is not sharpened like hooks, nor is it of a sharp point so that it does not injure the vein; and it is of thick flexibility, for if it were thin, it would cut the vein by its thinness; thus it has superfluous thickness as we have said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, like in al-Ategi in the land of the Arabs and in the hot and arid lands of India, of little humidity, and in which neither grass nor trees grow.
And very easily it is generated in other places of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from putrefaction that comes from under the skin, just as serpents, worms, scabies, and worms are generated between the skin and the flesh.
And a sign of the beginning of this vein is that a vehement inflammation happens in the leg; and nearby the place is blistered; and then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that blister, which resembles the root of a plant.
And when its extremity appears, then you should wrap around it a piece of lead weighing one to two drachms, and tie it and leave the lead suspended from the leg.
And as many times as you see something of it coming out externally, wrap that in lead and tie it. And if the growth is prolonged, then you incise a part of it and wrap the remainder; and do not incise the root until it has emerged, for if you incise it, it is retracted and enters the flesh, and causes an abscess and putrefaction in the place and a bad ulceration.
Therefore it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it is expelled completely and no part of it remains in the body.
And from this vein there has emerged in some men [a growth] that is five palms and fingers in length, and it has already happened to me that one of twenty palms emerged.
And if it is incised at the time of its treatment, then introduce a probe into the opening and perforate it with a long opening through the body, until all that is in the material is evacuated, and treat the putrefaction of the place for some days with medicines; and then treat it with the treatment for abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, and especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or in the foot, and many orifices come to it, and a branch emerges from each orifice.
And therefore treat it as we have said in the division and with what has been said above.
Concerning the incision above worms generated under the skin, and it is called bovine illness.
This illness is called in some lands near us bovine illness because it often happens to cattle, and it is a small worm which is born between the skin and the flesh, and goes throughout the body, ascending and descending, and is shown by the feeling of its movement from member to member until it breaks the skin and comes out.
And its generation is by the putrefaction of certain humors, just as worms and snakes and earthworms and ascarids happen in the belly.
And it is feared for its malice, for when it goes into the body and is elevated to the head, and arrives at the eye, then very quickly it opens it and enters and destroys the eye, and this happens many times.
Therefore, when you wish to cure this and extract it, then it is not done except by its movement and appearance to the feeling, and then it is necessary that you tighten that which is above it and that which is below it with strong binding and constriction, and then dry above it and extract it.
And if it is deeply embedded in the flesh and you do not find it, then you place a cautery with fire on the place until it dies, and the greatest cause that is feared is its corruption, with which it corrupts the place as we have said.
But if you see it has reached the head near the eye, then tighten above the eyebrow with a strong binding, and then dry above it, and extract it; and it is necessary that the patient use purification of their body with medicines evacuating bad putrid humors, and guard against foods generating putrefaction.
Concerning the incision for the disease nafir.
This illness is called nafir in our land, and it is a pain which comes in some limbs, and then is moved from limb to limb.
And I have already seen that as I will tell you. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I uncovered her arm and saw a small inflammation in the superficial vein of the arm, and after it remained for an hour, I saw that inflammation moving like a worm going upwards, but more lightly than one would expect, as if it were quicksilver when it runs from place to place.
And then the pain left that place and fixed itself in the muscle, and there it remained for about an hour.
And then it ran to the other side until it reached the other arm.
And then I realised that it was moving throughout her body, as I had seen; and I was greatly amazed by its change of location and its speed from limb to limb. And I had seen this before with my own eyes in this manner, however I have seen many who find pain moving from limb to limb. But I did not see it with my eyes as clearly as in this woman.
And I considered that this was so because the woman was old, with a dry body and exposed veins, and in her this shifting windiness was clearly perceptible.
And it is necessary that it does not appear, according to this reverse cure, in those who are delicate and have moist bodies with hidden veins.
And if you want to cure him, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not apparent to your eye as we have said, then you press on that place lightly and dry it until the constricted windiness is expelled, and cauterize the area.
But if you do not see it with your own eyes, then treat it with the expulsion of humors and with things that expel windiness, and drive it away with foul-smelling pills and pills of scammony and the like.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their types, and according to the places in the body where they fall, for their diversity is that some have large points, and small ones, and concave ones, and blunt ones, that is, solid ones; and some that have three angles, and some that have barbs; and some that have feathers.
And of the signs of the brain, when an arrow falls into it and pierces the bone and emerges from the skull which is above the brain, for there happens a sudden vehement pain, and scotoma (blind spot), and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and inflammation and redness of the tongue, and spasm and confusion of the mind and vomiting of bile, and quickly the speech is cut off and the voice is destroyed, and there issues from the place of the wound a white moisture resembling pus, and pus similar to the watery part of the flesh.
And therefore, if these signs appear to you, then abstain from the cure and the extraction of the arrow, unless it is outside.
But the signs of the arrow when it falls into the heart, are that it is near the left nipple, and it feels as if it were stuck in something hard, and not something empty; and quickly the movement of the arrow is similar to the movement of the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, and there is coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and syncope; and therefore know that death approaches without any doubt.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung are thus:  foamy blood issues from the wound, and the vessels that are near the neck are swollen, and the color of the patient is changed, and he breathes with great difficulty, and desires the intake of cool air.
And if the arrow falls into the diaphragm which is in the chest, the pain then is near the small ribs, and the breath is drawn in with great pain and with sighing, and not all the muscles of the limbs are moved.
And if the arrow falls into the stomach, then very quickly some undigested food comes out of the wound.
And if the arrow falls into the abdomen, and is fixed there, some tearing comes from the wound, or the flesh, or the intestine which is ruptured, and there is no hope in its cure without extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow falls into the bladder, and urine comes out, and some matter proceeds from it to the outer parts, and very quickly pain comes upon the patient, then know that it is mortal.
But the other members, such as the face, the neck, and the throat, and the shoulders and the armpits, and the vertebrae of the back, and the clavicle, and the chest, and the leg and similar members, are saved according to the greatest likelihood when the arrow does not find a vein or artery and is not a poisoned arrow.
And I recount this to you, that which I have seen of the nature of these arrows, so that you may signify by this [information] concerning your cure.
The reason is, because in one man an arrow fell into the tear duct of his eye, at the root of the nose, and I extracted it from him on the other side, below the lobe of his ear, and he was healed, and no harm befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which fell into him in the lobe of his eye, below the eyelid, further below; and the arrow was already hidden, and I found only a small extremity of it, with which it adhered to its shaft, and it was a large arrow of the sharp arrows of the Turks, with an iron head, on which there were no barbs.
And the Jew was healed, and no ill accident befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man, which had fallen into his throat; and it was an Arabic arrow, and it had barbs; and then I incised over it between two guides, and it was already submerged in his throat; and then I was subtle with it, and I extracted it, and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man which had fallen into his belly, and I thought that he would die from it; and then, after thirty days had passed and nothing had changed in his condition, I incised over the arrow and I extracted it, and he was healed, and no ill accident befell him.
And I saw another man into whom an arrow fell in his back, and the wound healed over him; and after about seven years, the arrow emerged at the root of the thigh.
And I saw a woman into whom an arrow fell in her belly, and the wound healed within her, and nothing changed in her condition, and the woman found no hindrance in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man into whom an arrow fell in his face, and the wound healed and the arrow broke, finding no great pain there, and many similar to these.
And I extracted the arrow from the prince of the king's man that had fallen into the middle of his nose and had already inclined a little to the right side, and the whole arrow was hidden.
And I was called to his cure about the wounding by the arrow within three days.
And then I found the wound of the arrow very narrow; and then I examined it with a subtle probe, and I did not feel it; but he felt a prickling below his right ear, and then I suspected that this prickling was due to the point of the arrow.
And I plastered the place with a plaster which has attractive and digestive virtue, trusting that the place would suppurate so that the location of the arrow would appear to me, so that I could operate on it.
And no significant accident occurred in the place that the arrow had reached; and I continued with the plaster on him for many days and no accident occurred; and the wound was consolidated meanwhile, and the patient refrained from extraction for many days until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and reported this to me.
And then I applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days until it was opened, and with a probe I examined it, and found the subtle point of the arrow, which adhered to the shaft.
And then I applied to the appearance of the wound that sharp medicine until the point of the arrow appeared to me visibly, and I spent time with this for a period of four months.
And then, since the wound was enlarged, and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it and remove it, and I did not succeed in this way, then I did not cease to subtly devise and contrive concerning it with ingenious methods, until one day I began to take it with ingeniously crafted forceps, according to the form which will be shown at the end of this chapter, until I extracted it; and then I restored the wound.
And some physicians judged that the cartilage of the nose would not be restored, but I restored it and the wound was consolidated, and the patient was healed completely, and I did not harm him in any way.
And I recount to you the manner of extraction of certain arrows so that you may take this as a rule and explanation for what I have not said, for the parts of this art and its definitions are not understood by narration nor do books comprehend them.
And wise artisans compare the little with the much; and that which is present with that which is absent; and they find new operations and instruments because of the descent of strange things from the art when it happens.
Therefore I say that arrows are not removed from the members in which they are fixed except in two ways: either by traction from the place where they enter; or by the opposite side.
And that which is removed from the place where it entered, is an arrow proceeding into a fleshy place, and is pulled and removed.
And if it does not yield to such a method at the time it is found, then it is necessary that you leave it for a few days until the flesh surrounding it softens, and its extraction and removal are eased.
And similarly, if it is fixed in bone and does not yield to you in this way, then leave it for a few days and repeat the attempt with traction and movement each day, and it will eventually be removed.
And if it still does not yield after a few days, then you must perforate the bone around the arrow on all sides with a fine drill until the space around the arrow is widened, and then extract it.
But if the arrow is hidden within the body and is beyond feeling, then you should probe for it with a probe, and if you feel it, then pull it out with instruments suitable for its extraction.
And if you cannot reach it because of the tightness of the wound and the length of the probe, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in the way, then you should enlarge the wound so that it is possible to extract the arrow.
And if it is held by barbs, then you should free the flesh in which it is embedded, by any means you can; and work on this, if you cannot manage the removal of the flesh by breaking the barbs and twisting them until they release.
And when you perform the extraction of the arrow, wherever it may be, then use twisting with your hand and forceps on all parts until it yields; and proceed with great care so that the arrow is not broken and that the extraction is not made difficult for you.
And if you cannot manage it at that time, then leave it for a few days until the parts surrounding it soften, and then try again, for then it will be easier. But if you encounter excessive bleeding, then administer the treatment we described in the appropriate chapter.
And be careful about incising a vein, nerve, or tendon, and use every possible method to remove the arrow; and do this with ease, calmness, slowness, and firmness, as I have recommended to you.
And therefore, counter the arrow so that the patient is positioned according to the trajectory of the arrow's entry, for this is most beneficial. But if that is not possible, then administer what is possible according to the circumstances.
But if arrows are protruding from the opposite side, or if some part of the arrow projects outwards, or if the arrowhead is bent due to the feeling of the part above the deep wound and you see this protrusion; then you should cut around it; and the incision should be the size that the forceps can grasp; and then pull it out, for it will come out easily in this way.
But if it is lodged in the bone, then twist the blade in the opposite direction until the arrowhead is freed from the bone, and widen the opening, and then extract it.
And if not, leave it for a few days, and then try again until it comes out.
And if the shaft of the arrow is still inside, then push it through.
But if the shaft has fallen off and you wish to administer a push, then introduce, if it is blunt, a concave instrument so that the concavity of it fits the tail of the arrow, and then push it through.
But if the arrowhead is concave, then push it through with a strong instrument entering into it, for the arrow will be eased out by it.
And if the arrow is poisoned, then you must remove all the flesh in which the poison is, if it is possible for you to do so, and then treat it with whatever is appropriate.
And if the arrow lodges in the foot, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side, and is close enough that you can touch it with a probe, and it is possible for you to cut it out, then cut it out and be careful not to cut any vein or nerve, and remove it, and then dress the wound if necessary, and treat it until it is healed.
The form of forceps with which arrows are drawn:
Let the ends of them be like a bird's beak, which are made like a saw when they grip the arrow so that they do not let go of it.
And of these, many types are made, large, small, and medium-sized, according to the size of the arrow and its smallness, and the size of the wound, and the constriction of the location.
like a ray, so that the entry into the concave arrow is eased and it is pushed with that.
Of phlebotomy of the veins.
The veins for phlebotomy, of which the customary number to bleed from is thirty, of which sixteen are in the head; that is, two pulsating veins which are near the ears, called alhasisain; two veins which are in the two apparent temples; and two veins which are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes which are called aspicientz; and the vein prepared in the middle of the forehead, and the vein which is at the tip of the nose; and two guidegi which are at the neck; and two veins which are in the upper lip; and two veins which are in the lower lip; and – these are the veins which are called algelic –; and there are two veins which are under the tongue. But the veins which are phlebotomized in the arms are five; for of these there is one cephalic, and it is on the ulnar side; and the common people call it "the vein of the head", and it is a deep vein, and is composed of a branch of the basilica, and of another branch of the cephalic; and the people call that one "the vein of the whole body", and the basilica is the vein which is situated on the radial side, and it is called "citillar", and the vulgar people call that one "the vein of the belly"; and the funis of the arm, and it is situated on the forearm, and it is that in which phlebotomy is performed, and it is that which appears above the elbow by manifest appearance; and the salvatella, and it is the vein which is between the index finger and the little finger, and there are two branches in it.
And in the foot and leg there are three veins; one of these is that which is below the bend of the knee on the ulnar side; and the second is the saphena, and its location is below the leg on the radial side; and the sciatic vein, and its location is below the leg on the ulnar side; and in the leg there are other veins similar to those.
The benefit of opening the veins near the ears is for chronic catarrhs, migraine, headaches, and old, bad head ulcers.
And the method of bleeding those veins, as I recommend to you, is that the patient's head should be shaved, and the patient's head should be rubbed at the occiput in the location of both veins with a wide cloth with good friction; then the patient should tighten their neck with a bandage until the two veins and their locations appear near the ears in two depressed areas of the head; and feel for them with your finger, and if you feel the pulse of those veins with your finger, then bleed them there with a lancet.
Then you take the lancet knife, which is sharp, and you insert it under the vein in the incision until the blood flows. Then raise your hand with the vein and the incision to the upper parts and incise the vein with a sharp incision. And let the length of the incision be the amount of two fingers laid together, or thereabouts; and let out as much blood as you wish.
Then bind it with powders and leave it until it is healed.
And already, at the beginning of the book, the incision of these and their cauterization has been described.
But of the two arterial veins that are at the temples, bloodletting is beneficial for chronic migraine, sudden severe headache, persistent ophthalmia, and a flow of acute superfluities poured into the eyes.
And the way to perform phlebotomy on these is as I have explained to you:  constrict the patient's neck very tightly until the two veins appear visibly to the senses, and their pulse is felt beneath your finger; and then you should bleed with a lancet.
Then you raise the skin above the vein in the upper parts with your index finger, and insert the phlebotomy lancet underneath, and lift the vein upwards; and incise it just as you have done with the other two veins; and let the blood flow according to your needs.
Then loosen the constriction on the patient, and place your finger on the vein for an hour, and then place cotton and powder on it and bind it tightly above, and leave it until it is healed.
And already the remembering of them, and of their incision, and the drawing [of blood] has been preceded in the beginning of the book.
Therefore, the usefulness of phlebotomy of the frontal vein is similar to phlebotomy of the cephalic vein for old facial diseases, and for migraine, stiffness, and foul ulcers.
And the manner of his phlebotomy is as I recounted to you; that is, that the patient constrict his neck with a bandage until the vein appears; and then take the instrument which is named a fossor, of which this is the form:
Place the prominent point which is at the head of the fossor upon that vein and strike it with a hammer or with another similar thing in that manner, and let the blood flow according to the quantity which you wish; and then untie the patient's constriction and bind it until it is healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a lateral phlebotomy, however it is not fitting that the phlebotomy be of acute extremity as are the other phlebotomies, but rather of blunt extremity, and perform that phlebotomy across [the vein], because the bone is near and would easily break there if the phlebotomy were subtle [or thin].
But the usefulness of the two veins that are at the lacrimal ducts of both eyes is for scabies of the eyes, for stiffness and swelling in the eyes, and for diseases of the face.
And the manner of the phlebotomy is that the patient constricts his neck with a bandage, and then phlebotomize those veins, and you should be standing over his head.
And let the phlebotomy be made across rather than lengthwise, a little with a small phlebotomy tool, because in that place there is no flesh; and if the phlebotomy tool is thin, its point will be broken very easily.
And then let the blood flow as much as is necessary for you, and place cotton over it, and bind it tightly for a night, and then unbind it.
But phlebotomy of the vein of the nose is beneficial for acute fever and violent headache, and for diseases of the face and migraine and swelling that occurs in the face, especially when it is chronic.
And the manner of his phlebotomy is that the patient constricts his neck, and then you hold his nose with your left hand, and take a long, thin phlebotomy tool and insert it into the middle of the nasal bridge in a straight line, because the vein is not palpable there, and because the blood begins to flow immediately.
As you slowly withdraw your hand with the small phlebotomy tool, let the blood flow according to your needs; and then bind it for a night, because it is easily restored.
But bloodletting from the two jugular veins helps with the constriction of the throat and the beginning of leprosy and many diseases, impetigo, and bad, corrosive ulcers, as well as herpes.
And the manner of phlebotomy of those veins is that the patient tightens each one on his neck with a ligature; and the practitioner stands over the patient's head, and the patient sits on a stool.
Then phlebotomize the vein lengthwise with a wide phlebotomy tool little by little; and then extract blood according to a moderate quantity or according to what you see is necessary; and then do the same with the other vein.
Then untie the ligature and tighten the two veins with a moderate constriction so that the patient is not suffocated, and leave it until morning because the wound is healed.
And bloodletting from the alveolar veins benefits small abscesses and pustules of the mouth, corruption of the gums, bad ulcers and fissures, and bad ulcers that are in the nose and around it.

And your veins that are phlebotomized in the elbow are those which are found in all men, and their phlebotomy is performed in two ways, either by using a lancet or an olive-shaped fleam with a fine point; or by cutting with a fleam knife, and this is called scarification, and this is its form:
Let its width be like this, and it is suitable for opening concave, full, prominent, thick veins, and those that contain thick, turbid blood.

But the benefit of phlebotomy of the basilic vein, which is one of three veins: because it draws blood from illnesses that are below the throat and neck, and from those that affect the feet and abdomen.

And then if the blood is severed, it is good; and if not, then treat it with that which is preceded in the remembrance of the place of the powders that sever the flow of blood.

But of the dark vein, phlebotomy is helpful that extracts the blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; for this reason, because it is composed of a branch of the basilica and a branch of the cephalic, as we have said.

And this nerve often appears to the feeling; and if it is hidden from some men and is subtle and not shown, then it is necessary that you use the phlebotomy instrument with which you perform phlebotomy with a skillful incision, and avoid the nerve with your skill.

The benefit of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is that it draws blood from the head and helps with eye diseases.

The first of these is that you should know that phlebotomy is administered only for the preservation of health and for the hope of it and caution against the onset of diseases, and that phlebotomy should be done in one of the veins that are in the elbow, that is: cephalic, median cubital, and basilic; and that phlebotomy should be done at the beginning of spring, on an empty stomach in spring and autumn, and in winter near mealtimes, when signs of repletion appear.
And let phlebotomy be done on the third day or on the first, after three hours have passed from the day.
But phlebotomy, which is administered, is more so when it provokes suitability and necessity for it, either by night or by day, at any hour and at any time.
And it is necessary that a child not be phlebotomized until they are fourteen years old, and a pregnant woman should not be phlebotomized except from the fourth month until the seventh, and old women who have passed sixty years should not be phlebotomized.

Furthermore, phlebotomy should be avoided near colic, vomiting, abdominal pain, excessive sexual activity, strenuous labor, excessive exercise, fasting, vigils, and any cause that weakens the body's vital strength, whether corporeal or animal.

And let the constriction be moderate, for if the constriction is not equal, or if the constriction is excessive, then it prevents the flow of blood; or if it is too loose, then it prevents the blood from flowing properly.
Then, near the constriction, the one who is to be phlebotomized should rub their hands together until the veins are swollen and clearly visible.
Then the phlebotomist should lubricate their phlebotomy tool with a little old oil; and then place the index finger of their left hand on the vein, below the spot where they intend to perform the phlebotomy, so that the vein moves slightly, and the percussion is stopped, because those veins that are found like a fleeing string should be avoided in phlebotomy; those that are full of wind and contract under the phlebotomy tool when you place it should be avoided by the phlebotomist; and if the phlebotomy tool does not open the vein, and if it opens, it is narrow.
Therefore, the phlebotomist must be careful and attentive to all these matters.
Then apply the phlebotomy tool; but if the vein is opened on the first attempt, it is good; if not, repeat the procedure a little below that spot, or above it gently, if the area is not abscessed.

Then repeat the phlebotomy if necessary.
And if the opening is small and the blood flows slowly, and you fear that you will not obtain the desired quantity of blood, then repeat the phlebotomy in that same spot, carefully and precisely enlarging the opening slightly, and do this gently before the area becomes abscessed, because in many men the area becomes abscessed with a very small opening.
But if you see that it is already abscessed, then do not repeat the procedure there, for there is no reason; and apply some olive oil to that spot, for it relieves the body of blood. And olive oil is better in this instance than other oils.
Similarly, administer olive oil in all phlebotomies with which you bleed the veins to prevent blood clotting, and this is done by theriac and by *sagzenea* (a likely misspelling or variation of a term for a medicinal substance) when one of these is applied to the area, because the blood is thinned and thickened when it is thick.

And it is necessary to do this immediately after the bleeding from that vein, supplementing it or another vein.
And if some blackness or greenness remains at the site for some days, it is not harmful; and if you wish, apply something made of dissolved aloes and myrrh, or something made of juice of mint or calamint or something similar.

And it is necessary to extract blood from every man according to the quantity of his strength and according to the color prevailing over the blood as it appears.

But whoever wants to draw blood from his body contrary to the part to which it inclines, it is necessary that he be bled on the second or third day.
But that body in which there is much already heated blood, and it is sharp and causes fever, it is necessary that blood be drawn from it at once, and a great quantity be drawn from it, and the opening be enlarged until syncope occurs, provided that you have observed all the conditions of phlebotomy; and that you place your hand on his pulse below the flow of blood, so that death does not occur instead of syncope, for this often happens when the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligent.
And it is not necessary, when you want to untie the arm and evacuate the blood a second time, that the opening of the vein is already closed and the exit of blood is made difficult, that you press on it with vehemence and that the arm be bent slightly, for this is very bad.

Of cupping and the qualities of using them.
Cupping instruments are made of horn, or of brass, or of glass.
And cupping is done in two ways: one of them is with scarification and extraction of blood, and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping that is without scarification is done in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And the cupping instruments which are administered with scarification and bloodletting are applied to fourteen places on the body, of which one place where cupping instruments are placed is called alnocrati, and it is the hinder part of the head; and the middle of it on two parts of the neck together; and cupping instruments on the chin under the jaw below the mouth; and cupping instruments on two shoulder blades; and cupping instruments on the loins, and it is above the end of the hipbone; and cupping instruments on two flanks, and they are on two sides; and cupping instruments on two thighs; and cupping instruments on two buttocks.
And cupping does not extract blood except from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore it does not diminish the strength by it, as is the case with phlebotomy; nor should cupping be done with fire or without fire in any illness caused by repletion until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity calls for cupping for an illness or for a continued [illness], let us administer it at any time, at the beginning of the month and at the end of it, and in the middle of it and at any time whatsoever.
Therefore, when one sees any of these symptoms, and especially if it is in the third or middle part of the month, then we order cupping to be done after two or three hours of the day have passed.
And the benefit of cupping the nape of the neck is that when it is applied, it relieves the heaviness in the head and that which is affecting the eyes; but this should be done after evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in the place of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.
And it is not advisable to use it on someone of cold complexion, or in whom there is a catarrh, for it causes great harm to such a person.
And therefore, it is not advisable to use this on the elderly, and on those whose heads suffer from cold illnesses.
But to one on whom this is being continuously applied, forgetfulness ensues; and therefore you should instruct the cupper to place his hand lightly with the cupping glass over the parts below, to prevent the onset of forgetfulness.
But cupping with alcohol is in the place of phlebotomy of the dark vein (vena fusca) and the basilic vein: and therefore it benefits asthma, and the constriction of the throat, and the splitting of the throat's cartilage, and coughs, and congestion.
And the alcoholic cupping glass should be slightly elevated, for if it is placed too low, it generates weakness in the body and in the stomach.
And cupping with *alhagdahan* (a likely medicinal substance, the exact nature of which is unknown without further context) benefits accidental pains in the head, and ophthalmia, and migraine, and suffocation, and pains in the roots of the teeth; and it is in place of phlebotomy of the basilic vein.
And you should instruct the cupper not to press his hand with the scarification, lest he cut the artery and cause a flow of blood.
And cupping below the chin benefits ulcers in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar oral diseases; and it is in place of phlebotomy of the *aliheric* (likely a specific vein, the precise location of which is unclear without further context) which is in the two lips.
But cupping done over the two shoulder blades benefits *caphatan* (a likely ailment or symptom, again the exact nature requires more context), which is caused by an excess of heat.
And cupping on the two inner parts of the ankles benefits in the same way as phlebotomy of three veins, that is, the basilic, median cubital (fusca - meaning dark in color, a possible reference to its location or appearance), and cephalic veins; because it draws the blood from all those smaller veins which are in the flesh, and those smaller veins from the other larger veins until the attraction reaches the three veins.
And instruct the cupper not to proceed excessively with scarification, because the place is devoid of flesh, and underneath are nerves and arteries.
And there is a cupping glass with which one cups over the buttocks, and it benefits hemorrhoids of the anus and deeper ulcers.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper that it should be a large cupping glass, and that it should be of brass, because the place requires strong suction; and thin cupping glasses are easily broken, and it should be scarified with scarification.
Moreover, cupping of the legs benefits greatly through manifest depletion, because it draws blood from the whole body, and it benefits old pains of the joints and of the womb, and of the bladder; and it provokes menstruation, and benefits swellings and inflammations; and it stands in place of phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins, and of the two jugular veins.
However, they greatly weaken the whole body and cause fainting in many men.
And of the jugular cupping, the benefit is similar to the benefit of cupping the two legs.
And the quality of the placement of the cupping glasses is that you place the cupping glass initially empty and draw by moderate suction, and do not prolong the placement of the cupping glasses, but place them lightly, and remove them carefully, so that the humors come to the place by equal admixture. And do not cease to repeat this and continue until you see the area redden and swell and the redness of blood appears.
And then you scarify and repeat the suction little by little, and consider the condition of the body.
Because that man who is of tender flesh and rare strength, it is fitting that you scarify him once and no more, lest the place be ulcerated.
And command the cupper to enlarge the scarification a little, and moderate the suction with ease.
And if the blood is thick, then it is necessary to scarify twice, for in the first time the way of thinning is opened for the blood and its watery parts. But in the second [scarification], to the thorough examination acquired by the extraction of thick blood.
And if the blood is very foul, then let a third scarification be made so that it achieves the desired effect.
Finally, when we want to extract the blood little by little or a small amount, one scarification is enough for us.
And if we want extraction of much blood, we make many scarifications; and if we know that the blood is thick, then it is necessary that we scarify with a deep scarification.
And the term "scarification" refers to the depth of the incision only.
And if the one whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are tight, the places for cupping should be anointed with lenitive, opening, and resolving ointments; if it is in summer, such as aloe oil, violet oil, sweet almond oil, or poppy seed oil; and if it is in winter, then narcissus oil, lily oil, chamomile oil, or blood oil, and similar ones.
And if the excess is thick and cold, then let the oil be amaranth oil, or balm oil, or aloe oil, or anise oil, and similar ones.
But if the patient has large pores and tender flesh, then the use of oil should be avoided, and for such patients the areas for cupping should be washed with vinegar or cold water of nightshade, or poppy water, or Bordeaux water, and similar things.
And the one whose blood is of much humidity, wash their cupping areas with sour wine, or with myrtle and sumac water, and similar things.
And for one whose superfluities are thick, wash their cupping areas with old wine, or with  self-heal water (or possibly a majorana plant), or with a decoction of anise, or chamomile, and similar things.
And the cupping should be done before a bath;  it should be administered about an hour or two before the bath, and the patient should sleep after the cupping.
What is it that ought to be regulated for the patient undergoing cupping or phlebotomy before cupping?
It is necessary to consider at the beginning: if the one being cupped or bled is choleric and has sharp blood and inflammation, then they should take cooling substances, such as sour pomegranates, and endive in sour wine and lettuce, and succory, and julep, and similar things.
And give them foods such as poultry, and the meat of domesticated birds, and foods seasoned with acids, and the like.
And for one whose complexion is cold, they should drink syrup of dates, or syrup of succory seeds, and sumac, and wine that is moderately sweet between old and new; and it should be recommended to them that they use moderation in drinking and eating, and give them foods such as poultry, hempseed, peas, small birds, and doves.
And it is necessary that on the day of cupping and phlebotomy, their drink be greater than their food.
And it is necessary that at certain hours, some men be given theriaca alpharac, or medicines of musk, that is, nutmeg, or theriac before cupping or before phlebotomy or soon after, so that the principal members are comforted and the blood is thinned; and they should not take it warm.
But the cuppings that are without scarification should be applied to the liver, and over the spleen and gout, and over the navel, in the areas of the knees, and the hip joint, because these members do not tolerate scarification.
And it is not desired for these, but rather the attraction of blood from a member, just as we place a cupping glass on the breast in the illness of a nasal hemorrhage; and we use this so that cold, invisible windiness is dissolved from the member, just as we place it on the belly and navel; for this rarefies the member and warms it and diminishes the pain because it resolves windiness.
And it is placed on boils when they occur with obstruction or swelling, because by the virtue of strong attraction it easily opens the obstruction and removes the swelling from its place.
And similarly it acts when it is placed on the liver and spleen because of windiness enclosed within them.
And these cuppings are administered empty with suction only; and sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of tepid water in illnesses of the spleen.
And this is so that the cupping glass is filled and is larger; that is, with hot water alone or water in which herbs suitable for this purpose have been boiled.
Method of applying a cupping glass with fire: let the width of the opening be two fingers, according to how we have formed it; and let the length of the cup be half a palm; and let it be on the side of the patient, in the place that is in the circumference, half of it, the hole should be small, enough for a needle to enter it.
Let it be made of bronze, copper, or tin, with thick edges, smooth, even, and polished so that it does not injure the limb because of its position on it.
And let there be a transverse bar of bronze or iron in the middle of it, on which a candle with fire is placed.
And now let this cupping glass be larger than the one we have described; and the smaller one smaller than that, according to the illness and the age of the one who uses it.
And the cupping glasses for infants and emaciated individuals are different from the cupping glasses for youths and those of robust builds.
And the manner of placing this cupping glass with fire on the limb is to extend the wick with a decent wood fire before a small wax candle, and place it on the middle of the transverse bar in the middle of the cupping glass, so that the fire is lit around the lower part so that the patient's body is not burned.
And then let it be placed on the patient's limb, and the finger on the hole we have mentioned, until when we hold the cupping glasses and do not need to remove the finger and let the vapor escape through that hole, then the cupping glass will immediately be detached.
And then ignite the wick according to its nature, and repeat if you need to.
But in cupping, which is administered in the illness of pleurisy, with water, in which there is no transverse bar or hole, and it is not administered unless it is filled with water, and it is placed on the limb only.
And this is its form: And this cupping glass, the larger it is, the better it is, because it can receive a large quantity of water.
Chapter 97.
Leech applications are not administered to all members, but only to those members where the application of cupping glasses is not possible because of their smallness, such as the finger and gum and similar; or to a member devoid of flesh, such as a finger and nose and their like.
And the quality of using those is that you should find leeches in clean, fresh waters of a stagnant pool.
And then let them be left day and night in fresh water until they are hungry, and so that no substance remains in their bellies; and then let the body be evacuated beforehand with phlebotomy and cupping; and then rub the member of the patient until it reddens; and then let them be applied to it.
And when they are full, remove them.
And if it is possible to draw blood from the area with a cupping glass, it is more beneficial afterwards.
And if not, then wash the area with vinegar and then with plenty of water; and let it be rubbed and squeezed.
And if the flow of blood is prolonged near the application of the leeches, and it oozes, then you dip a cloth in cold water, and place that on top until the oozing stops.
And if the bleeding is excessive, then sprinkle on it crushed vitriol, or gallnuts and similar styptic substances, until the bleeding stops; or place halves of roasted beans on the area, and leave them until the beans adhere to the place, and the bleeding stops.
And it is necessary, if needed, to iterate the leeches, when it is possible to change them.
And if the leeches are prevented from attaching, then rub the area with fresh blood or prick the area with a needle until blood comes out; and then apply them, for when they sense any blood they immediately attach.
And when you want them to fall off, sprinkle on them a little crushed aloe, or salt, or ash, and they will fall off immediately.
Thus begins the prologue of the third part: Of Restoration. This chapter is often necessary in the art of medicine; and it is the restoration of fractures and accidental dislocations in the bones.
O, son, know that foolish physicians and the common people boast of this chapter, those who never looked at the books of the Ancients or read a single letter from them.
And for this reason, this skill of the learned in our land is kept private; and I have never forgotten this at any time; and I have not acquired this knowledge except through long reading of the books of the Ancients and study of their understanding, until I extracted their science.
And then I added to it experiment and study throughout my life.
And I have already described in this chapter all that my knowledge contains and upon which my experience is based; since I have shortened it for you and freed it from lengthy occupation; and I have shortened it to the point of brevity; and I have shown it to the point of clarity; and I have included in it many forms of instruments that are administered in this, as is done by the addition of explanation, just as I have done in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter One.
Summary and collections concerning bone fractures, of those things which it is necessary to preface before we begin the discussion of broken limbs and the various [types] of each. Therefore, I will say in the prologue of this chapter the summaries of the discourse; and the chapters on the understanding of which the artisan is initially obliged to stand upon the truth of those things; that is, you and those who are ready to know or learn this noble art, others besides you.
And therefore I say that when a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall happens to someone, then you should initially treat it with phlebotomy or purging, or both together, if no cause prohibits it, such as weakness of constitution, or if the one to whom it happened is a child, or an old person in decrepitude, or it is a time of intense heat, or intense cold.
And then let him be content with a diet of cold herbs and the meat of birds and rabbits; and we forbid rich meats and overfilling oneself with food and any food that fills the veins with blood, until you are sure of avoiding a hot abscess, and if you do not fear effusion of matter to the limb.
And then return the patient to his former regime with which he is accustomed.
And so, as the broken bone begins to be restored, then the patient should be fed nutritious foods, very thick and heavy foods in which there is viscosity, such as foods made from wheat cooked with water, and rice, and heads [presumably of grain], and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish, and thick wine and similar things, and with this regime, coagulation of the fracture is made more easily and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in their first youth and in the decrepit, cannot be joined and consolidated according to their nature because of the desiccation of their limbs.
However, nature adjusts and applies to the broken bone from every side some substance similar to glue, in which there is thickness that adheres and binds it until they are completely conjoined and bound together, until they reach the end of strength and firmness, like fire at the beginning, until nothing prevents it from its work.
And for this reason it is necessary that you give the patient foods in which there is weight, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the types of fracture are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, because a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and the fracture of the bone of the foot is different from the fracture of the bone of the arm; and similarly all the other limbs are different from one another.
And I come to the recollection of every type of fracture, explained in its own chapter distinct from the others.
Many diversities of fracture in itself. And the types of bone fracture are diversified in themselves; for there is an arcuate fracture, another that happens in splinters; and sometimes a fracture is made according to the length of the bone: and these are the fractures of the shaft and splinters and additions proceeding and not proceeding, as is done in the quill when it is prepared for writing, and there is fracture with a wound and disruption at the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And to each type belongs a proper instrument or torment in its restoration, as will come its recollection distinct in its places.
Many signs of bone fracture. And by this a bone fracture is known: its crookedness, and the prominence and appearance of it to the touch, and that of it when we compress it with one's hand.
And therefore, when there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor is it made by your touching when you touch the bone with agitation and the patient does not find great pain, then there is no fracture here.
Perhaps there is a crookedness or small fissure, therefore you should not move it with your hand and with complete compression; rather place on it the medicines of which you have recollection appropriate for this moment which suits the place; and it should be held tight by a subtle bandage.
And know that if the place is arched and is not split in two, or another thing that does not happen to it suddenly, as if the whole part is moved from its comparison, then you must begin at once to rectify it before an abscess happens to it.
And if a hot abscess happens to it, then leave it for a few days until the abscess ceases; and then dress it according to any manner that you can of lightness and ingenuity.
And know that the restoration and healing of it is easier than of a bone in which a sudden injury has occurred.
And then tighten it as the recollection of it comes.
But if there is a sudden injury in the bone, then extension of that broken member from two parts is necessary, if it is the hand or foot; or with your hand if the member is small; or with two cords or with one cord and a hand.
And let your position be such that you place the injured limb in a splint according to its natural shape; while the broken parts of the limb are being extended, then it is necessary that you return those additions with ingenuity and in any way that you can to their places.
