And be careful, according to your strength, that no pain or injury comes to the patient from your operation; and endeavor, according to your ability, that you apply one bone to the other, its partner, according to their best shapes.
And it is necessary that at that time you skillfully touch that with your hand, and if you see there some different cause, rectify that and adjust it to the extent of your ability.
And avoid vehement extension and compression, as many foolish physicians do, who many times cause a hot abscess by their operation, or cause contraction in the limb, as we have seen this happen through their operation many times.
And the quality of the binding of the calmed limb is that which I recommend; so that you may know that broken limbs are diversified in their smallness and in their greatness and in their form.
And for those that are small, such as the arm and the finger and the wrist and similar to them, then it is necessary that the bandage be of very fine soft cloth.
But those that are large, such as the thigh or the leg, it is necessary that for them there be a wide and firm bandage, for the wide binding connects the large limb and binds it on all sides with even pressure, so that no blemish enters it.
And then begin, after your equal evacuation, to place a bandage suitable for this, over the place of the fracture with three or four layers depending on what the limb requires; and tighten your hand a little with the bandage.
And then come with that to the uppermost part of the place of the fracture, and tighten less than you did your binding with which you bound the place of the fracture. And then extend yourself with the bandage from the place of the fracture a little, and soften the binding little by little, until you reach from the place a large part.
And then take another bandage and wrap that other time over the broken place with a bandage, and then come with the bandages to the lowermost part of the fracture; and let your action in the tightening of the bandage and in the softening of it be as we have said in the first bandage above.
And then place between the layers of bandage linen or cloth with which the crookedness of the fracture is padded, if there is crookedness in it; and if not, place nothing there.
And then place another bandage over it, and then place over these bandages properly made splints immediately, if in the place there is no inflammation or hot abscess, and then place over it that which dries the abscess and removes the inflammation; and leave that for some days; and then you tighten the splints over it.
And let the splints be of half a smoothed, shaved cane, and well and wisely formed; or let them be of pine wood; or of palm branches; and let the width of each splint be according to this form and this figure:
However, it is necessary that the splint that is placed over the fracture be thicker and a little smoother than the other splints, and the length of the splint according to what is suitable for the limb in size; and then tighten over the splints with another bandage according to your first tightening; and then bind over it with thread decently as we have said of tightening, and that is that your tightening should be greater at the place of the fracture, and the more it is removed from the fracture, the less the tightening.
And it is necessary that the threads be moderate in thickness and thinness, and be of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen by the operation of some fools placing their threads of twisted rope, and it is a great error, when the tightening lacks equality.
And likewise, very fine threads are not suitable, because you will not achieve with those what you want by tightening, and it is not necessary that there be less than one finger's width between splint and splint.
And if the patient is hurt by the ends of the splints near the binding in those places, then place underneath those some carded flax or wool until it no longer causes injury to them.
Sometimes a fracture is accompanied by a wound, and a disruption occurs in the bone, and I will come to a recollection of that in the following chapter according to its particularity.
And know that not every broken limb is bound with splints on the first day; the reason is that, when the limb is large, it is not necessary to place splints on it until about five days or seven or more, according to your certainty of the occurrence of a hot abscess.
And this is the manner of the plasters with which the Ancients made restorations; these were placed on fractures, dislocations, and sprains.
Method of a common plaster, briefly described, for the restoration of fractures, suitable for many constitutions and especially for women and children, because it is of a substance that does not retain heat nor cold: it is that you take some miller's dust, which is the fine flour powder that settles on the walls of the mill from the action of the millstones.
And prepare it in this way, except that you sift it with egg white, and make the composition neither thick nor thin; and then use it.
Another method of a plaster restoring fractures, dislocations, and sprains: take of asafoetida, of ladanum, of acacia, of elecampane, and of wild pomegranate root, of each one drachm; of myrrh and aloes, five drachms; of aloes wood, twenty drachms; all should be crushed and sifted and mixed with  aloechil water or with egg white if the patient's constitution is hot; and then use this plaster, for it is of good composition and restores slightly broken bones, and it suits many people.
Another method of a plaster which is suitable for fractures and sprains: take wild pomegranate root and marshmallow root, ten drachms each; of myrrh and aloes, five drachms each; of acacia, six drachms; of Armenian bole, twenty drachms.
All should be crushed in a mortar with great care, and sifted, and mixed with water or with egg white and applied.
A method for a plaster for joints and bones separated from their places, and if pain occurs in those and in fissures and fractures: let greasy wool be taken and submerged in sour wine and boiled oil and placed on the area.
But in this plaster there is no power of restoration, but it draws out hot abscesses and expels pain effectively.
Another method of a plaster that mends a broken bone: take leaves of the wild fig and leaves of wild poppy; pound everything and make a plaster from them.
A concise method for a plaster administered for the restoration of a bone fracture and when you want resolution of the remaining abscess: take roots of marshmallow, chamomile, and violet flowers, barley flour, of each an equal part; let them all be crushed and mixed with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
But if it is inflamed, then mix it with moist coriander water or with water and use it.
Another method for a plaster more powerful in resolution than the first, when a hard abscess occurs during the restoration of the limb: take roots of marshmallow and linseed, and pomegranate, and melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, of each an equal part; let all be crushed and mixed with willow water or with fresh water or with wine, all this according to the quality of the limb and the restoration of its color.
However, Hippocrates did not say in his book that it should be placed on the broken limb during its restoration, but rather a cerate made of wax and oil and nothing else; and he recommends that it be of medium thickness.
But in Gaul it is seen that they are placed on the broken limb during its restoration, and the ingredients which are drying with a little heat, such as myrrh, incense, and similar to those things that we have mentioned.
Method for a plaster that relieves injury and pain: take millet, linseed, shaved human hair or bird down, and marshmallow, and salt, equal parts; let them be crushed and sifted and mixed, and a plaster made.
But the amount of time the bandage should remain and when it should be loosened is that you consider whether no pain or itching has occurred and the bone is not broken or dislodged; then you do not loosen it for many days. And if vehement pain, itching, or inflammation occurs at the site, then immediately loosen or unbind it, and remove the plaster.
And take linen cloth and a damp sea sponge, and submerge it in lukewarm water, and with that wash the area until the itching ceases and the pain is soothed; then let the limb rest for an hour; and then place over it soiled wool soaked in sour wine and oil or rose oil, and bind it over for a night until you are sure of a hot abscess, and that the inflammation of the limb has subsided, and its pain is calmed.
And then repeat that subtle binding and small plaster and do not tighten it as much as with your first constriction, but make it subtle until it is healed.
And if you see that the abscess, the redness, the pain, the inflammation, and all the symptoms have subsided completely, and you need a plaster and binding, then repeat it as you did at the beginning, equally.
And if none of the conditions we have mentioned occur in the limb, then do not unbind it before three or four or five or seven days; and sometimes all this is left for twenty days, depending on what appears to you upon the loosening of the limb, as we have said, until the fracture adheres, and approaches the coagulation of the flesh upon it.
And then adjust the binding more than in your first binding, and adjust it to the thickening of the patient's flesh, according to the previous recollection of it.
But if you see the fracture site drying and thinning more than it should, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it; and therefore, bathe it with lukewarm water every time you unbind it; and let this be done every three days; and loosen the binding a little, and by this operation nourishment will flow to the limb, and it will heal easily.
But what foolish restorers of a fractured limb do, when it is not set correctly at the beginning, and is set crookedly, is an error according to their procedure, and a great danger; and if it were straight, the Ancients enlarged upon this in their books and worked with this.
And I have not seen anyone of them do this, wherefore it is more reasonable that you do not do this.
Chapter Two: Of fractures when they occur in the head.
The types of fractures are many, and their forms are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for some fractures are made by the blow of a sword, and the blow crushes all the bone into the ciphac (possibly referring to the cancellous bone), which is beneath the bone, just as it does in the shaft, and for this reason this type is called ascial (possibly meaning 'axial'); or the sword cuts some part of the bone, and it cuts the flesh and does not pass the cut to the back of it; and this type of fracture is called levatio absoluta (possibly meaning 'complete elevation' or 'complete separation').
And the wound of these two fractures is either large or small, and of fracture which is contusio (contusion) or atricio (attrition); and its cause is a blow with a blunt object or a fall on a stone, and similar things.
And this fracture is either penetrating, which approaches the panniculus (tissue layer) which is beneath the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the wound of these two fractures is either large or small.
And there is a fracture that is hidden in the bone, as small as a hair's breadth, and for that reason this type is called a capillary fracture.
And there is a fracture that is made either by a fall or by a blow with a stone and the like, which causes some lamina (thin layer) of the bone to enter into the inner parts, and is made in a concave place, just as it happens to the edges of a helmet when it receives a blow and a part of it is driven inwards.
And this happens mostly in the bodies of moist bones, such as the heads of infants.
And in all these types of comminuted fractures, the pieces are like separated stars. And I will return everything to its place with re-alignment (or repositioning).
And we recognize all these types of fractures by palpation of them and by investigation with probes and removal of corrupted flesh that is over them.
And a hairline fracture is known because it is uncovered on the bone, and is wiped clean, and then examined with a probe, because the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of fractures, consider first the patient's symptoms. Then, if you see symptoms that clearly signify danger, such as vomiting of bile, convulsions, loss of consciousness, loss of voice, syncope, acute fever, and swelling and redness of the eyes, and similar symptoms in greater intensity, without a doubt it is not healing.
And if you see no unusual symptoms and you expect recovery, then you begin his treatment.
The reason is that if the injured person has come to you at the beginning of the injury, and this is in the winter days, then it is necessary that you study the setting of the bone before the fourteenth day, according to all the circumstances.
And if it is in the summer days, then you should strive to set the bone before the seventh day, before what is under the bone is corrupted by the panniculus (tissue), and before the symptoms that we have recounted occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the panniculus covering the brain, and is with small splintered parts and attrition, then it is necessary to incise the comminuted and atrophied part, as I shall explain to you.
And so you shave the head of the injured patient, and uncover the bone according to custom and manner as possible according to the shape of the wound, and that it be light on the patient.
And if you encounter, during the uncovering of the bone, a flow of blood or a hot abscess, then you must oppose it with what is necessary.  That is, you fill the place with cloths infused in wine and rose oil, and allow the wound to rest until the abscess is settled, and you are sure of the flow of blood; and then begin the encircling of the bone and its removal.
And this should be according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you incise the bone with a narrow incisor, and this is its form:  And then use another incisor, this one a little wider than that one; and this is its form:
And then use even wider incisors than the second.
And it is necessary that you have a number of different incisors, some of which are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than others, and let them be sharp at the ends, and be of Indian iron or steel.
And use them with ease in percussion on the incisor so that you do not hit the head and injure it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then it is necessary that you perforate its circumference before you use the incisors with the drills, which are called shallow drills.
And they are not called so except because those do not reach the limit of the skull bone that is near it, because the drill has a round end or edge below its sharp head, similar to a small edge or circle, which prevents it from being submerged and passing the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take a large number of these drills, each of which suits the thickness of each bone, until you have present in all parts of the skull a drill whose end is sharp, the length and shortness of which is according to the thickness of that part of the skull.
And this is the form of three types of drills: The method of perforating the circumference of the broken bone is to place the drill on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone is penetrated; and then move the drill to another place, and place them so that the space between each hole is the size of the width of a finger or thereabouts.
And then incise with incisors that which is between each hole in the bone, and make it as thin as you can in order to lighten it, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with some other instrument that you have prepared for this, such as fine pincers and forceps.
And you must take great care that the incisor does not touch any part of the brain.
And so, when you have encircled the bone by perforating it, and it is separated from the brain if it is connected to it, and you have extracted it, you must scrape and smooth its roughness with an instrument similar to an incisor, but it should be finer and more delicate than the other incisors.
And if any small and jagged bone fragments remain, then remove them easily with whatever instruments you have; and then treat the wound with liniments and ointments which we have mentioned.
And another method of operation is easier and less frightening, which Galen and Avicenna praised highly; and this is his description: it is necessary for you at the beginning to begin by uncovering the part of the bone at the location where the fracture is most severe and most evident, until, when you have uncovered that part and have placed beneath it the end of that slow and gentle incisor of which this is the form:
And it is such that the lenticular part is broad and does not cut anything, and the sharp part is divided into two edges lengthwise, as you see; so that the lenticular part is applied to the brain with its face, the sharp edge cutting into the bone.
And then strike the incisor on one side with a small mallet until you cut through all the bone easily as it is rotated, and you are sure that the panniculus (tissue layer) will not suffer any damage that you fear in any way; and even if the medicant were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, even if he were asleep.
Then, if any material attached to the bone remains in the panniculus in some places of the bone, cut it away with the lenticular end of that incisor, and remove it easily, for it is removed without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the panniculus, and that which is separated or removed from the bone is its surface, and there remain roughness and small splinters, then it is necessary to smooth away that roughness and remove those splinters with fine rasps, for which you have prepared a number of different sizes so that you can use those that are most suitable and most appropriate to the place, according to how you proceed with your operation on that broken bone and its shape.
And you must proceed in your rasping of the bone, beginning with the broadest of those rasps, and then gradually use the finer ones, and do not cease to do this continuously until you come to use the finest and most subtle of all those rasps.
But in all other small, hairline fractures and subtle fractures, you must administer care according to how you proceed with their rectification.
And this is a matter that is not hidden from all those who have studied this art. And I stand by what we have written and shown regarding large fractures.
And if the panniculus (skin and subcutaneous tissue) is retracted when you rasp the bone, then you must take a linen cloth according to the size of the wound, and submerge it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound; and then take another doubled or tripled cloth and submerge it in wine and rose oil and place this over the thin cloth, doing this as lightly as possible so as not to burden the scalp; and then apply a broad bandage over this, and do not tighten it except to the extent that it holds the wound only.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are sure of a hot abscess; and then untie it and apply medicines that counteract excessive dryness, such as flaxseed, barley flour,  powdered chickpea and birthwort, and similar things.
And it is necessary that you make a powder of these medicines to be sprinkled on the wound, once they are dry; and universally, apply any medicine whose property is to cleanse and not to bite (i.e., cause further damage).
And take care, according to your skill, that the wound in your treatment remains clean; and that there is no pus or anything else; and do not allow virulence to accumulate in it; for when it is applied to the panniculus (skin and subcutaneous tissue) of the brain, it corrupts it and festers it, and this malignant evil quickly affects the patient.
And it happens in the scalp, as the bone is cleaned, especially when its treatment is neglected, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore, if the patient suffers the consequences we have mentioned, know that it is undoubtedly pernicious.
And if the blackness is not caused by the medicine applied, but is due to the medicine itself causing that blackness, then you should take one part honey and three parts of rose oil, and with these anoint a cloth, and then place that on the scalp; and then treat it with appropriate healing remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Concerning the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except in its two upper parts together, or one of those sometimes, because it has two bones, for the lower part is cartilaginous, and is not broken and suffers only abrasion, twisting and sinuosity.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then you must insert your little finger into his nose, and you should manipulate that fracture inside with your index finger, and with the thumb outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And let this be done by you with ease, and take care that your operation does not cause the patient pain.
And if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it should be set with the end of a thin rod.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the same thing.
And aim to restore it on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then around the seventh or tenth day you should apply the remedy for the inflamed wound.
And then insert into the end of the nose a small roll of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part, or two small wedges, if the fracture is in two parts, and let the wedge be of such a size that it fills the opening in the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers say that the wedges should be soaked in a solution, and changed every day, but I have not seen this; rather, it is necessary that the roll be soaked in egg white mixed with powdered barley; and then leave the wedge in until the bone is set and the cartilage hardened.
And sometimes, instead of a wedge, a goose feather, wrapped in linen cloth, may be inserted into the nose; and this is appropriate if the nasal fracture is severe and the patient is not prevented from breathing.
And this method is not necessary for you; and if you wish, do this, and if you wish, make wedges.
And if another time a hot abscess occurs, apply a plaster to the nose with cerate or cotton soaked in sour wine and rose oil, or with diachylon.
But if a hot abscess does not occur, then you should plaster that area externally with sesame flour and cooked lentil powder mixed with egg white; and then place linen over it, and do not bind the nose with anything.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are comminuted, then it is necessary to make an incision over them and extract them with instruments suitable for this; and then close the incision and treat it with things that promote healing and consolidation, and with plasters suitable for this.
And if a wound occurs inside the nose, then you should treat it with wedges and administer lead cannulas until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4.
Of the restoration of the mandible when it is fractured.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, observe then if the fracture is only external, and is not broken in two and is bent inwards at the broken parts, then its diagnosis is easy.
And it is necessary, therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, that you insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly if the fracture is in the left mandible, then insert the index finger of your right hand; and push with it the hump of the fracture inwards, easily towards the outside parts, with your other hand on the outside of the bone; thus properly setting it.
And if the fracture of the mandible is already separated into two, then it is necessary that you administer extension from two sides according to straightness until its alignment is possible.
And if there is concussion of teeth with disruption, then bind those which you expect will remain with gold or silver wire or silk; and then place over the fractured mandible a cerate, and then place over it a doubled cloth, and place over the cloth a well-made large compress, or a piece of linen equal to the length of the mandible; and then bind that over it according to how the bandage is prepared for you and how the application suits, until it is healed.
And command the patient to use rest and quiet, and give him food of sorbitive lions.  (Note: "sorbitive lions" is likely a mistranslation or archaic term; it probably refers to a type of easily digestible food.)
And if you estimate that the figure is already altered in some manner, then you should examine it and unbind it on the third day; and then rectify what is already altered and plaster it with powdered lily, with egg white, or with starch, after you have removed the cerate, and place wool over the plaster.
And then, when this plaster is attached and adjusted, and the position of the bone is not altered, leave it; do not unbind it until it is healed and strengthens the fracture; for many times this fracture is healed in three weeks.
And if another time a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times in its sedation until it is healed, and the abscess is softened.
And if the fracture is with a wound, then consider if a piece of the bone is separated or splintered; then you should carefully remove those splinters with whatever is suitable for that in the removal of those from the instruments.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you should widen it with a fleam according to your necessity.
And then, when you have removed those splinters, and nothing remains of them, then you should sew the opening of the wound if it is wide; and if not, place on it one of the plasters that is suitable for it and heal that, until it is healed.
Chapter 5.
Concerning the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
Often the clavicle is broken in the front part above the muscle, and this fracture is done in one of three ways.
Because it is either broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without splinters; and it is easy to restore it; or it is so that splinters occur in it, and it is difficult to restore it; or it is so that the fracture is with a wound.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds the aid and aligns the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck towards that part.
Then align the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be, and there is neither little prominence nor concavity in it.
And if you need more extension, then you should place under the armpit of the patient a roll of cloth or wool, and its size should be according to the amount of your need; and extend and elevate the clavicle and compress the roll with your hand until you align the fracture as needed.
And if you cannot pull the end of the clavicle to the outer parts, because it has reached the acromion, then the patient should be supine on his neck, and place a moderately sized cushion on his muscle.
And the assistant presses the muscle downwards until the bone of the clavicle which is on the acromion above is elevated, and then you rectify the fracture and align it with your fingers.
But if you feel splinters of the clavicle and they are bad, then you should make an incision over it and extract those splinters easily.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you make an incision with one of the scalpels which you have prepared for this, after having placed under the clavicle the instrument that serves as a support; and this instrument is of wood or iron, of which this is the form:
And it is similar to a spoon, of which this is the concavity; and let its width be according to your need, according to the size or smallness of the bone.
But its length should be as long as is possible for you in the operation; that it has two ends like this, and one end is wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the wound which you have incised when you extracted the splinter from the bone is wide, and there is a risk of a hot abscess, then you join the two lips of the wound with suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear a hot abscess, then you fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's incision.
And if a hot abscess occurs, then soak a cloth in sour wine and rose oil and wine, and place it on that.
And ensure that the tablet is not moved from over the broken bone; and for this reason it is necessary that you see the patient each day, so that when the ligament is softened, or you see that the tablet has been moved, then you rectify this and tighten the bandage.
And then place the patient's pillow under their neck, and place a small cushion under their testicle at night on top of their pillow; so that with this the support of the limb is elevated, and thus the fracture of the clavicle is elevated by the muscle; or let their arm be tied to their neck.
And do not untie the bandage unless an abscess or swelling occurs at the site, until twelve days have passed.
And then renew the plaster if you have seen its surface adhere to it, and adjust the constriction and restore it, and leave it until the fracture of the clavicle is restored and coagulated.
And it is strengthened and healed, according to the measure, in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the shoulder.
The shoulder blade is broken at its lateral side, and not broken through, but only at the edges.
And so when the place of it is broken — and it is broken in the middle of that place — and is not known except by touch, then, according to what the figure of the fracture is, force the preparation of it and reduce it according to its natural figure in every way that is possible for you.
Then place on the area a powder made from finely ground egg white and lion's wool; then place a dusting of doubled cloth; then place over it a thin linen bandage, according to the size of the entire shoulder blade or a little more.
But if under the bandage there is a concavity in any part of the shoulder blade, then you should fill that concavity with lion's wool, and then tighten the bandage evenly.
Then bind it above with a long bandage with decent constriction and firmness of the bandage until it is not moved from its place.
And examine the bandage each day; and every time that it is loosened, bind it again and tighten it, and adjust the bandage if it has moved from its place.
And have the patient lie on their healthy side.
And the shoulder blade is restored in 20 or 25 days.
And then when this number is complete, then you unbind the bandage, and you should know for certain that this is from the bones of which neither the beginning nor the dissolution is feared.
And if it proceeds from the location of the hip bone and extends below the groin, then you incise over it, and remove it, and proceed as we have said in the fracture of the clavicle for the treatment of a hot abscess if it occurs here.
Chapter 7.
On the restoration of a fractured foot.
The foot is sometimes broken in its middle, and this heals slowly; but its extremities heal much faster because they are more compact and less fragmented.
And among the accidents of a broken mid-foot is that it inclines inwards, causing great pain, difficulty breathing, coughing, strong fever, and vomiting blood; and the cavity in the broken bone is evident and not hidden from sensation.
And its restoration is that the patient should be laid supine on his back, with a cushion placed between his shoulders; and then press on his muscles, and align his bones with hands on both sides, and be meticulous in the operation and adjustment in every way possible and appropriate, until the bone's shape is restored as it should be.
Then apply a plaster and lint, and over that place a splint made of thin willow or hazel wood, or similar light materials, wrapping it in cloth; and be careful in binding it over the broken bone so that it is not moved; and bind it in a circular fashion around the foot sometimes, and tighten it with a suitable constriction; and check the bandage frequently, and tighten it whenever it becomes loose.
And if necessity dictates loosening the bandage because of corrosion, itching, pain, or an abscess forming in the area, then carefully unbind it, remove the plaster, and rectify the problem with appropriate treatment; and then reapply the plaster, if it seems appropriate; and adjust the binding until it is healed.
Chapter 8.
Concerning the restoration of ribs when they are fractured.
Know that a fracture of the ribs only occurs in the thicker parts, that is to say, in the two [middle] sections; and in their extremities, on the front part, a break does not occur, except for the fact that they are cartilaginous. And its diagnosis is not hidden from the senses, but is discovered by questioning the patient.
And the restoration should be such that you align the fracture with your hands, as much as possible, until it is aligned as it should be; and then apply a plaster and bandage the broken bone tightly, if that is needed.
And if the rib fracture is inclined towards the inner parts, then the patient experiences vehement pain and a stabbing sensation, like the stabbing pain that occurs in someone with pleurisy, and this is because the bone pierces the membrane; and the patient experiences difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting a lot of blood; and this is difficult to cure.
And already the Ancients devised many ingenious things for this.
And of them it is said: it is necessary to give the patient foods that cause inflation and flatulence, so that the belly is inflated and distended, and the fracture is pushed outwards.
But we should avoid this so that it does not easily cause a hot abscess, if it does not already exist; and if it already exists, then it helps with that.
And some of them said that a cupping glass should be placed on top and that it should draw with force; and this seems reasonable, but it is feared that the cupping glass will draw superfluities to the place because of the weakness.
And some of them said: the place should be covered with wool that has been submerged in hot oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is filled, so that the bandage is equal when it envelops that roundness; and then the patient should be treated with a treatment for pleurisy, with diet and medicine.
And if a violent cause constricts the patient, which he cannot bear, and the bone pierces the membrane with a sharp puncture, threatening the patient, then it is necessary to cut open the area, uncover the broken rib, and then place on top of it an instrument that preserves the ciphac (presumably a type of bandage or splint), the preparation of which has been previously described, and that we incise the bone easily, and extract it.
And then we should bring together the two edges of the wound, if it is large, with sutures, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs internally, then you should take and dip powders in rose oil and place them on the area, and the patient should be treated with what is appropriate for an internal abscess.
And let him lie supine on the side on which he sleeps until he is healed.
Chapter 9.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the spine and neck.
Sometimes a fracture occurs in the bones of the neck, and then attrition follows, and similarly to the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then consider: for if you see his hands already softened and stupefied and dead, and he cannot move them, nor extend nor clench them, and when you constrain them with your fingers or prick them with a needle and he feels no pain in them, then know that he is not healed, and in most dispositions, it is mortal.
And if he moves them and feels constriction in them with the fingers and puncture, then know that the marrow of the bone is saved and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if the same thing is seen in the vertebrae of the neck and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then consider his feet.
Then if you see that they are already softened, that which happened in the hands happens to them, and also when he lies on his back, he experiences flatulence and defecation without his will, and sometimes he lies supine on his back and wants to urinate and cannot, then know that it is mortal, and then do not labor in his cure.
But if none of these things happen, the condition is better and milder.
And the cure for him is that you should strive for the sedation of the hot abscess, that you place on the affected or rubbed vertebra plain olive oil, or with beaten egg whites; place this on it several times a day until the hot abscess is sedated.
And then apply one of the comforting and stimulating plasters, and bind it on with a bandage; and command the patient to use rest and tranquility and not to sleep on the side where he feels pain until he is healed.
And if there is a separation of the bone from the affected area or any part that has already separated from it, then it is necessary that you make an incision over the bone and remove that bone; and then bring together the two lips of the wound with sutures if it is large; and then treat it with some incarnative ointment, until it is healed.
But if the back of the hip bone is broken, and it is the end of the tailbone, then you must insert the little finger of your left hand into the anus, and reposition the broken bone with your other hand as much as possible by second intention; and then apply a plaster and splint, if you need them, and then bind it.
And if you feel broken fragments there, then you should open it up, remove them, and treat the wound with the treatment described above until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
On the restoration of the hip.
Hip bones are rarely broken, and if they are, the fracture is only a separation at their extremities and they are split lengthwise and lean inwards;  and the patient experiences pain and throbbing in the area, and the leg is numb when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until it is over the fracture, which is its shape.
And if the fracture is only at its extremity, then you align that according to what is suitable for you by equal alignment until it resembles its natural shape.
But if the fracture is lengthwise or leans downwards, then you make the patient lie on their stomach until the restoration of that fracture is prepared by you. And then, when you have aligned it, place a plaster over it, and then place a splint of wood or reed over that, and bind it tightly so that you do not fear a puncture of the fracture or movement of the splint.
And fill the cavity with something that will fill it until it takes the binding equally, and command the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then you cease the extension of it and its restoration until the hot abscess is settled, and place on it that which settles it as has been described before; and then return to its restoration, and bind it as is necessary.
And if it happens that the bones are pierced or comminuted, then you extract them.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured joint.
The joint is that which is between the ribs and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a wooden stick of moderate thickness according to this form:
And let two bandages be tied at its two extremities, and then let it be suspended from a raised location.
And seat the patient on a stool; and then place his broken arm over the stick until it reaches his armpit, in the middle of which the curve of the stick should be attached.
And then suspend some heavy object from it, or let the assistant pull it downwards; and then let the physician hold the fracture together with his hands, until the fracture returns to its proper place.
And the other method is that the patient lies supine on his back, and suspend his arm from his neck with a bandage; and then command two assistants that one of them hold that which is above the fracture with his hands; and the other hold the part below; and each of them pull in his direction.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then you bind above the fracture with a bandage, and above that with another bandage, and each of the two assistants pulls in their respective directions.
And so if the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, then it is necessary that half of the bandage be placed on the armpit and the other below the fracture near the elbow.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary that the bandage be placed on that location and over that elbow.
Swathe that with ease and without trouble, and then when it is swathed as necessary and is applied with a good application, then bind it tightly unless a hot abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then leave the binding until the seventh day, and place on it clean wool soaked in sour wine and rose oil until the abscess subsides, and then you bind it.
And the manner of binding it is that you place a plaster over the fracture, and then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm on that support, and place your open hand over its muscle.
And place the cloth and bind the arm to the support so that the arm is in a steady position, unless some cause of the broken bone prohibits this.
And then use splints.
And it is necessary to place over the fracture that splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the break with the addition of three fingers on each side.
And then bind over the splints with the bandage which I described at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is necessary that your bandage over the place of the fracture be tighter. And the longer the fracture, the less tight the bandage should be.
And so if you see the placement of the splints and the bandage as we have said at the time when you restore the limb to its proper position, then you proceed.
And if you fear a hot abscess, then you leave the bandage and splints in place until the seventh day, as we have said.
And then examine the bandage in all parts for three days, so that no itching or inflammation occurs at the site; or it is forbidden for food to reach the limb due to the tightness of the bandage; and so rectify all this as we have said.
But if you are confident that none of these things will occur, then do not untie the bandage until three days or more.
And let the patient's sleeping position be on their back, and their hands should be on their stomach.
And place on the splint a poultice full of well-carded wool, and check every hour of the night or day that the shape of the broken limb is not loosened or dissolved, or that the bandage is not softened; then rectify it all with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order which we have previously given, so that the food is light at the beginning until the tightening (of the injury/bandage) begins; and then it is fitting that you increase his food.
Because it is customary for the splint and his leg to be bound for forty days; and then it is necessary that they be unbound and a bath be administered and it be medicated with ointments which rectify that (injury).
And if the fracture is badly comminuted (shattered), then you should not unbind the bandage and splints until 10 days or until two months.
Chapter 12.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two focils (forearm bones); of which one is small, and it is that which is next to the little finger, and the other is large and it is that which is placed below the little finger, on the lower side.
And very easily the larger focil (radius) is broken alone, or the smaller one (ulna), and very easily they are broken together.
And when the small focil (ulna) is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is quicker.
And when the focil (ulna) is broken below, then the fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the broken bone is the smaller focil (ulna) above, then it is convenient for the physician, during its restoration, to apply gentle extension easily until it is aligned.
But if the large focil (radius) is what is broken, then it is necessary to apply more forceful extension.
And if both focils (radius and ulna) are broken together, then you must apply the extension much more strongly.
And you must position the hand during its restoration and extension stretched out on a cushion, and the thumb of the hand should be higher than all the other fingers, and the little finger lower than the other fingers, and the patient should be lying on a level surface.
And let there be a cushion in front of him at his height or level, so that the patient does not suffer discomfort.
Then let the assistant extend the arm from the lower part with his hand or with a bandage, and another assistant extend it from above similarly.
Then the physician should manipulate the bone until he reduces it to the best possible position.
And if in the fracture of the bone there are splinters, then you must forcefully reduce all the pieces to their place with your skill.
And if a separated splinter appears that protrudes and you have no hope of restoring it, then you should cut it off and remove it according to the method that we have described above.
But if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, I have already dedicated a special chapter to this, and therefore take care of that.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your treatment, then you should apply a cloth with a cerate made of rose oil and white wax, of moderate consistency, neither too thick nor too thin, and bind the cloth tightly over it until the abscess subsides; then remove the cerate and apply a plaster prepared from pulverized mollusk shell with egg white, and then apply a splint; and the splint that is placed over the fracture should be as long and strong as possible.
And know that the number of splints for the arm varies depending on the major conditions, whether the fracture is in one bone or in two bones together.
Then apply your binding over the fracture site as firmly and tightly as possible, and if the binding is applied to the parts above the fracture or to the parts below it, apply the binding a little more loosely, as was mentioned earlier at the beginning of this chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist cloths, not hard or rough, and the bandage with which it is bound should be of moderate thickness, neither too thin nor too thick, as we have already recommended.
And examine the limb and the bandage after a few days. And if any complication arises requiring correction, such as itching that affects the limb, then you should bathe the limb with warm water until that itching ceases.
And leave the limb unconstrained until it rests, and then reapply the bandage.
And if the fracture is already softened and the bone is reset and appears similar to the others, then correct all this with your skill.
And consider further if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness of the bandage, for then you must loosen it a little and leave it so for a few days until nourishment flows to it, and then tighten it again.
But if the patient experiences none of the complications we have mentioned, then the bandage should not be loosened for about twenty days or thereabouts.
Then the patient's hand should be suspended by its own active movement, and make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is healed in thirty days or in thirty-two days, or very quickly in twenty-eight days, and all this depends on the constitution and the strength of the patient.
Chapter 13.
On the restoration of a fracture of the wrist and fingers.
Know that fractures rarely occur in the wrist and finger joints; attrition is more common.
And when a fracture or attrition occurs in the hand, then the patient should lie down and a cushion should be placed in front of him for support, and then place his hand on that cushion.
Then the assistant should extend the broken bones, and the physician should align them until they are properly joined.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and lint, unless an abscess is present; and then place a bandage over it according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
But if the fracture is in the inner parts of the palm of the hand, then make a kind of ball of cloth, and instruct the patient to hold it with his injured hand; then it should be firmly bound with a long cloth.
And let the bandage be of leather with cloth in which it is softened, so that the leather is applied to the palm; and tighten it as needed.
But if the fracture is on the outer parts, then it is necessary to place a bandage on top and another bandage on the underside of the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply the splint, as if the hand were held straight between the fingers with bandages.
And if a fracture occurs in any of the knuckles of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be bandaged as needed and then bound to the palm; and if you wish, place a small, straight splint so that the fracture is elevated and not moved.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle finger, or in the index finger, or in the ring finger, or in the little finger, then it should be bandaged and tied to the finger next to it; or all should be tied together continuously, which is better; or place a small splint on it, as we have said for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and near it, the disposition of a hot abscess and oppose—with your operation—what is necessary when any of these things occur, according to the repeated narration of the case.
Chapter 14.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken and is felt according to whether it is broken in the front or the back part.
And its restoration is done in this way: a ligament is tightened above the fracture and another below the fracture, and the patient is laid prostrate on their stomach and face.
Then the physician extends the entire ligament to its proper place, according to the nature of the fracture, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is close to the hip joint, then let the ligament be close to the knee on the hamstring side, so that the extension is upwards, and the other ligament below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the knee, then let the ligament be placed near the knee, and let the extension be towards the lower parts.
Then the physician aligns the bone with both hands until it is reduced to its natural shape and the bone is joined by a good joint; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and a bandage, unless an abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess occurs, then you leave it for some days until the abscess has matured and subsided, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be made as follows:  Wrap a firm bandage around the fracture, and fold it two or three times, removing any excess; then wrap the leg until it reaches the ankle and the hip joint.
And insert a long strip of cloth between the thigh and the leg near the knee on the lower side, with the ends of the cloth coming from the upper part of both sides, and then wrap around the leg and thigh that which is superfluous of the bandage.
And then place splints on the thigh at the location of the fracture, and place one splint over the bone of the leg.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths so that the bandage is firm.
Then begin with a tight bandage in the middle over the fracture site with three or four wraps, and as you move further away from the fracture site, make your bandage looser, lighter, and softer.
Then extend the two ends of the cloth to the lower parts until they reach the ankle, and tie both ends of the splints on the other side so that the bandage is not dislodged from its place.
Then leave the bandage on, ensuring that it does not cause corrosion, hot abscess, swelling, or similar problems to the limb; and if any of these problems occur, then carefully loosen it, and correct what has happened, as we have said many times before.
But if there is a piece of bone protruding from the bone, then you must set it as well as possible; and if not, cover it and secure it, and treat it with what has been mentioned previously until it is healed.
And sometimes this thigh fracture is tightened beyond the application of splints, as we have described in the aid to the arm.
However, in the restoration done in this manner, the patient does not experience limping; and if it is restored alone, without the other leg being used as support, then it is necessary that he limp on the affected leg permanently.
And know that the thigh is held tightly for forty days or a little more or a little less, and all this depends on the diversity of complexions and other dispositions (of the patient).
Chapter 15.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the kneecap.
Know that a fracture of the kneecap heals slowly, but very often attrition (or wearing away) occurs.
But if a fracture occurs, then it is only a fissure or hole in its parts, and this is with a wound or without a wound, and above all, it is accompanied by pain.
And its restoration is thus: that which is broken of its parts should be aligned with said splints until it is reunited and joined as much as possible by equalization and ease and fabrication (or construction/setting).
Then apply a plaster and place a round splint over it, if you have one; and bind it above with a bandage suitable for this.
And then consider all the precautions which we have recommended in other fractures, such as a hot poultice and similar things, so that every accident is opposed with what is convenient for him until he is healed.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the restoration of a leg fracture.
Know that in the leg there are two bones, of which one is large and is called by the name of the leg; and the other is thin, and is called the fibula.
And the same types of fractures occur to each of these bones of the leg as occur to the two bones of the arm, and therefore its restoration is done in the same way as the restoration of the arm, and the operation is one and the same.
And if both bones are broken together, the leg is deformed in all its parts.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is deformed in the front part.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find that below, and it is one of those causes which are not easily noticed by you.
Then administer extension, setting, and bandaging of the splints according to that same method.
However, if the leg fracture is large with many splinters, the extension should be less and gentler, and alleviate it with your skill.
And in the leg, there is some cause for operation similar to the arm, and when you set the splints and complete your operation, then you will take pieces of pine wood, which are placed under the bandages which are placed on the soles, and are usually made from palm branches; choose those which are of small thickness, and not at all the thin ones, and let their length be according to the length of the leg, from the knee to the bottom.
Then wrap around each leg two cloths according to their length, and place one on one part of the leg and the other on the other part, and let them extend from the knee to the lowest part of the foot.
Then bind the two splints in three places: at the two ends and in the middle.  For with this structure, the leg is prevented from bending to the right or left, and remains in a good position.
And now a small wooden splint, according to the length of the leg, has been applied; and let the leg be placed on it so that it conserves its position and prevents movement.
And especially it is necessary that you do this in a fracture, and when there is a wound properly so called.
Then examine the leg each day and study it carefully. Observe it for abscesses, inflammation, or other similar problems; and if any of these occur, then treat it accordingly until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the fracture of the talus and the bones of the foot and toes.
A fracture of the whole talus does not often occur; but fractures of the bones of the foot do sometimes occur; and fractures of the toes rarely occur, and only attrition in cases of dislocation.
And if a fracture occurs in the bones of the foot and those bones are raised up between themselves, then the patient should place his foot on the ground prepared as before.
Then place one foot on that which is raised of those bones, and then press and squeeze those until they return to their places.
Then apply a plaster and tow over it, and place under the sole of the foot a small board to which two flat pieces are attached.
Then bind that to the foot with a descending bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloths and other necessary materials.
And when three or four days have passed for him, then unbind the bandage, for you will find the bones aligned, or whether they are fractured or dislocated, and the operation on them is as you know.
But if some say they are fractured, then restore them and set them as I have recounted to you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then apply to the fractured finger a splint according to the length of the finger, and let it be a little longer than it; and place under the foot that board which I have described to you, and bind it with a suitable bandage.
But if what is fractured of the bones is two or three or more, then you place on each finger a splint of its size wrapped in linen cloth, and bind the board at the lowest part of the foot, and let it be resting on one of the ends of the board outside the sole of the foot so that it holds it with good retention.
And it is convenient for you that you not forget to consider all the causes which I have mentioned to you in the other fractures of the accidents that we have discussed; and oppose yourself to every accident with what is appropriate.
Chapter 18.
Of the fracture of the woman's hip and of the pubic bone and of the man's penis.
When the hip of a woman is fractured, then make her lie down in a lying position, and gently incline her to one side, and let her be held from behind.
And then the midwife fills the hip with cotton, and it should be made into a ball in her hip; and then the woman should be held, and her hip raised little by little, for all this is thrown in until the hip area is like a ball and the fractured bone returns.
And then place powders on her back, and when she wants to urinate, remove the cotton easily and let her urinate, and reduce and return it according to the manner in which you first set it, and then return to the first powder.
And do this for seven days or thereabouts, for by then it will be restored.
And if you wish, take a pig's bladder and attach a reed tube to its opening; and insert the whole bladder into her hip, and then blow into the tube gently until the bladder is inflated inside the hip, for this will help the fracture to heal.
And then it should be filled with cotton and left for several days as we have recommended, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the pubis of a man or woman is broken, then administer for its restoration and healing what we have recommended for the hip bone.
And what is right is not hidden in these nearby fractures which happen infrequently, especially to one who studies and uses and understands this my book well; for that fracture signifies to you the most appropriate methods in its restoration and binding, so understand well.
But in the case of a broken penis, take goose grease and apply it to the penis; and then place a cloth bandage over it, and let it be wrapped, and let it remain for three days or so, and it will be healed.
Chapter 19.
Of the restoration of bone fractures when accompanied by a wound.
It is convenient for one who suffers a fracture with a wound, and especially if the bone is large, such as the thigh bone or the humerus, or similar, that you treat and bleed him immediately if the conditions for bloodletting indicate it as we have said above.
And if the wound flows with blood, then you must attend to stopping it, so that you sprinkle crushed sage on it, if no other remedy is at hand.
And then begin to reset the fracture on that same day, and do not delay, lest an abscess form.
But if a hot abscess forms, then delay resetting it until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it at all on the third and fourth days, for you will cause harmful complications.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin, uncovered, then you must carefully reduce it and align it with your hands gently and with little extension.
But if extension is not available to you, and the alignment with your hands is insufficient, then you reduce it with your hands using this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, its length being seven or eight digits, and its width according to the size of the wound; and for this, it is necessary for the physician to have three or four of these, according to the amount needed for the treatment of all types of fractures.
And let it be round, and of little thickness, so that it does not bend under compression during the operation.
And let it have a sharp end which has a bevel at its extremities; and let the upper part of it be of gradually increasing thickness, from the middle to the ends.
And this is its form:
And it is named in Greek "labre volentz atelarem," that is, a small spatula.
Because it is necessary that its sharp end be placed raised above the end of the prominent bone.
And push that in at once until the bone returns, and is held in place with some equalization/alignment.
And then strive to align the ends of the fracture with each other, and if the broken end of the bone is thin, and does not receive the instrument well, then it is necessary that you incise the end of that bone until the instrument can grasp it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone completely with what we have recommended, then incise it with whatever is suitable from the incisors which we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
And then scrape away what remains on the bone of roughness and small splinters.
And when the bone is reduced, and after the reduction the patient finds vehement and persistent pain, then know that the bone is not returned to its natural place; and then do what you can to give great relief to the patient.
When your restoration of the bone is completed, then immerse a cloth in black styptic wine, especially if it is summer, and do not place on the wound cerate or anything else containing oil, lest it cause putrefaction and corruption.
And then administer stitches at the time of your completion of the bone's restoration, and leave the wound uncovered so that you may incise the opening with scissors and use bandages according to the size of the wound.
And take great care not to constrict the wound with the fracture, for many times foolish physicians have done this, and it has caused death or a corrosive and persistent illness, that is, madness, in their patients. And if your constriction is light, soft, different from the constriction of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or a bad wound, and you fear some bad accidents which we have recounted, and you find pain in the place, then it is not fitting that you place stitches there, but apply a bandage of hard cloths instead of stitches and bind it with those.
And when after a day or two the wound begins to suppurate, then remove the cloth which you had placed on it with wine, and then apply balms, ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds, such as tetrapharmacon ointment and similar ones.
And it is necessary for you to untie the bandage and examine the wound each day, morning and evening, until it is consolidated and closed, and it is necessary that the limb be positioned in such a way that the pus flows down from it easily.
If many days pass and the wound is not healed nor is the pus removed from it, then know that here are signs of small foreign bodies, for which it is necessary to explore the wound with a probe. And of those foreign bodies, those that are separated, remove and extract them; those which are not separated and which irritate the limb and cause pain, force you to incise them as far as is possible for you.
And if there occurs in the wound swelling or inflammation, that is,  inflammation, or another type of corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary that you treat each of these with that which is appropriate for it, according to the treatment which has been previously narrated in its chapter.
And this is what my discourse should address, and you should take note: when a large bone is fractured, such as the bone of the thigh and similar large limb bones, and there is no pulling or extraction of it, because many times death results from this; rather, leave it until it suppurates, for it will fall off by itself quite quickly, within twenty or thirty days; and then treat the wound if you see fit, and if not, leave it alone.
Chapter 20.
Chapter 20: On the treatment of a swelling that occurs at the site of a fracture.
Often this swelling occurs at the site of a healing fracture, especially if it is near the joints, and it deforms the shape of the limb; and it quickly prevents the limb from its natural function.
Then consider if the swelling is recent and apply medicines that are styptic, such as aloes, enses (likely a misspelling or archaic term, perhaps referring to a specific plant), myrrh, cerecloth (or a similar binding material), and acacia, and similar things; so that you take some or all of these, and mix them with styptic wine, or with egg white, or with sour wine, and apply this to the swelling on a piece of linen, and bind it tightly.
And leave the bandage untouched for many days; then unbind it and apply another, until the swelling is reduced; or apply a lead plate tightly, for lead has the property of reducing anything swollen in the limbs.
But if the swelling has already become hardened and constricted and necessitates its careful removal, then incise it at its most superior part, and cut away the excess and protruding matter, or scrape it with some instrument until it is reduced; and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Chapter 21. Of the restoration of a fracture when it is set, and the limb remains thin and weak, not according to its natural state.
When a bone fracture is set, and the limb remains thin and weak, this is due to many causes: one of which is excessive loosening of the ligaments and the binding done not as it should be; or by excessive tightness of the bandages such that nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb; or by an excessive amount of superfluous embrocation; or by excessive movement not at the proper time; or by a lack of blood in the patient's body and their weakness.
And the cure for this is to give the patient nourishing food and to cheer their heart so that their blood is increased; and administer baths; and introduce joy and mirth to them.
And then apply a poultice to the limb, so that the poultice draws much nourishment to it, or continuous embrocation with warm water, until the nourishment flows and returns to its natural form.
Chapter 22.
Concerning the treatment of fractured bones when they are set crookedly and the proper operation is prevented.
When, in a limb already set, there is a return of crookedness and prominence at the fractured bone and swelling, and because of this a deformity of the limb, but nevertheless the limb is not prevented from its natural function, then you should not accept the word of someone who thinks that limb is newly fractured.
But there have been many foolish physicians and bone-setters who have done this in our land; and this practice is very reprehensible, leading to great fear, the sign of which is death.
But if the lameness and swelling are recent, then they should be bathed in lukewarm water in which are boiled softening herbs, such as mallow leaves and roots, melilot, and similar things; and a softening plaster should be applied, such as well-made diachylon; or a mucilage of mallow root should be prepared and mixed with white of egg or olive oil, and applied as a plaster; or ripe figs should be crushed with pigeon droppings, and similar things from among the medicines that are called vulnerary consolidants.
And sometimes it is resolved with frequent, gentle rubbing with moist hands, and movement of the limb in every direction should be administered at all times.
But if the crookedness has already progressed and become strong and hardened, and the need arises to treat it with iron, then the uppermost part should be removed and the continuity of the bone dissolved, and that which superfluously swells with the bone should be incised with fine scalpels, and relief should be provided for this with care and skill.
Chapter 23.
Concerning speech on dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of any of the joints from their place, wherefore movement is impeded, and the shape of the limb is distorted, and it brings great pain and injury to the patient.
Therefore, when a dislocation happens to someone, then it is necessary to immediately strive that it be reduced, and not delay that reduction, because if it is delayed, the place becomes inflamed and very quickly the reduction of the dislocation becomes difficult.
And for this reason it should not be moved or stretched at the time of inflammation, because many times it causes spasms and excruciating pains.
But when this happens, then you should attend to the phlebotomy of the patient, and then let it be until the inflammation subsides; and then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil, and then it should be reduced with ease.
And every limb should be treated with that which will bring it back to its proper place (or function).
And I have already ordered the chapters on dislocation according to what preceded on fracture; from the uppermost part of the body down to the lowest part.
Chapter 24.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
If the two jaws are dislocated, and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are dislodged from their places by slight movement, and are softened a little; or they are dislocated by complete and total dislocation, until they are softened at the joint, so that saliva runs from the patient, and he cannot hold it, nor close his mouth, and his tongue is entangled in his speech.
But when his dislocation is slight, then in most cases it returns by itself with little intervention.
But if the dislocation is complete and total, then reduction must be administered quickly, and not delayed at all.
And let the assistant hold the patient's head, and let the physician insert the index finger of one hand into the root of the dislocated jaw inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or let him insert both index fingers together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of his hands should be outside with which he grasps; and he commands the patient to relax his jaws and release them from either side; and the physician grasps the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, and especially if both jaws are dislocated together, then administer a vaporization with warm water and oil, until the reduction of both is made easier, and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And when they return and are grasped, and the patient's mouth is closed and not softened, then place over that powders of cloth with cerate, and let it be made of wax and oil, mixed; and then let it be bound easily with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and his head should be between two pillows, so that it does not incline to the right or left; and do not force him to chew anything; before his food should be broth, until when the pains are relieved and the jaws are healed, then let him eat what he wants.
And this should be administered easily, and he should not exert himself in opening his mouth during eating and drinking and speaking, until the jaw is healed and sound.
And if the reduction of the two jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at any time and are not returned to their places, then many times fevers and continuous thirst occur, and the patient's abdomen is quickly distended, and he quickly vomits pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is perilous; and many times he to whom this happens dies within ten days.
Chapter 25.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and the extremity of the muscle.
The clavicle is not dislocated inward by its connection with the ribs; and sometimes it is dislocated outwards, and this is apparent to the touch/feeling.
And its restoration is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms. Then constrict the area with your palm with firm compression, for it will return.
Then apply a plaster and powders to that area and bind it tightly.
But the extremity of that which is the hip and is continuous with it, is not dislocated on any day; then it must be reduced and bandaged as we have said and as is appropriate for you.
Then apply a plaster, and powders, and a bandage, and instruct the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that a muscle is dislocated in only three ways, one of which is that it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated to the upper part of the muscle, and this is done slowly; and it is not dislocated in the back parts by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated in the front parts by the nerves.
And it is mostly dislocated and protrudes outwards in the lower parts below the armpit, and especially in those whose flesh is scant; it is easily dislocated; but in those in whom there is much flesh, it comes out with difficulty.
And it quickly happens to some men through a blow or fall, and their muscle is inflamed with a hot abscess, and it is believed because of this that it is dislocated.
Then you must prove this before you are completely certain of it, and then you proceed with its treatment.
And the dislocation is known when it is in the lower parts below the armpit; so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy muscle, because you find a manifest difference between them; and you find in the head of the muscle a concavity and below the armpit, towards the touch of the head of the muscle, like an egg, and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it in all movements.
If it is dislocated towards the feet or upwards, and you then find a manifest touch, and it is not hidden.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easily done when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the minister raises his hand to the upper parts; and then places his hands below his armpit, and raises the joint forcefully upwards to its place, and the minister raises his hand, and extends it upwards; and then places it downwards because it returns lightly.
But if by what we have said it does not return, and from the beginning of the dislocation, many days have passed, then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water and that a softening and relaxing embrocation be administered, such as boiled roots of marshmallow, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and be applied.
And then the patient should lie supine on his back, and a wool ball of medium firmness be placed under his armpit, and then the physician places his heel on the ball, and raises the head of the muscle with force, and pulls the patient's arm so that it is not pulled downwards, and another minister holds the patient's head so that it is not pulled downwards, because then it immediately returns to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it in this manner:  that you have present a man who is taller than the patient, and he stands on that side, and inserts his shoulder under the patient's armpit and raises his armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air, and another minister pulls the patient's arm from beneath his belly. But if the patient is light, then it is necessary that something else be suspended with him to make him heavier, because the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is reduced thus by another method: a long, round-topped pole, like a mortar pestle, neither thick nor thin, is fixed in the ground. Then, linen cloths are placed on top of the pole, and the patient stands along the length of the pole. Then his arm is extended downwards on the other side; and his body is pulled forcefully on the other side, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if reduction is difficult by all the methods we have mentioned, then use this treatment: take a pole two cubits long and four fingers wide, and its thickness should be lessened at the end that goes under the armpit, in this manner:
And then linen cloths should be tied over the round end so that the pole does not injure the patient.
And then it should be placed under the armpit of the patient, and the hand or arm should be extended over the pole down to the lower parts, and the pole should be tied to the armpit, the arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then the arm should be placed on a step of a ladder according to its width, and the hand extended downwards, and the rest of the body suspended on the other side, for the joint will immediately be replaced.
And when the replacement is accomplished in whichever way is possible for you, then you should place under the armpit a ball of wool of suitable size; and then apply a plaster prepared from finely ground barley flour and egg white over all the muscle, so that it is wrapped around it; and then bind tightly under the armpit with a suitable bandage; and the bandage should be wrapped over the plaster, and the patient's hand should be tied to their neck, and it should be left, and the patient's hand should not be moved for seven days.
And the patient's diet should be light until the limb is strengthened, for it is easier for its healing.
And then the diet should be gradually increased after seven or five days, and some movement should be exercised; and therefore if it is firm and not softened, then it is healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times due to humidity affecting it or another cause, then in that case cautery with three holes should be administered, as previously described in the chapter on cautery.
