And if all this is done and after seven days the bandage is untied and the joint is not firm, and the plaster and the binding are reapplied several times, and it is not firm and falls, and the patient cannot raise that arm upwards, then know that the nerves there, which are at the head of the muscle, are severed, or stretched, or softened, and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
Regarding the dislocation that occurs between the foot and the ankle or the parts behind, the reduction is done with impulse and extension with the hands, until it returns [to its place].
And let another binding be applied to it, and cure that until it is healed.
And if during healing there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement, then let the patient use baths many times until that heaviness is relieved and it returns to its former natural state.
Chapter 27.
Of the treatment of hip dislocation.
Know that the hip joint is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, and especially to the front and to the back.  The dislocation is not hidden from you, because it is evident to the sight or touch, depending on how it is dislocated. And when you compare the dislocated hip with the healthy one, this is clearly shown to you, for the joint is concave and cannot bend the leg, nor can it touch its muscle with it.
And it is necessary that you attend to it and reduce the dislocation immediately before a hot abscess develops, for if a hot abscess develops, its reduction is difficult, and it is not easily healed completely; and especially when it is in the posterior parts, for it is worse than all other types of dislocation, and more painful, and often causes death with it.
And the restoration of it, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the patient's leg should be extended with two hands, and the patient's leg should be extended, and the physician's hands should be on the hip and under it, and he should push the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the base of his palm until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the front parts, then return it by bending the leg with your hand, until you strike the hip with the base of your palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer forceful and very strong extension.
And it is necessary that two assistants extend the arm, and let two other assistants hold it firmly so that it is not moved during the extension.
And then the arm should be rotated in every direction, after it has been wrapped with a long doubled cloth over the patient's hand, or a bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that this may assist in lubricating the joint with ease; and then push the joint with strong pressure until it returns to its place.
And after the reduction, it is necessary that a plaster be applied which has styptic and drying properties with egg white, and be bound tightly with a descending bandage, and the arm be suspended from the neck, and left like that for some days.
And then it should be unbound; and if the joint is set in place, then you untie the bandage and leave it.
And if you see that the joint is not well tightened, then you reapply the plaster and bandage, and leave it for some days until it is tightened; and then unbind it.
But if there is great difficulty in reducing the joint, and the patient is restless, then administer a warm bath and gentle friction, and compression, until relief is obtained; or apply a poultice of soothing herbs, something very greasy; and then bind it, and leave it on the bone day and night. And then remove it, and put it in a bath; and when it is sufficiently softened, then rub the joint with a gentle friction, and then reapply the soothing poultice, that is, very greasy ointment, once, and a second time, and a third time. with the application of a bath, until it is healed.
And if you wish, place upon it a warm, moist cow's bladder, bound tightly over it; and do this several times, for it is moistening and helps to reduce the swelling.
Chapter 28.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated wrist, that is, of the joint that is between the arm and the hand.
A wrist is sometimes dislocated, and its reduction is easier than that of other joints; however, speed is necessary in its reduction at the time it is dislocated, before the place becomes inflamed or a hot abscess forms there.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the patient's wrist joint on a table, and the assistant extends the patient's hand, and the physician places his hand or palm over the prominence of the joint, and pulls it until it returns to its place.
However, it must be considered whether the palm is already facing the belly of the hand; then the patient places the back of their hand on a table during extension and reduction. And if the dislocation is towards the back of the hand, then let the position of that hand be inside on the table, so that the physician's hand falls on that prominence of the joint.
And so if it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then constrict it with a poultice, allowing the inflammation to subside, and leave it; and do not repeat this, for otherwise you will not be able to achieve the reduction once several days have passed.
However, if the joint remains twisted, and causes no harm to the patient except that the hand is weakened and cannot grasp anything, then you should know that the nerves are pinched or crushed, and then there is no remedy except to constrict it with a cautery near the injured area, and quickly apply something to it.
Then, when the limb is reduced, apply the poultice we have recommended, and let it be left and constricted for five days; and afterwards, let it be unbound and left, and let the patient exercise their hand.
But if its movement is impeded and some severity occurs, then soothe it with warm water and friction several times until it is relieved.
Chapter 29.
On the treatment of finger dislocations.
Sometimes fingers are dislocated in their entirety.
And when a finger of those on the side of the palm or the belly of the hand is dislocated, then extend the finger and push the dislocation back with your thumbs until it returns to its place.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it below the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it like that for two days.
Then suspend it and extend it until it is straightened and set in its proper place on that day.
And when it is night, bind it according to that narrative, and do not cease to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night; and do this for several days until it is tight/firm.
And do similarly if it is dislocated on the palm of the hand, and bind it to that part and perform your first operation until it is healed.
And similarly do this when it is dislocated in other parts.
Chapter 30.
Of the treatment of the dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When a complete dislocation happens to any of the vertebrae of the back or neck, and many vertebrae are dislodged from their places, then in that case there is no cure, for death is expected for the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's excrement is expelled involuntarily, and he cannot retain it; and many times some of his limbs are paralyzed, or two legs, or two arms, or one of them; or one vertebra is dislodged from its place, for many times it is dislodged and many times the removal is done in four parts.
Therefore, that is already dislodged to the posterior parts and is called yellowness (jaundice, possibly referring to a discoloration associated with the injury).
But the cure for him is that then you consider if the yellowness (jaundice) comes from a purulent infection, for then in that case there is neither cure nor healing at all.
But in that which comes from a fall or from percussion or similar to those, the Ancients already described the methods of cure with lengthy discourse, of which the most returns with profit; but I have already abbreviated that, of which the little excuses the excess of it, for my proximity to my intention exposition, with which I have exposed and formed this from various instruments of that with which I will show this and expound that.
And of those, only that which occurs in the two (sides/feet?) properly is cured with what I have recounted.
And it is that the patient should be extended on his face on a soft cushion near a wall, and a soft mat should be placed under him so that his feet are not hurt.
And then a straight pole should be placed, fixed in the earth at the end of the cushion near his head, and another pole near his feet at the other end of the cushion.
And the attendant should hold the pole, and it should not be fixed in a pit; and another attendant should hold the other one in that manner.
Then wrap a bandage around the patient's head and under the armpits, firmly; and extend the end of the bandage to the pole which is near his head, and tie it there; and then tighten another bandage around his hips, his thighs, and over his legs; and then adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other pole which is near his feet.
And then each attendant should extend the bandage to the part of the pole, and the two poles and the bandages should not be moved from their places where they are fixed, but nevertheless they are not fixed as we have said.
And the physician should place his palms on the spine with firmness until it returns, or place a board on it, and then adjust his feet on the board until it returns.
But if it does not return by this method, then take a board whose width or length is about three braccia, and make a hole in the wall which we have said is near the patient, into which the end of the board fits. And then place half of the board on the spine, and the physician places his feet on the other end, and presses firmly until the spine is compressed and returns to its place.
And if you wish, open with a drill which is turned by hand; and it is that you should place in the ground near the patient's head at the back of the neck two poles, the length of which is one braccio, and near the patient's head at the back of the neck exactly two poles; and let the distance between each pole be one palm, and let there be a hole made in each pole through which the drill passes, and each should be firmly fixed in the ground so that it does not move.
And let there be added a round pole on which the drill is mounted, and let it be rotated in the two holes of the two poles.
And at its end is a hole into which the drill bit is fitted, the length of which is one palm including the rotating part; and the other two poles are similar to this one.
And then tighten the ropes that you have tied to the patient's feet at the hole which is near their head, and the ropes that you have tied to their thighs at the drill which is near their feet.
And then near each drill let there be a servant who turns the drill with his hand, and the physician realigns the vertebra as we have said.
And then, once the vertebra is returned and the location is realigned, you should apply a drying plaster with egg white, and then lint; and then place over the plaster a piece of linen cloth whose width is three fingers or thereabouts, and whose length is sufficient to cover the area of the swelling, and the part of the healthy vertebra.
And then it should be bound with the bandage we have mentioned, and feed the patient a light diet until he is healed.
And if any swelling remains in the area at the end of the healing process, then medicines should be administered that soften and smooth, with the application of the linen cloth for a long time, which we have recommended; and a lead plate is then applied to that area.
And sometimes swelling occurs in the area behind the spinal vertebrae, and is caused by that dislocation; and it is that bone which already swells, so that no attempt should be made to treat it with this medicine, for it will quickly cause death.
Chapter 31.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the muscle joint suffer only dislocation, and not what happens to other joints with small movement and concavity.
And the hip joint is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated inwards, outwards, forwards, or backwards.
And it is mostly dislocated inwards, and less often forwards or backwards.
And a sign of its inward dislocation is that, when you align the patient's healthy leg with the affected one, the affected leg is longer and the knee more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend their foot without pain.  And this is the location where the pain is often inflamed with a hot abscess because the head of the femur has already arrived there.
And the sign of one to whom dislocation happens outwards, is that its symptoms are contrary to these symptoms.
And a sign of one to whom dislocation happens forwards is that he extends his leg completely, but nevertheless cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if he tries to walk he cannot put weight on the front; and his urine is retained and his groin is inflamed, and he walks on his heel.
And a sign of one to whom dislocation happens backwards is that he cannot extend his knee, nor can he bend it before bending his groin, and his leg is shorter than the other, and his groin is softened, and the head of the femur is towards that place.
But the reduction of the types of this dislocation is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and ancient, which is already healed with the aforementioned having been applied to it, and it remains according to its disposition, then it is not in that cure at all; and then it is not suitable that presumption be made in that matter.
But in one in whom the dislocation is recent, and it is one of the four types of dislocation, then take care that you return the joint and extend it inwards and outwards, and move it to the right and left, for it very quickly returns and needs no other cure.
But, if it does not return in this manner, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong assistant and extend his leg from the lower part, either with his hands, or with a bandage which is tied above the knee; and another assistant extend the hand of that one from above so that he puts his hands below the patient's testicles; and then tighten with a strong bandage above the root of the thigh, and let another assistant hold the end of the bandage.
And let the extension be from him or from the inner part to the groin to the part of the fork (of the legs/pubic area), or from the back part to the part of the back.
And let the extension of all those be at once until the patient is raised with his body from the ground and remains suspended.
And this type of extension is common to all four (types of dislocation).
And therefore, if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it will be good; and if not, then it is necessary for every type [of dislocation] that which we will say of its own cure.
In the reduction of the hip itself, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is in the inner parts, it is necessary that the patient be supine on his sound side; and then place the ligatures on the root of the thigh in the place which is between the head of the thigh and the place which is below the groin; and then extend the ligatures above the part of the groin to the highest part of the fork (pubic area).
And then another strong attendant should take his arms, and firmly grasp the thick part of the affected thigh and extend it outwards with a vehement extension, for by this it returns to its place.
And this type [of dislocation] is of easier cure, with which this member is reduced, than the other types.
But if it is [the dislocation] excused above you and does not yield to treatment with this type of cure at all, then it is necessary that the two feet of the patient be tied together with a strong, light ligature, above both ankles and both heels.
And let the length of each [ligature] be equal to the quantity of four digits, and let the affected leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two digits.
And then the patient should be suspended from the head of a beam, and let him be inside the house; and let his height from the ground be the quantity of two arms' lengths.
And then command a strong young man to lower the head of the thigh, and let another young man be suspended with the patient, and the other young man, with strong pulling, should reduce [the dislocation], for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But in the proper reduction, when it is a dislocation to the outside, it is necessary that the patient lie on his back, as we have recounted in the case of [a] cold abscess; and let the ligature be tightened on the affected leg properly and on his feet.
And then two beams should be placed, one at his feet and the other at his head; and then let suitable beams be placed in the middle of the ridge [of the back] upon which wet cloths should be wrapped, so that the patient is not harmed because the beam is between his thighs, so that during the extension the lower parts are not pulled.
And then each assistant extends on his side, and the physician with his hand skillfully performs the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then it is necessary that the leg be extended slightly, and it is according to this same narrative about the ridge [of the back]; and the physician places the palm of his right hand on the dislocated joint, and then compresses it with the other hand, and with that compression he places that which is extended in the lower parts at the place of the knee.
And if the dislocation is in the back parts, then it is not necessary that the patient be extended in the lower parts, and he is raised above the ground; rather it is necessary that he be placed on something hard, just as it is necessary for the elevation if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we have said about his lying on two hips; and it is on his face and the ligaments are extended, as we have said, there is little harm.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a board to the place where the joint is.
Therefore, when the reduction is completed as it should be — and the sign of its reduction is not hidden from you, which is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see those equal and the patient straightens his leg and extends it without difficulty, then you know that the limb is returned as it should be — and then you adjust the two thighs and apply a plaster and bind it so that the hip is not moved in any of the parts; and let the patient rest for three or four days.
Then untie the bandage and the plaster, and compare one leg with the other.
Then, if you see them equal in appearance, then you know that the dislocation is now healed, and then let the patient go.
And if you see any cause for softening, then you return and plaster that, and bind it according to your first binding, and leave it for three days.
Then untie it, and have him delay walking on it for a few days, until it is strengthened.
Chapter 32.
Concerning the treatment of knee dislocation.
The knee is dislocated in three ways: outwards, inwards, and downwards/behind; it is not dislocated forwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the patient to bring his leg to his thigh; then you will know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration for all its types is that you make the patient lie straight, so that he extends his leg as much as he can;  apply your two hands to his face, and place his foot between your feet; then firmly grasp his knee with your palms, and hold those between your fingers on his knee with strength, and another assistant extends his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its reduction is that his leg adheres to the thigh easily and without effort.
Then apply a plaster and bind the leg to the thigh, and then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, and then unbind them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for some days, until he is healed and strengthened.
But if the reduction is difficult for you, then administer strong extension with the ligatures which have been previously described in the treatment of the hip.
Chapter 33.
On the treatment of the dislocation of the talus (ankle bone).
The talus is sometimes dislocated by a slight movement, and sometimes completely dislocated; and its dislocation occurs either inwards or outwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that sometimes the talus swells, extending towards the side to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of its slight displacement is by gentle reduction, and this is done by extending it easily with the hands and pulling it until it returns to its place.
But in treating it when it is completely dislocated, you must make the patient lie straight, and have a strong and capable assistant hold him from behind at the level of his back.
Then you, with your right hand, take the foot from its uppermost part, and with your left hand from the lower part of the foot at the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with your left hand, twice, and then extend it with your left; and push the toes of the foot with your right hand towards the leg without effort.
And do this twice as we have explained.
Then, push the toes of the foot towards the leg a third time, and you will be at the ankle.
Then it will return in one or two attempts according to this method, and if you see the foot straightened, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation on it, for it will return.
But if the reduction is forbidden to you by what we have recounted, then make the patient lie on their back on the ground, and tighten a tightly bound pad on the ground, so that it falls between their thighs, and you have already wrapped a cloth around them so that it does not harm the patient.
Then let the patient's attendant hold their thighs, and let another attendant extend the foot with their hands or with a bandage tied around the ankle; and then let each attendant extend against the extension of their counterpart, and let the pad remain between the patient's thighs, holding it back, so that their body is not pulled downwards against their extension.
Then let the physician straighten the dislocation with his hands, and let another attendant hold the healthy thigh downwards, because the dislocation easily returns.
And so when the dislocation returns and the success of its reduction is clear to you, then apply a plaster and bind it tightly with bandages, and bind the foot to the leg with a bandage.
And you must be careful of or guard against the nerves which are behind the heel, so that the bandage over it is not too tight and strongly injures them.
Then leave it for two or three days; and if the bandage is loosened, tighten it.
Then unbind it on the third or fourth day, and forbid the patient from walking for forty days. But if he is forced to walk before that time, there is no certainty that the dislocation will not be loosened and worsened, and then it will not receive a cure.
And if a hot abscess happens to him, then you must administer to its healing that which we described before, in another place of treatment and embrocation, until it is healed.
Chapter 34.
On the treatment of dislocations of the toes.
The dislocated [toe] should be bandaged with gentle extension without effort; and this is not difficult, rather it is easy.
But if the dislocation is in some bone of the instep, then you should make the patient raise his foot onto a place level with the ground or on a table, and remain thus as if he were walking.
And then you stand, and place your foot on what protrudes from the joint, and then press that with your foot gently until it returns, and you see that it is bandaged and no prominence appears in the place.
Then place under the sole of his foot a board that supports the whole foot, to which are attached two straps; and then tighten that with a decent and firm bandage for three days; and then untie it and have him rest from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and its reduction is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the types of dislocation that are made with a wound or with a fracture or with both together.
When any of these causes occur, and you are diligent in its cure and his restoration, death often results.
And therefore, one should not presume to treat him, except for someone skilled in the art, experienced, merciful, and compassionate, slow, not rash or presumptuous; and who uses at the beginning only such medicines that soothe hot abscesses, and leaves the patient to divine judgment, except for that one in whom you hope for salvation from harm, with alleviation of the illness, and in whom some hope appears. And then strive to make his reduction immediately at the beginning of the cause, before a hot abscess develops.
And then if the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regime which soothes hot abscesses and treat the wound with what is suitable, using drying ointments.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and the jagged bone ends are separated, then you must force their alignment, and do in this what we have said in the simple illnesses, as preceded in those places.
And guard and avoid, according to your virtue, that entry into the paths of fear as my preaching has preceded you; for this is better for you before your Creator, and more permanent for your glory, and more salutary for your true body, those in your time. Thus end the words of Albucasis perfectly, for which may God be blessed. Amen.
